Dialectic -- Jadal

The Definition of Answer (Jawab) in Dialectic
Author defines answer in dialectic and its linguistic root.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the linguistic root of 'answer' and how it functions as a decisive cut between affirmation and negation in dialectic.

Core Idea
An answer in jadal is a decisive cut between yes and no, providing a final determination.

This passage defines the second key term in dialectic: 'answer' (al-jawab). The author traces its linguistic root to 'cutting' (al-qat'), which is fitting because the answer cuts between two possibilities: affirmation and negation. Using the simple example of 'Did Zayd come?', he shows how the answer either affirms or negates, providing a definitive resolution. This lesson will deepen your understanding of what constitutes a proper answer in debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic root of 'answer'
The author states that the origin of 'jawab' in language is 'cutting' (al-qat'). He gives the example of cutting through land (yajub al-bilad) and the Quranic verse about Thamud cutting through rock. This root metaphor emphasizes that an answer cuts through uncertainty.
The same root appears in the word 'jabiha' (cut across).
2
Understand the decisive function of an answer
The answer 'cuts' by deciding between affirmation and negation. The author uses the example: 'Did Zayd come?' The possible answers are 'yes' (affirmation) or 'no' (negation). Each answer cuts off the other possibility, providing a final determination.
This shows that an answer in jadal is not merely a response but a decisive resolution.
3
Formulate the definition of jawab in jadal
Based on the passage, an answer is the response that corresponds to the question and cuts between the two poles of affirmation and negation. It must be clear and directly address the question.
The definition is implicit; the author does not provide an explicit terminological definition beyond citing Ibn Aqil's earlier definition of question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال أصله؛ الطلب. فمنه : سؤال الفقير الغني والعبد للرب، وهو أن يطلب منه ما يصلحه من مال أو عفو . ومنه : سؤال الاستفهام، نحو: زيد يسأل عن حال عمرو، أي: يطلب منه معرفة كيفية حاله وهو بالجملة طلب معرفة حال المستفهم عنه . ومنه : سؤال الاستفادة كسؤال المتعلم للمعلم كيف يكون؟ وسؤال العناد والتعجيز كسؤال المعترض للمستدل : لم قلت كذا وكذا؟ وهو طلب الفائدة أو الدليل، والمراد بالسؤال ها هنا أحد هذين، لأن الأسئلة الجدلية قد تكون من مستفيد يقصد معرفة الحكم خالصًا مما يرد عليه وقد تكون من معاند يقصد قطع خصمه ورده إليه»(1) وقال إمام الحرمين في تعريف السؤال اصطلاحا: وأما السؤال ، فهو الاستدعاء، وقيل: هو الطلب، وقيل: هو الجواب ) (2). وقال ابن عقيل في تعريفه : هو الطلب للإخبار بأداته في الإفهام» (3) . وهذا - في الحقيقة - تعريف للسؤال بوجه عام، أما سؤال الجدل بوجه خاص، فقال ابن عقيل في تعريفه : اعلم أن سؤال الجدل، هو الذي يقصد به نقل الخصم عن مذهبه بطريق المحاجة» (1) . ویرى ابن عقيل ضرورة أن يكون السؤال واضحًا كل الوضوح مفهما كل الإفهام، وإلا لم يجب على المسئول الالتفات إليه أو الرد عليه أصلا، قال: واعلم أن كثيرًا من الجهال بحقائق النظر وقوانين الجدل يتوهمون المسألة كلمة واحدة من تجاوزها فقد جاء بأخرى من غير جنسها وخرج عن واجبها، وفي الحقيقة أن كل استخبار تمَّ وفُهِمَ معناه فهو مسألة تامة، على معنى أنه قد لحق بالسؤال واستحق اسمه، ولولا ذلك لم يجب على المسئول أن يجيب عن سؤال قائم مفهوم عن شيء، ولا استحق اسم مسألة. فأما ما ليس بسؤال البتة، أو ليس بسؤال تام، فليس يجب الجواب عنه، لأن ما لم يتحقق الكلام سؤالا فلا يقتضي جوابًا، والمسألة الناقصة لا يفهم معناها وإنما يجب الجواب بعد الإفهام، مع أنه ليس لسائل أن يسكت ويقتضي الجواب إلا بعد الإفهام وتمام المسألة، فمتى سكت عن كلام غير تام، فما تحقق له سؤال، ولا وجب لكلامه جواب»(2) . ب - تعريف الجواب : أما الجواب، فأصلهُ في اللغة ؛ «القطع ، من قولهم : هو يجوب البلاد، أي: يقطعها، وقوله سبحانه وتعالى: ﴿ وَثَمُودَ الَّذِينَ جَابُوا الصَّحْرَ بِالْوَادِ﴾ (3) ، أي قطعوه .. وإنما سمي به ما قابل السؤال، لأنه يؤدي إلى القطع ، لأن المجيب يقطع بمعنى الخبر على طريقة الإثبات والنفي، فإذا قال السائل : هل أتاك زيد ؟ فقد علق سؤاله بأحد أمرين : إما نعم وإما لا فنعم للإثبات ولا للنفي، فالقاطع المجيب إما بنعم، فيقطع بأنه أتاه أو لا، فيقطع على أنه لم يأته» (4)
The origin of 'question' is request. Among it is the question of the poor to the rich and the servant to the Lord, which is that he requests from him what benefits him of wealth or pardon. Among it is the question of inquiry, like Zayd asking about the condition of 'Amr, i.e., requesting knowledge of the state of his condition, and it is in sum a request for knowledge of the state of the one inquired about. Among it is the question of learning, like the student's question to the teacher: 'How is it?' And the question of obstinacy and incapacitation, like the objector's question to the one providing evidence: 'Why did you say such and such?' And it is a request for benefit or evidence. What is meant by 'question' here is one of these two, because dialectical questions may be from a learner intending to know the ruling purely from what is presented to him, or from an obstinate person intending to cut off his opponent and refute him. (1) Imam al-Haramayn said in the terminological definition of question: 'As for the question, it is the summons; and it is said: it is the request; and it is said: it is the answer.' (2) Ibn 'Aqil said in its definition: 'It is the request for information with its tool of understanding.' (3) This, in reality, is a definition of question in general. As for the question of dialectic specifically, Ibn 'Aqil said in its definition: 'Know that the dialectical question is that by which one intends to move the opponent from his position through the method of argumentation.' (1) Ibn 'Aqil sees the necessity that the question be completely clear, fully understood; otherwise, the respondent is not obligated to pay attention to it or respond to it at all. He said: 'Know that many of the ignorant of the realities of theoretical investigation and the laws of dialectic imagine that a single issue is one word, and that if one goes beyond it, he has brought another not of its kind and has departed from its duty. In reality, every completed inquiry whose meaning is understood is a complete issue, in the sense that it has attained the status of a question and deserves its name. Were it not for that, the respondent would not have to answer a standing, understood question about something, nor would it deserve the name of issue. As for that which is not a question at all, or is not a complete question, it is not necessary to answer it, because speech that is not verified as a question does not necessitate an answer, and an incomplete issue is not understood in its meaning. The answer is only required after understanding, with the condition that it is not for the questioner to remain silent and demand an answer except after understanding and the completion of the issue. So whenever he is silent about incomplete speech, he has not realized a question, and his speech does not obligate an answer.' (2) B - Definition of answer: As for the answer, its origin in the language is 'cutting', from their saying: 'He cuts through the land,' i.e., he traverses it. And His statement, Exalted and High: 'And Thamud, who cut through the rock in the valley' (Surah al-Fajr, verse 9), i.e., they cut it. It is only named that because it corresponds to the question, because it leads to cutting, for the answerer cuts by means of the meaning of the report on the method of affirmation and negation. So if the questioner says: 'Did Zayd come to you?' he has suspended his question on one of two things: either yes or no. Yes is for affirmation, no for negation. The decisive answerer either says yes, thereby cutting that he came, or no, thereby cutting that he did not come.' (4)
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms in dialectic: 'question' (السؤال) and 'answer' (الجواب). It begins with the linguistic origin of 'question' as 'request' (الطلب), then distinguishes several types: a request for material benefit (like a poor person asking a rich person), a request for information (like asking about someone's condition), a request for learning (student to teacher), and a request out of obstinacy or challenge (objector to arguer: 'why did you say that?'). The author clarifies that in dialectic, the relevant types are the last two: either a genuine learner seeking the ruling (سؤال استفادة) or an obstinate challenger seeking to refute the opponent (سؤال عناد وتعجيز). Next, the passage cites Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) giving a terminological definition: 'question is a summons' or 'a request' or even 'an answer' — the latter perhaps meaning the answer is implicit in the question's framing. Then Ibn 'Aqil defines question as 'the request for information with its tool of understanding' — i.e., using interrogative particles to convey meaning. The passage then moves to the specific dialectical question: Ibn 'Aqil defines it as 'that by which one intends to move the opponent from his position through argumentation' — so its purpose is to force a concession or change of stance. A critical point follows: the question must be clear and complete. If it is vague or incomplete, the respondent is not obliged to answer. Ibn 'Aqil criticizes those who think a 'question' (مسألة) is just a single word; in reality, a question is complete if its meaning is understood. An incomplete question is not a valid question and does not demand an answer. Finally, the definition of 'answer' (الجواب) is given. Its linguistic root is 'cutting' (القطع), as in cutting through land or rock. The answer is called such because it 'cuts' between affirmation and negation, providing a decisive response. For example, to the question 'Did Zayd come?' the answer is either 'yes' (affirmation) or 'no' (negation), each cutting off the other possibility.

Key Terms
الجواب
القطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
An answer in dialectic is a decisive cut that resolves the question into affirmation or negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Formulate a decisive answer to the question 'Is qiyas (analogy) a valid source of law according to the Hanafi school?' Use the model of cutting between affirmation and negation.
?
Identify the linguistic root of 'jawab' and explain how it applies to the answer in a debate about the validity of a hadith.

The Definition and Conditions of the Dialectical Question
Defines the dialectical question and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a valid dialectical question according to Ibn 'Aqil and determine whether a given question obligates a response.

Core Idea
A dialectical question must be clear and complete to obligate an answer.

In jadal (dialectical debate), not every utterance counts as a valid question. Ibn 'Aqil, a leading Hanbali jurist and dialectician, defines the dialectical question precisely and sets conditions for when a respondent must answer. This lesson dissects that definition and its practical implications for debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Find the term being defined (definiendum) and the statement that defines it (definiens). Here, Ibn 'Aqil defines 'the dialectical question' (su'al al-jadal) as 'that by which one intends to move the opponent from his position by way of disputation'.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is the broader category: 'question' (su'al). The differentia is the distinguishing feature: 'intended to move the opponent from his position by way of disputation' (muhajja). This separates dialectical questions from ordinary information-seeking questions.
3
Recognize the conditions of clarity and completeness
Ibn 'Aqil stresses that the question must be 'perfectly clear and fully understood'. If it is ambiguous or incomplete, the respondent is not obliged to answer. The passage also warns against thinking a question is just a single word; a complete inquiry whose meaning is understood is required.
4
Apply the conditions to example questions
Test sample questions against Ibn 'Aqil's criteria. For instance, 'What is your evidence?' is complete and clear. 'But...' is incomplete and does not obligate a response. Practice distinguishing valid from invalid dialectical questions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا - في الحقيقة - تعريف للسؤال بوجه عام، أما سؤال الجدل بوجه خاص، فقال ابن عقيل في تعريفه : اعلم أن سؤال الجدل، هو الذي يقصد به نقل الخصم عن مذهبه بطريق المحاجة. ويرى ابن عقيل ضرورة أن يكون السؤال واضحًا كل الوضوح مفهما كل الإفهام، وإلا لم يجب على المسئول الالتفات إليه أو الرد عليه أصلا، قال: واعلم أن كثيرًا من الجهال بحقائق النظر وقوانين الجدل يتوهمون المسألة كلمة واحدة من تجاوزها فقد جاء بأخرى من غير جنسها وخرج عن واجبها، وفي الحقيقة أن كل استخبار تمَّ وفُهِمَ معناه فهو مسألة تامة، على معنى أنه قد لحق بالسؤال واستحق اسمه، ولولا ذلك لم يجب على المسئول أن يجيب عن سؤال قائم مفهوم عن شيء، ولا استحق اسم مسألة. فأما ما ليس بسؤال البتة، أو ليس بسؤال تام، فليس يجب الجواب عنه، لأن ما لم يتحقق الكلام سؤالا فلا يقتضي جوابًا، والمسألة الناقصة لا يفهم معناها وإنما يجب الجواب بعد الإفهام، مع أنه ليس لسائل أن يسكت ويقتضي الجواب إلا بعد الإفهام وتمام المسألة، فمتى سكت عن كلام غير تام، فما تحقق له سؤال، ولا وجب لكلامه جواب.
This – in reality – is a definition of the question in general. As for the dialectical question in particular, Ibn 'Aqil said in its definition: Know that the dialectical question is that by which one intends to move the opponent from his position by way of disputation. Ibn 'Aqil holds that the question must be perfectly clear and fully understood; otherwise, the respondent is not obliged to pay attention to it or respond to it at all. He said: Know that many of those ignorant of the realities of theoretical inquiry and the rules of dialectic imagine that the issue (mas'ala) is a single word; whoever goes beyond it has brought another of a different kind and has departed from its duty. In reality, every inquiry that is completed and whose meaning is understood is a complete issue, in the sense that it has attained the status of a question and deserves its name. If not, the respondent is not obliged to answer a standing, understood question about something, nor does it deserve the name 'issue'. As for what is not a question at all, or is not a complete question, it is not obligatory to answer it, because speech that has not been verified as a question does not necessitate a response, and the incomplete issue is not understood in its meaning; rather, the answer is only obligatory after comprehension, and the questioner does not have the right to remain silent and demand an answer except after comprehension and completion of the issue. So when he remains silent with incomplete speech, he has not established a question for himself, and his speech does not necessitate an answer.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage defines the dialectical question (su'al al-jadal) as a technical term in the science of jadal. It begins by stating that the preceding discussion defined the question in general, and now it turns to the specific dialectical question. The author quotes Ibn 'Aqil, who defines it as a question intended to move the opponent from his position by way of disputation (muhajja). This distinguishes it from ordinary questions seeking information. Ibn 'Aqil then emphasizes a crucial condition: the question must be perfectly clear and fully understood by the respondent. If it is ambiguous or incomplete, the respondent has no obligation to answer it. He criticizes those who mistakenly think that a 'mas'ala' (issue/question) is just a single word, and that deviating from that word invalidates the question. In reality, any complete inquiry whose meaning is understood is a complete mas'ala, deserving of the name. Conversely, an incomplete question that is not understood does not obligate a response. The passage thus establishes that clarity and completeness are essential for a dialectical question to be valid, protecting the respondent from vague or fragmented queries.

Key Terms
سؤال الجدل
مسألة
استخبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It must be perfectly clear and fully understood.
C) It must be derived from the Qur'an and Sunnah.
D) It must be posed by a qualified mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
A valid dialectical question must be a complete inquiry aimed at moving the opponent via disputation, and it must be perfectly clear to obligate a response.

?

Linguistic and Technical Definition of Jawab
Defines answer linguistically as 'cutting' and terminologically as informative speech containing the question's meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic origin of jawab and reproduce Imam al-Haramayn's technical definition, identifying its genus and differentia.

Core Idea
The answer cuts doubt – its name reveals its function: decisive informative speech that mirrors the question.

The term 'jawab' (answer) is central to dialectic (jadal). Before you can evaluate whether an answer is valid, you must know what an answer is. This lesson unpacks the definition from language and technical usage, giving you the precise genus and differentia that every answer must satisfy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic origin
Find the root (qat') and how it leads to 'cutting'. The answer is called jawab because it cuts uncertainty.
Qur'anic verse about Thamud cutting through rock illustrates the root.
2
Extract the technical definition
Locate Imam al-Haramayn's definition: 'al-khabar al-mudammun bi-ma'na al-su'al'. Note that it is an informative statement.
3
Identify genus and differentia
Genus = khabar (informative speech). Differentia = 'incorporates the meaning of the question'. This excludes statements that do not address the question.
Not all khabar are answers; only those that contain the question's meaning.
4
Verify the definition's completeness
Check if it includes all genuine answers and excludes non-answers. Does a simple 'yes' or 'no' qualify? Yes, because they implicitly contain the question's meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - تعريف الجواب : أما الجواب، فأصله في اللغة : القطع، من قولهم : هو يجوب البلاد، أي: يقطعها، وقوله سبحانه وتعالى: ﴿ وَثَمُودَ الَّذِينَ جَابُوا الصَّحْرَ بِالْوَادِ﴾ (3) ، أي قطعوه .. وإنما سمي به ما قابل السؤال، لأنه يؤدي إلى القطع ، لأن المجيب يقطع بمعنى الخبر على طريقة الإثبات والنفي، فإذا قال السائل : هل أتاك زيد ؟ فقد علق سؤاله بأحد أمرين : إما نعم وإما لا فنعم للإثبات ولا للنفي، فالقاطع المجيب إما بنعم، فيقطع بأنه أتاه أو لا، فيقطع على أنه لم يأته. أما في الاصطلاح فقد عرفه إمام الحرمين بأنه: «الخبر المضمن بمعنى السؤال ؛ فلا جواب إلا خبر ومن الخبر ما لا يكون إلا جوابًا» . ويرى ابن عقيل ضرورة مطابقة الجواب للسؤال من غير تغيير أو تبديل، قال : وينبغي للسائل أن ينظر إلى المعنى المطلوب في السؤال، فإن عدل المجيب لم يرض منه إلا بالرجوع إلى جواب ما سأله عنه، فإن كثيرا ممن لا يضبط الجدل ولا يد له فيه، يسأل عن شيء، فيجيب عن غيره وهو يظن أنه قد أجاب، ويقنع منه السائل، إذ كان السائل أقصر منه علما بتحديد الجواب.
B - Definition of the answer: As for the answer, its origin in language is 'cutting', from their saying: 'He cuts through the land', meaning he traverses it. And Allah the Exalted said: 'And Thamud who cut through the rocks in the valley' (89:9), meaning they cut through it. It is called by this what corresponds to the question, because it leads to cutting off (doubt). The respondent cuts off with the meaning of a statement in the manner of affirmation or negation. So when the questioner says: 'Has Zayd come to you?' he has suspended his question on one of two things: either yes or no. 'Yes' is for affirmation, 'no' for negation. The respondent cuts off either with 'yes', affirming that he came, or with 'no', negating that he came. As for the technical definition, Imam al-Haramayn defined it as: 'The informative speech that incorporates the meaning of the question; for there is no answer except an informative statement, and among informative statements there is that which is only an answer.' Ibn 'Aqil holds the necessity of the answer matching the question without change or alteration. He said: The questioner should consider the intended meaning in the question; if the answerer deviates, he should not be satisfied except by returning to answer what he was asked. For many who do not master dialectic and have no skill in it ask about something and answer about something else, thinking they have answered, and the questioner acquiesces, since the questioner is less knowledgeable in specifying the answer.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'jawab' (answer) from two perspectives. First, it gives the linguistic origin: the word 'jawab' derives from 'qat'' (cutting), as in cutting through land, or the Qur'anic verse about Thamud cutting through rocks. The answer is so named because it 'cuts' the uncertainty of the question, providing a decisive affirmation or negation. The author illustrates this with the example: 'Has Zayd come to you?' The response is either 'yes' (affirmation) or 'no' (negation). Second, it presents the technical definition from Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), who says the answer is an informative speech (khabar) that contains the meaning of the question. Not every khabar is an answer, but some khabar are answers. This highlights that the answer must be propositional and directly address the question. Finally, Ibn 'Aqil adds a crucial practical requirement: the answer must exactly match the question without any change or alteration. This prevents the respondent from evading the issue by answering a different question, a common trick among the unskilled. The passage thus establishes both the etymology and the technical conditions for a proper dialectical answer.

Key Terms
جواب (jawab): The answer: in language, it means 'cutting', and in terminology it is an informative statement that incorporates the meaning of the question, eliminating doubt.
خبر (khabar): An informative statement that can be true or false. Here, it is the genus of the answer; every answer is a khabar. [Note: In Arabic grammar, khabar is the predicate of a nominal sentence; here it is used in a logical sense.]
قطع (qat'): Cutting; the etymological root of jawab, indicating that the answer decisively cuts off doubt or uncertainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Imam al-Haramayn defines the answer (jawab) as:
A) Any speech that follows a question.
B) The informative speech that incorporates the meaning of the question.
C) A rhetorical question that surprises the opponent.
D) An imperative command to the questioner.

Key Takeaway
The answer (jawab) is a specific type of informative statement (khabar) that incorporates the meaning of the question, linguistically rooted in 'cutting' off doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following exchange: Questioner: 'Is fasting in Ramadan obligatory?' Respondent: 'Fasting is a pillar of Islam.' Determine if the answer fits the technical definition of jawab. Explain why or why not.
?
Construct a definition of 'jawab' in your own words using the genus and differentia from Imam al-Haramayn, and test it on the example: 'Has the Prophet (peace be upon him) ascended to heaven?' Provide a model answer.

The Condition of Answer Matching Question in Jadal
Establishes the rule that the answer must exactly match the question without alteration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify violations of the matching condition in dialectical exchanges and explain why Ibn 'Aqil requires exact correspondence.

Core Idea
A mismatched answer is no answer – the respondent must answer the exact question asked.

Even if an utterance is technically a khabar containing some meaning, it may fail to be a valid jawab if it does not directly address the question. Ibn 'Aqil establishes a crucial rule: the answer must match the question without change or alteration. This lesson analyzes that condition and its application in dialectic.

Guided Path
1
State Ibn 'Aqil's condition
Find his exact words: the answer must match the question without change or alteration.
The condition is about preserving the intended meaning.
2
Identify the rationale
Why does the answer need to match? To cut off doubt definitively; otherwise the question remains unresolved.
The linguistic root 'cutting' implies decisiveness.
3
Recognize the violation
In the passage, the common error is answering a different question while thinking one has answered. Recognize this as a violation.
Ibn 'Aqil says: 'if the answerer deviates, he should not be satisfied except by returning to answer what he was asked'.
4
Apply the rule to an exchange
Given a question-answer pair, decide if the answer meets the condition. If not, state how it should be corrected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - تعريف الجواب : أما الجواب، فأصله في اللغة : القطع، من قولهم : هو يجوب البلاد، أي: يقطعها، وقوله سبحانه وتعالى: ﴿ وَثَمُودَ الَّذِينَ جَابُوا الصَّحْرَ بِالْوَادِ﴾ (3) ، أي قطعوه .. وإنما سمي به ما قابل السؤال، لأنه يؤدي إلى القطع ، لأن المجيب يقطع بمعنى الخبر على طريقة الإثبات والنفي، فإذا قال السائل : هل أتاك زيد ؟ فقد علق سؤاله بأحد أمرين : إما نعم وإما لا فنعم للإثبات ولا للنفي، فالقاطع المجيب إما بنعم، فيقطع بأنه أتاه أو لا، فيقطع على أنه لم يأته. أما في الاصطلاح فقد عرفه إمام الحرمين بأنه: «الخبر المضمن بمعنى السؤال ؛ فلا جواب إلا خبر ومن الخبر ما لا يكون إلا جوابًا» . ويرى ابن عقيل ضرورة مطابقة الجواب للسؤال من غير تغيير أو تبديل، قال : وينبغي للسائل أن ينظر إلى المعنى المطلوب في السؤال، فإن عدل المجيب لم يرض منه إلا بالرجوع إلى جواب ما سأله عنه، فإن كثيرا ممن لا يضبط الجدل ولا يد له فيه، يسأل عن شيء، فيجيب عن غيره وهو يظن أنه قد أجاب، ويقنع منه السائل، إذ كان السائل أقصر منه علما بتحديد الجواب.
B - Definition of the answer: As for the answer, its origin in language is 'cutting', from their saying: 'He cuts through the land', meaning he traverses it. And Allah the Exalted said: 'And Thamud who cut through the rocks in the valley' (89:9), meaning they cut through it. It is called by this what corresponds to the question, because it leads to cutting off (doubt). The respondent cuts off with the meaning of a statement in the manner of affirmation or negation. So when the questioner says: 'Has Zayd come to you?' he has suspended his question on one of two things: either yes or no. 'Yes' is for affirmation, 'no' for negation. The respondent cuts off either with 'yes', affirming that he came, or with 'no', negating that he came. As for the technical definition, Imam al-Haramayn defined it as: 'The informative speech that incorporates the meaning of the question; for there is no answer except an informative statement, and among informative statements there is that which is only an answer.' Ibn 'Aqil holds the necessity of the answer matching the question without change or alteration. He said: The questioner should consider the intended meaning in the question; if the answerer deviates, he should not be satisfied except by returning to answer what he was asked. For many who do not master dialectic and have no skill in it ask about something and answer about something else, thinking they have answered, and the questioner acquiesces, since the questioner is less knowledgeable in specifying the answer.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'jawab' (answer) from two perspectives. First, it gives the linguistic origin: the word 'jawab' derives from 'qat'' (cutting), as in cutting through land, or the Qur'anic verse about Thamud cutting through rocks. The answer is so named because it 'cuts' the uncertainty of the question, providing a decisive affirmation or negation. The author illustrates this with the example: 'Has Zayd come to you?' The response is either 'yes' (affirmation) or 'no' (negation). Second, it presents the technical definition from Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), who says the answer is an informative speech (khabar) that contains the meaning of the question. Not every khabar is an answer, but some khabar are answers. This highlights that the answer must be propositional and directly address the question. Finally, Ibn 'Aqil adds a crucial practical requirement: the answer must exactly match the question without any change or alteration. This prevents the respondent from evading the issue by answering a different question, a common trick among the unskilled. The passage thus establishes both the etymology and the technical conditions for a proper dialectical answer.

Key Terms
مطابقة (mutabaqa): Correspondence; the requirement that the answer exactly matches the question.
تغيير (taghyir): Change or alteration; the answer must not alter the meaning of the question.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn 'Aqil, what should the questioner do if the answerer deviates from the question?
A) Accept the answer as given.
B) Insist on a return to the original question.
C) Start a new topic.
D) Concede defeat.

Key Takeaway
A valid answer must match the question exactly; deviation violates the purpose of dialectic and must be rejected.

Understanding Farḍ in Dialectical Exchange
Defines farḍ and gives example of farḍ in fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a farḍ (hypothetical answer) in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Farḍ: when asked generally, answer with one specific case.

In jadal, every answer is either a farḍ (hypothetical) or a binā' (construction). This lesson focuses on farḍ: the art of answering a general question by selecting one or two specific images. This technique is crucial for managing complex multi-faceted issues in fiqh debates. The passage provides a definition and an example from marriage law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general question
In a farḍ, the questioner (sā'il) asks a comprehensive question that demands a response covering all possible cases. For example: 'What do you say about a woman: Is it valid for her to directly conduct the marriage contract or not?'
2
Recognize the respondent's selection
The respondent (mustadill) does not answer every possible case. Instead, he 'cuts off' (farḍ) one or two specific images and answers only those. This is deliberate, not an oversight.
The term farḍ literally means 'cutting off' and 'estimation'.
3
Classify the answer type
The passage states farḍ has two types: farḍ in fatwa (legal opinion) and another type (not given here). In the example, the respondent lists multiple specific scenarios (independent, delegation, guardian permission, etc.) as possible answers, but does not give a single universal rule.
This is a farḍ because the general question is answered with specific images.
4
Apply to fiqh debates
When you encounter a fiqh debate where a scholar gives a limited answer to a broad question, you are likely seeing farḍ. Recognize it as a dialectical strategy to simplify or avoid ambiguity.
Farḍ helps prevent hasty generalizations and forces precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : مفهوم كل من الفرض والبناء هذا، وقبل بيان وجوه السؤال والجواب لابد من تناول قضية مهمة لها صلة وثيقة بكل من السؤال والجواب، ألا وهي مسألة الفرض والبناء، لأن كل جواب إنما هو فرض أو بناء، فوجب معرفة المراد من كل منهما . أولا : الفرض ومعناه : أن يسأل المستدل سؤالاً عامًا، فيجيب بجواب خاص بصورة واحدة أو صورتين من صور السؤال .. مثل : أن تكون المسألة ذات صور، فيسأل السائل عنها سؤالا يقتضي الجواب عن جميع صورها فيجيب المستدل عن صورة أو صورتين منها، لأن الفرض هو القطع والتقدير، وكأن المستدل اقتطع تلك الصورة عن أخواتها فأجاب عنها . والفرض ضربان : فرض في الفتوى ومن أمثلته ؛ أن يقول السائل : ما تقول في المرأة، هل يصح منها مباشرة عقد النكاح أم لا ؟ فقال : لا يصح منها الاستقلال به، أو يصح فيما إذا وكلت فيه دون غيره، أو يصح بإذن الولي أو يصح فيما إذا نظر الولي في مصلحة
The Second Section: The Concept of Both Farḍ and Binā'. Now, before explaining the aspects of the question and the answer, it is necessary to address an important issue closely related to both the question and the answer, namely the issue of farḍ (hypothetical) and binā' (construction), because every answer is either farḍ or binā'. So it is necessary to know the intended meaning of each. First: Farḍ and its meaning: It is that the questioner (mustadill) asks a general question, and he answers with a specific answer concerning one or two images of the question. For example: When the issue has multiple images, the questioner asks about it in a way that necessitates an answer covering all its images, but the respondent (mustadill) answers with one or two of them, because farḍ is cutting off and estimation; it is as if the respondent cut off that image from its sisters and answered it. Farḍ is of two types: Farḍ in fatwa (legal opinion). An example: The questioner says: 'What do you say about a woman: Is it valid for her to directly conduct the marriage contract or not?' So he says: 'It is not valid for her to do it independently,' or 'It is valid if she delegates someone else,' or 'It is valid with the guardian's permission,' or 'It is valid when the guardian considers her interest.'
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage introduces the important concepts of farḍ (hypothetical) and binā' (construction) in the context of jadal. It states that every answer in dialectic is either a farḍ or a binā', so it is necessary to understand both. The author first defines farḍ: it occurs when the questioner asks a general question requiring a comprehensive answer covering all aspects, but the respondent answers with a specific case (one or two images). This is called farḍ because it involves 'cutting off' (qat') and estimation (taqdir); the respondent selects a particular image from the set of possible answers. This is akin to a hypothetical scenario. Then the author says farḍ has two types, and he gives an example of the first type: farḍ in a fatwa (legal opinion). The example is about a woman conducting her own marriage contract. The questioner asks generally, but the respondent gives multiple specific possibilities (independent, with delegation, with guardian permission, with guardian oversight). The passage ends mid-example, with only the first type mentioned. The second type is missing from this window.

Technical Definition of Jadal
Author presents Imam al-Haramayn's definition of jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define jadal according to Imam al-Haramayn and explain its key components (khabar and question).

Core Idea
Jadal is a statement that inherently carries a question.

This lesson introduces the precise technical definition of jadal as formulated by Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). Understanding this definition is crucial for the entire discipline of dialectic (jadal).

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Find the term being defined (jadal) and its definition: 'al-khabar al-mudammin bi-ma'na al-su'al' (the statement that contains the meaning of a question).
2
Distinguish khabar from question
Recognize that khabar is a proposition that can be true or false, while a question is an inquiry. The definition ties them together.
3
Understand the implication
The definition implies that every answer in jadal is a khabar, and some khabars are exclusively answers (i.e., they do not stand alone).
4
Apply the definition
Test whether a given statement qualifies as jadal by checking if it contains the meaning of a question and is a khabar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما في الاصطلاح فقد عرفه إمام الحرمين بأنه: «الخبر المضمن بمعنى السؤال ؛ فلا جواب إلا خبر ومن الخبر ما لا يكون إلا جوابًا» (1). ويرى ابن عقيل ضرورة مطابقة الجواب للسؤال من غير تغيير أو تبديل، قال : وينبغي للسائل أن ينظر إلى المعنى المطلوب في السؤال، فإن عدل المجيب لم يرض منه إلا بالرجوع إلى جواب ما سأله عنه، فإن كثيرا ممن لا يضبط الجدل ولا يد له فيه، يسأل عن شيء، فيجيب عن غيره وهو يظن أنه قد أجاب، ويقنع منه السائل، إذ كان السائل أقصر منه علما بتحديد الجواب (2).
As for technical terminology, Imam al-Haramayn defined it as: 'The statement that contains the meaning of a question; no answer is except a statement, and among statements there is what is only an answer.' Ibn 'Aqil held the necessity of the answer matching the question without alteration or substitution. He said: It is incumbent upon the questioner to look at the intended meaning in the question. If the respondent deviates, he should not be satisfied except by returning to answering what he was asked about. Indeed, many of those who do not master dialectic and have no hand in it, ask about something and he answers about something else, thinking that he has answered, and the questioner is satisfied with him, because the questioner is shorter in knowledge of defining the answer.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'jadal' (dialectic) in its technical sense and establishes a fundamental rule for disputation: the answer must precisely match the question. The author first presents the definition of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni): jadal is a statement (khabar) that inherently contains the meaning of a question. The implication is that every answer in a dialectical exchange is a statement, and some statements are exclusively answers (i.e., they do not stand alone as independent propositions). Then the author reports Ibn 'Aqil's view, which emphasizes the necessity of correspondence between answer and question. Ibn 'Aqil adds procedural guidance: the questioner must carefully consider the exact meaning sought in his question; if the respondent deviates, the questioner should insist on a direct answer. The passage criticizes those who are unskilled in dialectic, answering a different question while believing they have satisfied the inquiry. The common misunderstanding would be that jadal is any argumentative exchange; in fact, it is a highly structured discourse where the answer is determined by the question.

Key Terms
الجدل
الخبر
السؤال
الجواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Any argument between two people.
B) A statement that contains the meaning of a question.
C) A question that contains a statement.
D) A debate with fixed rules.

Key Takeaway
Jadal is defined as a statement that carries a question, making it the foundation of structured dialectical exchange.

Rule of Answer-Question Correspondence
Author reports Ibn 'Aqil's view on answer-question correspondence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the answer must match the question in a dialectical exchange and identify deviations.

Core Idea
In jadal, the answer must be a direct response to the exact question asked.

Ibn 'Aqil stresses a key rule that distinguishes proper jadal from sophistry: the respondent must answer the precise question, and the questioner must hold him to it. This lesson explores that rule and its implications.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Ibn 'Aqil requires the answer to match the question without alteration or substitution.
2
Identify the questioner's duty
The questioner must carefully consider the intended meaning of his question.
The questioner is not passive; he actively monitors the correspondence.
3
Recognize the respondent's violation
The respondent may answer a different question, either intentionally or out of ignorance.
The passage highlights this as a common error among the unskilled.
4
Apply the remedy
The questioner should insist on a direct answer to the original question, not accept a substitute.
5
Understand the consequence
If the questioner is less knowledgeable, he may be satisfied with an irrelevant answer, undermining the purpose of jadal.
The rule protects the integrity of the dialectical process.

Source Text

Understanding Farḍ: Assumption in Dialectics
Author defines farḍ and its two types with an ensuing debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of farḍ (assumption) in dialectical contexts and analyze the arguments for and against its use.

Core Idea
Farḍ is a justified dialectical tool when evidence is incomplete, balancing between ignorance and silence.

Before exploring the types of questions and answers in dialectic, the author insists we understand farḍ (assumption) and its counterpart binā'. Every answer is either a farḍ or a binā'. This lesson focuses on farḍ: its definition, two types, and the scholarly debate about its legitimacy. By mastering farḍ, you gain insight into how dialecticians handle incomplete evidence while striving for productive discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of farḍ
Find the passage where farḍ is defined: it is answering a general question with a specific case (one or two forms). The author notes the literal meaning 'cutting off'—the respondent isolates a particular form.
Contrast farḍ with binā', though binā' is not elaborated here.
2
Distinguish the two types of farḍ
The passage distinguishes farḍ in fatwa (legal ruling) and farḍ in evidence. For farḍ in fatwa, the questioner asks about a general ruling (e.g., woman's marriage contract) and the respondent answers with a specific scenario. For farḍ in evidence, a general fatwa is supported by evidence that only applies to a specific case (e.g., using the hadith on ribā to invalidate a corrupt sale).
The example for farḍ in fatwa lists several scenarios; any one chosen is a farḍ.
3
Analyze the debate on permissibility
Ibn Furak rejects farḍ because the answer must match the question (specific does not match general). Others allow it to avoid two greater harms: giving a fatwa without knowledge (ḥarām) or concealing knowledge (ḥarām). They invoke the principle 'what cannot be fully attained should not be entirely abandoned'. Imam al-Haramayn adds a condition: farḍ is allowed only if the cause (ʿilla) of the assumption encompasses all aspects; the commendable farḍ falls within the generality of the question.
Note the shift: farḍ in usul al-fiqh means obligation; here it means assumption/restriction.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : مفهوم كل من الفرض والبناء هذا، وقبل بيان وجوه السؤال والجواب لابد من تناول قضية مهمة لها صلة وثيقة بكل من السؤال والجواب، ألا وهي مسألة الفرض والبناء»، لأن كل جواب إنما هو فرض أو بناء، فوجب معرفة المراد من كل منهما . أولا : الفرض ومعناه : أن يسأل المستدل سؤالاً عامًا، فيجيب بجواب خاص بصورة واحدة أو صورتين من صور السؤال .. مثل : أن تكون المسألة ذات صور، فيسأل السائل عنها سؤالا يقتضي الجواب عن جميع صورها فيجيب المستدل عن صورة أو صورتين منها، لأن الفرض هو القطع والتقدير، وكأن المستدل اقتطع تلك الصورة عن أخواتها فأجاب عنها (3) والفرض ضربان : فرض في الفتوى ومن أمثلته ؛ أن يقول السائل : ما تقول في المرأة، هل يصح منها مباشرة عقد النكاح أم لا ؟ فقال : لا يصح منها الاستقلال به، أويصح فيما إذا وكلت فيه دون غيره، أو يصح بإذن الولي أو يصح فيما إذا نظر الولي في مصلحة العقد ولم تبق إلا مباشرته ؛ فكل هذه صور لمباشرة المرأة عقد النكاح، فأيها خص بالجواب كان فارضا (1) . وفرض في الدليل مثل أن يفتي عاما ثم يدل خاصا مثل أن يقول : لا ينعقد البيع الفاسد، لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن بيع درهم بدرهمين وقد ذهب الأستاذ ابن فورك (3) إلى أن الفرض في الفتوى أو في الدليل لا يجوز، لأن حق الجواب أن يطابق السؤال، وحق الدليل أن يطابق المدلول، والخاص من ذلك لا يطابق العام، فلا يجوز . . عداه وذهب من من الجدليين إلى خلافه في ذلك وجواز الفرض، لأن المسئول قد لا يجد دليلا إلا على بعض صور السؤال فيدور الأمر بين أن يكلف له (4) أن تعميم الجواب فيما عدا تلك الصورة فيكون مفتيًا بغير علم وهو حرام، وبين يسكت عن الجواب بالكلية فيكون كاتمًا للعلم في الصورة التي عرف دليلها وهو حرام أيضًا ومنع من فائدة يستفيد بها السائل، وما لا يدرك بكليته لا يترك بكليته، وبعض الشر أهون من بعض وقال إمام الحرمين : إنما يجوز إذا كانت علة الفرض شاملة لسائر الأطراف، قال : والمستحسن منه هو الواقع في طرف يشتمل عليه عموم سؤال السائل، وذلك محمول على استشعار انتشار الكلام في جميع الأطراف وعدم وفاء مجلس واحد باستتمام الكلام فيها (5)
Second topic: The concept of farḍ (assumption) and binā' (construction). Before explaining the aspects of question and answer, it is necessary to address an important issue closely related to both question and answer, namely the issue of 'farḍ and binā'', because every answer is either a farḍ or a binā'. So one must know the meaning of each. First: farḍ, and its meaning: that the respondent asks a general question, and he answers with a specific answer concerning one or two forms of the question... For example: the issue has forms, the questioner asks about it with a question that requires answering all its forms, and the respondent answers concerning one or two forms of them, because farḍ is cutting and estimation, as if the respondent cut that form from its sisters and answered about it. Farḍ is of two types: farḍ in fatwa (legal ruling) – an example of it: the questioner says: 'What do you say about a woman: is direct conclusion of a marriage contract valid from her or not?' and he says: 'It is not valid for her independently, or it is valid if she appoints an agent, or it is valid with guardian's permission, or it is valid if the guardian considers the benefit of the contract and nothing remains but her direct execution.' All these are forms of a woman directly concluding a marriage contract. Whichever is specified in the answer is a farḍ. And farḍ in evidence, such as giving a general fatwa and then giving specific evidence, like saying: 'A corrupt sale is not concluded because the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) forbade selling a dirham for two dirhams.' Al-Ustadh Ibn Furak held that farḍ in fatwa or in evidence is not permissible, because the right of the answer is to match the question, and the right of the evidence is to match the proven, and the specific does not match the general, so it is not allowed. But some of the dialecticians differed and allowed farḍ, because the respondent may not find evidence except for some forms of the question, so the matter revolves between either requiring him to generalize the answer beyond that form, thus giving a fatwa without knowledge, which is forbidden, or remaining silent about the answer entirely, thus concealing knowledge in the form for which he knows evidence, which is also forbidden and prevents a benefit the questioner would obtain. And 'what cannot be fully attained should not be entirely abandoned', and 'some evil is lesser than another'. Imam al-Haramayn said: 'It is only allowed if the cause (ʿilla) of the farḍ encompasses all the other aspects.' He said: 'The commendable among it is what occurs in an aspect that is included in the generality of the questioner's question, and that is based on perceiving the spread of speech across all aspects and that a single session is insufficient to complete discussion of them.'
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of 'farḍ' (assumption) and its counterpart 'binā'' (construction) in dialectical methodology. Before analyzing the types of questions and answers, the author states that every answer is either a farḍ or a binā', so understanding these terms is essential. The definition of farḍ: it is when the respondent gives a general question but answers with a specific case (one or two forms) from among the possible forms. The word 'farḍ' literally means cutting off or estimating; the respondent effectively isolates one form. Two types are distinguished: (1) farḍ in fatwa, where the questioner asks about a general ruling and the respondent answers about a particular case, as in the example of a woman concluding a marriage contract under various circumstances; (2) farḍ in evidence, where a general ruling is given but the evidence cited supports only a specific case, e.g., invalidating a corrupt sale citing the prohibition of ribā al-faḍl (selling a dirham for two dirhams). The passage then presents a disagreement: Ibn Furak rejects farḍ entirely because the answer must match the question (specific does not match general). Others allow it, arguing that the respondent may lack evidence for all forms; forced either to give a fatwa without knowledge (ḥarām) or to conceal knowledge (ḥarām and wasteful). The principle 'what cannot be fully attained should not be entirely abandoned' justifies choosing the lesser evil. Lastly, Imam al-Haramayn permits farḍ only if the cause (ʿilla) of the assumption applies to all aspects; the commendable farḍ is one that falls within the scope of the question's generality, acknowledging the impracticality of covering everything in one session.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-farḍ)
البناء (al-binā')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is an example of farḍ in fatwa given in the passage?
A) A question about the validity of a sale, answered with a specific type of currency.
B) A question about a woman's marriage contract, answered with one specific scenario.
C) A question about prayer times, answered with a specific hadith.
D) A question about inheritance shares, answered with a general principle.

Key Takeaway
Farḍ allows a respondent to answer a general question with a specific case when evidence is limited, justified by the principle of choosing the lesser evil and conditional on the 'illa's comprehensiveness.

Identify the two types of farḍ in the following scenario: A student asks 'What is the ruling on selling dates for dates?' The teacher responds by citing a hadith about ribā al-faḍl (selling dates for dates with excess) and concludes that any unequal exchange is prohibited. Which type of farḍ (if any) is this? Explain.
?
Evaluate the permissibility of the following farḍ using Imam al-Haramayn's condition: A questioner asks about the rulings on all types of financial transactions. The respondent answers only about sale (bayʿ) and claims his evidence (a specific verse) covers all transactions. Does this satisfy the condition? Why or why not?

The First Benefit of Al-Fard: Answering What You Know
Al-Tufi defends al-fard and explains its first benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first benefit of al-fard and construct a dialectical response using a specific case when general evidence is unknown.

Core Idea
Al-fard lets you answer a known case, achieving benefit without harm.

In dialectical debate, a respondent may not have evidence for all cases of a general question. Al-Tufi defends the practice of al-fard (specifying a case) as a legitimate strategy. The first benefit is that by answering only the case whose evidence is known, the respondent provides a correct answer and obtains a pure benefit, since the questioner is also asking about that case. Let's explore how this works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general question
Imagine the questioner asks about a broad category, e.g., 'Is it permissible for a woman to directly conduct a marriage contract?' This includes many individual cases.
The general question is the starting point.
2
Locate the specific case you know
Al-Tufi says the respondent should answer only the specific case (sura) for which he has clear evidence. For example, the case where the guardian has done everything except the utterance.
This is al-fard: narrowing to a specific instance.
3
Assess benefit and harm
The benefit is providing a correct answer for that case. The harm is absent because the questioner, by asking about all cases, is also asking about that particular case, so the answer is relevant and not misleading.
Al-Tufi calls this a 'pure benefit not opposed by any harm'.
4
Formulate the strategical response
The respondent should say: 'Regarding the specific case where the guardian has completed all preliminaries except the utterance, the ruling is such-and-such.' This uses al-fard to give a precise, evidence-based answer.
General answers are avoided when evidence is lacking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الطوفي منتصرًا لجواز الفرض، ومبينا فائدته : وَبَيْن، أن يجيب فيما ظهر له دليله دون غيره، وذلك متضمن لمصلحة محضة لا تعارضها مفسدة، فيكون متعينًا، أما مصلحته فحصول الفائدة بجواب الصورة التي عرف دليلها وأما انتفاء المفسدة فلأن السائل عن جميع الصور سائل عن بعضها فالجواب بالنسبة إلى ذلك البعض الذي عرف دليله مطابق . وأما الفائدة الثانية للفرض، فلأن المسئول قد يرد على جوابه العام إشكال لا يندفع فيتخلص منه بالفرض الخاص، مثل أن يُسأل عن جواز مباشرة المرأة عقد النكاح فيقول : لا يجوز لها الاستقلال به لقصورها فإن هذا يتمشى له، ولو أجاب عاما وعلل بالقصور لم يتمش له في جميع الصور، لأن من جملة صور السؤال أن ينظر الولي في مصلحة النكاح ويقرر مقدماته حتى لا يبقى إلا التلفظ بصيغة الإيجاب فتوجب المرأة، وهاهنا القصور مأمون الغائلة فلا يصلح بمجرده أن يكون مانعا فيبطل به دليل المستدل، فبعدوله إلى نفي خصوص الاستقلال تخلص عن هذا الإشكال. وهاتان فائدتان جليلتان للفرض فلا يهمل تحصيلهما
Al-Tufi said, in defense of the permissibility of al-fard (specifying a case) and clarifying its benefit: It is clear that one should answer regarding what appears to him its evidence, not others. This entails a pure benefit not opposed by any harm, so it becomes necessary. As for its benefit: it is the attainment of benefit by answering the specific case whose evidence he knows. As for the absence of harm: because the questioner, regarding all cases, is a questioner regarding some of them, so the answer relative to that particular case whose evidence he knows is correct. As for the second benefit of al-fard: the respondent may face an objection to his general answer that cannot be repelled, so he escapes from it by a specific case. For example, he is asked about a woman directly conducting a marriage contract; he says: 'She is not permitted to be independent in it due to her deficiency (qusur).' This works for him. But if he answered generally and gave the deficiency as the reason, it would not work for him in all cases, because among the cases of the question is that the guardian considers the benefit of the marriage and completes its preliminaries until nothing remains but the utterance of the offer (ijab), and then the woman makes the contract. Here, the deficiency is safe from harm, so it alone cannot be a prevention, thus the evidence of the one seeking proof would be invalid. By turning to denying the specific independence, he escapes this objection. These are two great benefits of al-fard, so they should not be neglected in attainment.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is an excerpt from al-Tufi's defense of the practice of al-fard (specifying a case) in dialectical debate (jadal). Al-Tufi explains that al-fard is permissible and beneficial. He presents two benefits. First, the respondent can answer a specific case (sura) whose evidence he knows, while general answers might lack evidence. This is a pure benefit with no harm because the questioner, who asked about all cases, is also asking about that particular case, so answering it is correct. Second, al-fard allows the respondent to escape a difficult objection that a general answer might face. Al-Tufi provides a fiqhi example: if asked about a woman conducting her own marriage contract, a general answer that she cannot due to deficiency (qusur) fails if the guardian has already done everything except the utterance — then deficiency is not a real issue. By narrowing to the specific case of 'independence' (istiqlal), the respondent avoids the objection. Al-Tufi concludes that these two benefits are significant and should not be neglected.

Key Terms
الفرض
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It allows the responder to give a general answer that covers all cases.
B) It allows the responder to answer only the specific case whose evidence he knows, achieving benefit without harm.
C) It forces the questioner to accept the answer.
D) It makes the debate shorter.

Key Takeaway
Al-fard enables the respondent to answer a known case, providing benefit and avoiding the harm of unsupported general claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a respondent in a dialectical debate. The questioner asks: 'Is it obligatory to perform ghusl (ritual bath) before every prayer after janaba? You only know the evidence for the case of the fajr prayer. Use al-fard to formulate a precise answer that achieves benefit without harm.

The Second Benefit of Al-Fard: Escaping Objections
Al-Tufi defends al-fard and explains its second benefit: escaping objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a general answer is vulnerable to an objection and use al-fard to construct a specific response that avoids the objection.

Core Idea
Al-fard rescues you when a general answer faces an unanswerable objection.

Sometimes a general answer is logically sound in principle but becomes invalid when tested against a specific case. Al-Tufi illustrates this with a fiqhi example: answering that a woman cannot conduct a marriage contract due to deficiency fails when the guardian has already done everything except the utterance. By specifying the case as one of independence (istiqlal), the respondent escapes the objection. This is the second great benefit of al-fard.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general answer and its 'illa
The respondent first gives a general answer: 'A woman cannot directly conduct a marriage contract due to her deficiency (qusur).' The 'illa is the deficiency.
General answers use a universal reason.
2
Recognize the objection to the general answer
The questioner presents a case within the general question where the deficiency is not present: the guardian has completed all preliminaries except the utterance. In that case, the deficiency is 'safe from harm', so the 'illa does not apply. Thus the general answer fails.
This is a classic dialectical objection: the 'illa is not universal.
3
Apply al-fard to specify a case
The respondent narrows the answer to a specific case: 'She is not permitted to be independent in it.' Instead of ‘deficiency’, he uses ‘independence’ as the key factor. This avoids the objection because the problematic case is about dependency, not deficiency.
Al-fard transforms the answer into a specific one that withstands criticism.
4
Evaluate the escape from objection
The specific answer is correct for the case of independence, and the objection no longer applies. The respondent has successfully defended his position using al-fard.
The debate map below formalizes this exchange.

The Meaning of Al-Bina' in Jadal
Defines al-bina' with an example from marriage annulment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-bina' in jadal and illustrate it with the marriage annulment example.

Core Idea
Al-bina' extends a ruling from one case to others by qiyas.

In Islamic dialectics, al-bina' is a crucial tool allowing a responder to maintain consistency. This lesson unpacks its definition and a vivid example from marriage law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find the sentence that defines al-bina'. Notice the phrase 'معناه' and the structure of the definition.
2
Parse the example
Read the example carefully: the question is about annulment due to defects. The responder chooses leprosy as a specific case and cites a hadith.
3
Extract the method of building
Identify the two ways mentioned: either by using the same evidence for other cases, or by finding a common attribute (معنى جامع) for analogy.
4
Understand the role of qiyas
See how the responder uses qiyas: he states a general attribute ('a defect that prevents the purpose of the contract') and applies it to all similar defects.

البناء : ومعناه : أن المسئول إذا أجاب في صورة الفرض فهل يلزمه أن يبني غيرها من صور السؤال عليها، إما بأن يقرر عين دليلها في بقية الصور أو يبني غيرها من صور السؤال عليها، إما بأن يقرر عين دليلها في بقية الصور أو يجمع بين بقية الصور وبينها بمعنى جامع على قاعدة القياس، مثل أن يسأل عن فسخ النكاح بالعيوب فيفرض الكلام في البرص ويدل عليه بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم تزوج امرأة فوجد بكشحها بياضا، فقال لها : (الحقي بأهلك) ، ثم يبني الفسخ ببقية العيوب عليه، بأن يقول : عيب مفوت لمقصود العاقد فيثبت الفسخ به كالبرص . فهذا معنى البناء ومثاله . أما حكمه فمن القائلين بالفرض من أوجبه على المسئول توفية بعموم اقتضاه السؤال، أو بعموم ما ألزمه هو من الفتوى فيما إذا كان فرضه في الدليل. ومنهم من لم يوجبه لوجهين أحدهما ؛ أنه يضيع فائدتي الفرض المذكورتين لأنا إذا ألزمناه الجواب في بقية صور السؤال مع أنه قد لا نجد دليلا إلا في بعضها، فقد ألزمناه القول بغير علم أو كلفناه ما لا يطاق وهو الجمع بين الجواب في تلك الصور وعدمه، والوجه الثاني ؛ أن ذلك تناقض ممن أوجب البناء لأن تجويزه للفرض تقرير منه للمسئول على الاقتصار في الجواب على بعض صور السؤال، وإيجاب البناء عليه رجوع عن ذلك التقرير .. ومنهم من فصل فقال : إن كان الفرض في بعض صور السؤال كما إذا سئل عن إزالة النجاسة بما سوى المائعات ففرض الجواب في بعضها كالخل ونحوه ، لم يلزمه البناء، وإن كان في غير محل السؤال، مثل : إن سئل هل يقتل المسلم بالذمي؟ فقال : لا يقتل بالمعاهد، لزمه البناء.. والفرق أنه في الأول أجاب عن محل السؤال في الجملة وفي الثاني لم يجب عنه أصلا فهو كما قيل: سالته عن أبيه، فقال: خالي شعيب . وأصح الأقوال وأشهرها جواز الفرض وعدم وجوب البناء
Al-bina' (building): Its meaning is that when the responder answers on a specific case (al-fard), is he obligated to build other cases of the question upon it, either by establishing the same evidence in the remaining cases or by combining the remaining cases and it by a unifying meaning on the basis of qiyas (analogy)? For example, he is asked about annulment of marriage due to defects; he specifies the discussion to leprosy (baras) and provides evidence that the Prophet (peace be upon him) married a woman and found whiteness on her side and said to her: 'Go back to your family.' Then he builds the annulment for the remaining defects upon it, by saying: 'A defect that prevents the purpose of the contracting party, so annulment is established by it, like leprosy.' This is the meaning of al-bina' and its example. As for its ruling: Among those who allow al-fard, some oblige it upon the responder to fulfill the generality required by the question, or the generality of what he himself obliged of fatwa if his fard was in the evidence. Some did not oblige it for two reasons: First, that it wastes the two mentioned benefits of al-fard, because if we oblige him to answer for the remaining cases while he may not find evidence except in some of them, we have obliged him to speak without knowledge or charged him with something he cannot bear, which is combining answering those cases and not answering. The second reason: that is a contradiction from whoever obliges al-bina', because his permitting al-fard is an acknowledgment for the responder to limit the answer to some cases, and obliging al-bina' is a retraction from that acknowledgment. And some made a distinction: If the fard is in some of the cases of the question, like when asked about removing impurity with non-liquids, he specifies the answer in some of them like vinegar etc., then building is not obligatory. But if it is outside the scope of the question, like when asked: 'Is a Muslim killed for a dhimmi?' He says: 'He is not killed for a mu'ahid (covenanted person),' then building is obligatory. The difference is that in the first he answered the scope of the question in general, and in the second he did not answer it at all, as it is said: I asked him about his father, and he said: My maternal uncle Shu'ayb. And the most correct and well-known opinion is the permissibility of al-fard and that al-bina' is not obligatory.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage defines al-bina' (building) in jadal. Al-bina' refers to the obligation of the responder, after answering a specific case (al-fard), to extend that answer to other cases of the original question. The author illustrates with a fiqhi example: if asked about annulling marriage due to defects, the responder may first discuss leprosy (baras) using a hadith where the Prophet told a woman to return to her family due to whiteness. Then, to build other defects upon it, he uses a general attribute: 'a defect that prevents the purpose of the contract' — thus analogizing all such defects to leprosy. The ruling is debated: some make al-bina' obligatory to cover the generality of the question; others argue it wastes the benefits of al-fard and is contradictory; a third group distinguishes based on whether the fard was within the scope of the question or outside it. The author concludes that the most correct and famous opinion is that al-fard is permissible and al-bina' is not obligatory.

Key Terms
البناء
الفرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two methods of building mentioned in the definition of al-bina'?
A) By direct evidence and by analogy
B) By induction and deduction
C) By consensus and analogy
D) By disjunction and conjunction

Key Takeaway
Al-bina' is the dialectical practice of extending an answer from a specific case to other cases using either the same evidence or analogical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following scenario: In a debate about whether sale of fruits before they ripen is valid, the responder first discusses grapes using a hadith. Write a short al-bina' extension to dates using a common attribute.
?
Identify which method of building (same evidence or unifying meaning) is used in a given example from fiqh of prayer.

The Ruling on Al-Bina': Obligation or Permissibility?
Discusses the ruling on al-bina' with three opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the three opinions on the ruling of al-bina' and identify which is considered most correct according to the passage.

Core Idea
The most correct view is that al-fard is permissible but al-bina' is not obligatory.

After understanding what al-bina' is, we now turn to the debate about whether it is obligatory. This lesson examines the arguments for and against, and a nuanced distinction, before revealing the preferred position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first opinion
Locate the group that obliges al-bina'. Note their reasoning: to fulfill the generality of the question or the fatwa.
2
Identify the second opinion
Find those who do not oblige it. Note their two reasons: wasting the benefits of al-fard and contradiction.
3
Understand the third, detailed opinion
This group distinguishes based on whether the fard was within the scope of the question or outside it. Examine the two examples: impurity removal vs. killing a Muslim for a dhimmi.
4
Conclude with the preferred view
The author states the most correct and famous opinion: permissibility of al-fard and non-obligation of al-bina'.

The Ruling on Farḍ and Binā' in Jadal
Presents the two opinions on obligating the respondent to build upon a hypothetical case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three positions on obligating binā' and identify which scenario requires binā' according to the detailed view.

Core Idea
Farḍ narrows the debate; binā' builds on it – but not all scholars agree it is obligatory.

In jadal, when a respondent specifies a hypothetical case (farḍ), must they also address the broader question (binā')? This passage presents the differing opinions among usuli scholars, including the reasons for and against obligating binā', and a crucial distinction between farḍ within or outside the question's scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
Locate the three groups in the passage: those who obligate binā', those who do not (with two reasons), and those who make a detailed distinction.
2
Extract the two reasons against obligating binā'
What are the 'wasting benefits' argument and the 'contradiction' argument? Paraphrase them in your own words.
The first reason is that it forces the respondent to answer without evidence or burdens him impossibly; the second is that permitting farḍ then obligating binā' is a contradiction.
3
Understand the detailed distinction
When does farḍ fall within vs. outside the question's scope? Give examples from the text: vinegar example vs. mu'āhad example.
Farḍ within: e.g., specifying vinegar among liquids for impurity removal. Farḍ outside: e.g., answering about mu'āhad when asked about dhimmī.
4
Determine the preferred view
Which opinion is considered 'the soundest and most famous'? Is it the same as the detailed view?
The passage concludes that farḍ is permissible and binā' is not obligatory, which aligns with the second opinion, but the detailed view is also discussed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما حكمه فمن القائلين بالفرض من أوجبه على المسئول توفية بعموم اقتضاه السؤال، أو بعموم ما ألزمه هو من الفتوى فيما إذا كان فرضه في الدليل. ومنهم من لم يوجبه لوجهين أحدهما ؛ أنه يضيع فائدتي الفرض المذكورتين لأنا إذا ألزمناه الجواب في بقية صور السؤال مع أنه قد لا نجد دليلا إلا في بعضها، فقد ألزمناه القول بغير علم أو كلفناه ما لا يطاق وهو الجمع بين الجواب في تلك الصور وعدمه، والوجه الثاني ؛ أن ذلك تناقض ممن أوجب البناء لأن تجويزه للفرض تقرير منه للمسئول على الاقتصار في الجواب على بعض صور السؤال، وإيجاب البناء عليه رجوع عن ذلك التقرير .. ومنهم من فصل فقال : إن كان الفرض في بعض صور السؤال كما إذا سئل عن إزالة النجاسة بما سوى المائعات ففرض الجواب في بعضها كالخل ونحوه ، لم يلزمه البناء، وإن كان في غير محل السؤال، مثل : إن سئل هل يقتل المسلم بالذمي؟ فقال : لا يقتل بالمعاهد، لزمه البناء.. والفرق أنه في الأول أجاب عن محل السؤال في الجملة وفي الثاني لم يجب عنه أصلا فهو كما قيل: سالته عن أبيه، فقال: خالي شعيب . وأصح الأقوال وأشهرها جواز الفرض وعدم وجوب البناء
As for its ruling: among those who affirm farḍ, some obligate it on the respondent as a fulfillment of the generality implied by the question, or as a fulfillment of the generality of what he himself obligated of fatwā in case his farḍ was in the evidence. And among them are those who did not obligate it for two reasons: one of them is that it wastes the two benefits of farḍ mentioned, because if we obligate him to answer in the remaining forms of the question while we may not find evidence except in some of them, then we have obligated him to speak without knowledge, or we burden him with what cannot be endured, namely combining answer in those forms and its absence. The second reason is that this is a contradiction from whoever obligated binā', because his permitting farḍ is an affirmation from him to the respondent of restricting the answer to some forms of the question, and obligating binā' upon it is a retraction from that affirmation. And among them is one who made a detail: if the farḍ was in some forms of the question, such as when asked about removing impurity with something other than liquids, and he specified the answer in some of them like vinegar and the like, then binā' is not obligatory upon him; but if it was outside the place of the question, such as: if he is asked 'Is a Muslim killed for a dhimmī?' and he says 'No, he is not killed for a mu'āhad', then binā' is obligatory upon him. The difference is that in the first he answered the place of the question in general, and in the second he did not answer it at all. So it is as was said: I asked him about his father, he said: my maternal uncle Shu'ayb. And the soundest and most famous opinion is the permissibility of farḍ and the non-obligation of binā'.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the ruling of farḍ (hypothetical specification) and binā' (building upon it) in jadal. It opens by stating that among those who affirm farḍ, some obligate binā' on the respondent to fulfill the generality of the question or the fatwā. Then it presents the opposing view with two reasons: first, it wastes the benefits of farḍ by forcing an answer where evidence may be lacking; second, it contradicts the permission of farḍ itself, which allows restricting the answer. A third opinion makes a distinction: if the farḍ is within the scope of the question (e.g., specifying vinegar among liquids for impurity removal), binā' is not required; if the farḍ is outside the scope (e.g., answering about a mu'āhad when asked about a dhimmī), binā' is required, as the respondent did not answer the actual question. The passage concludes with the preferred view: farḍ is permissible, but binā' is not obligatory.

Key Terms
الفرض
البناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, binā' is obligatory in which case?
A) farḍ in some forms of the question
B) farḍ outside the place of the question
C) both
D) neither

Key Takeaway
The soundest opinion in jadal is that farḍ is permissible, but binā' is not obligatory unless the respondent fails to answer the original question.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In a jadal debate, a student asks: 'Is it permissible to sell dates for dates?' The respondent says: 'It is permissible if the quantities are equal.' According to the passage, does binā' oblige him to also answer for unequal quantities? Use the distinction between farḍ within or outside the question's scope.
?
Task 2: Construct a scenario where farḍ is outside the question's scope (like the mu'āhad example) and explain why binā' would be obligatory. Use an example from fiqh (e.g., zakāt on different types of wealth).

Al-Juwaynī's Rules of Farḍ and Binā' in Jadal
Explains the procedure of farḍ and binā' between questioner and respondent in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a jadal exchange and determine when a participant is cut off or obstinate based on al-Juwaynī's rules.

Core Idea
In jadal, strategic flexibility is key - but inconsistency leads to cutoff, and obstruction leads to obstinacy.

Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwaynī provides a detailed procedural guide for the dialectical method of farḍ (specifying a focus) and binā' (building on a precedent). This passage outlines the rights and responsibilities of both the questioner and the respondent, and the critical junctures at which a participant may be deemed defeated (munqati') or obstinate (muta'annit). Understanding these rules is essential for engaging in structured theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the strategic choice of focus
The questioner should choose a side where his doubt is strong and the respondent weak; but the respondent has the right to shift the focus unless he gave the questioner a free choice.
Respondent's flexibility is a key advantage.
2
Determine when the respondent is cut off
If the respondent gives a general answer and later specifies, he is cut off because his general answer implied he could support the entire issue.
Consistency is paramount; changing tactics mid-debate signals defeat.
3
Analyze the dynamics of binā'
When the respondent insists on building on another case, the questioner must follow unless the respondent cannot explain the building (then the respondent is fleeing).
The questioner cannot force a narrow focus if the respondent justifies building.
4
Recognize obstinacy and legitimate challenges
The questioner is obstinate if he insists on a specific farḍ that prevents the respondent from proceeding. However, if the questioner accepts the basis but then demands proof, he is mistaken unless he intends to expose the respondent's inability within his own principles.
Strategic challenges are allowed, but unreasonable persistence is not.
