ويبين إمام الحرمين الجويني رحمه الله طريقة جريان الفرض والبناء في الجدل بين السائل والمسئول، فيقول : إذا كانت المسألة عامة الحكم في جوانب كلها على الخلاف؛ فالأولى بالسائل أن يعين الموضع الذي تكون الشبهة عليه فيه أكثر والكلام عليه فيه يسهل وعلى المسئول أشد فربما يعين المسئول الكلام فيه ويفرض الدليل عليه، فإذا أطلق ربما فرض الكلام حيث يتعذر على السائل مكالمته ولا تنحل شبهته بكلام المجيب في ذلك المكان . غير أن الأمر إلى المسئول في الفرق، فإن عين السائل عليه مكانًا، فله أن ينقله إلى أي جانب شاء، فإن خير المسئول السائل في فرض المسألة في جانب، فاختار جانبا يصعب على المسئول الكلام فيه لم يكن له نقل السائل بعده، فإن فعل كان منقطعًا . . وإن أطلق السائل سؤاله في مثل هذا وأطلق المسئول الجواب حوله بحيث يعم جوانب المسألة، ثم بعد الشروع في النظر وإقامة الدلالة على الإطلاق تزيد في فرض المسألة وتعيينها في بعض الجوانب كان منقطعا، لأنه بإطلاقه في جوابه ودليله تضمن نصرة المسألة بأطرافها وقد ظهر عجزه عن تصحيحها بما ذكر من الكلام . . . ومتى امتنع المسئول عن جواب ما يسأل عنه إلا بالبناء على غيره، وكشف عن وجه صحة ابتنائه عليه لزم السائل متابعته وترك ما فرض به سؤاله، فإن لم ينفذ للمسئول إلا بالكلام فيما بين وفرض فيه سؤاله، كان متعنتا، اللهم إلا أن يكون المسئول غير قادر على بيان كيفية البناء، حينئذ كان هاربًا، والسائل في ترك متابعته له مصيبا . وإذا بان للسائل وجه البناء، وكان ما بنى عليه مُسَلَّما عنده، كان في مطالبته له بتصحيحه ثم ببناء الفرع عليه مخطئا، إلا أن يريد به بيان عجزه بأصوله عن تصحيحه، فيكون كالقادح في أصوله، لكنه لا يتم للسائل ذلك إلا بأن ينازعه في شيء مما يهدم به هذه القاعدة عن أصوله التي لا نكون في ترك المنازعة في تسليم هذه القاعدة وتصحيحها»
And Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwaynī, may Allah have mercy on him, explains the method of the flow of farḍ and binā' in jadal between the questioner and the respondent. He says: If the issue is general in its ruling in all aspects, on the basis of disagreement, then it is better for the questioner to specify the place where the doubt is more against him and where speech is easier for him and harder for the respondent. Perhaps the respondent specifies the speech in it and makes the farḍ upon the evidence. If he leaves it open, perhaps the farḍ is placed where communication with the questioner is difficult and his doubt is not resolved by the speech of the respondent in that place. However, the matter is up to the respondent regarding the distinction. If the questioner specifies a place for him, he may transfer it to any side he wishes. If the respondent gave the questioner a choice in making farḍ of the issue on a side, and he chose a side that is difficult for the respondent to speak about, then he cannot transfer the questioner afterwards. If he does, he is cut off. And if the questioner made his question open in such a case, and the respondent made his answer open around it such that it encompasses the sides of the issue, then after beginning consideration and establishing the evidence on the open basis, he adds to the farḍ of the issue and specifies it in some sides, he is cut off, because by his openness in his answer and his evidence, he implied supporting the issue in its entirety, and his inability to correct it by what he mentioned of speech has become apparent. And whenever the respondent refrains from answering what he is asked about except by building upon something else, and reveals the aspect of the correctness of his building upon it, the questioner must follow him and abandon what he made his question based on farḍ. If the respondent cannot proceed except by speech in between, and made his question based on farḍ in it, he is obstinate. Unless the respondent is unable to explain the manner of building; then he is fleeing, and the questioner is correct in not following him. And if the aspect of building becomes clear to the questioner, and what he built upon is accepted by him, then he is mistaken in demanding him to correct it and then build the branch upon it, unless he intends by that to show his inability according to his own principles to correct it, then he becomes like one attacking his principles. But the questioner cannot achieve that except by disputing something that demolishes this principle from his principles, which we are not to abandon disputing in conceding this principle and correcting it.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a detailed procedural exposition by Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwaynī on how farḍ and binā' function in jadal. It begins by advising the questioner to strategically choose a focus where his doubt is strong and the respondent is weak. If the respondent specifies a focus, he can later change it unless he gave the questioner a free choice. If the respondent gives a general answer and later tries to narrow it, he is considered 'cut off' (munqati'), showing failure. When the respondent insists on building upon a different case, the questioner must follow unless the respondent cannot explain the building, in which case the respondent is deemed evasive. Finally, if the questioner accepts the basis but then demands proof for it, he is mistaken unless his aim is to expose the respondent's inability within his own principles.

Key Terms
السائل
المسئول
انقطاع
تعنت

According to al-Juwaynī, what happens if the respondent first gives an open answer and then later specifies a particular side of the issue?
A) He is considered cut off (munqati'), because his open answer implied he could handle all sides.
B) He is allowed to specify because he is the respondent.
C) The questioner cannot change sides after the respondent specifies.
D) The respondent must ask the questioner for permission.

Key Takeaway
Al-Juwaynī's procedural rules ensure that jadal is fair and logical, penalizing inconsistency and obstruction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a mock jadal session, assign one person as questioner and another as respondent on the topic 'Is silence a valid indicator of consent in legal transactions?' Apply al-Juwaynī's rules: the questioner should choose a specific scenario where the doubt is strong; the respondent must answer or build on a precedent. Types of Questions in Jadal: Position and Evidence
Begins enumeration of four types of questions in jadal, giving the first two: question about the position and question about the evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the question about the position (madhhab) and the question about the evidence (dalīl) in a jadal exchange and identify which type is being used in a given dialectical example.

Core Idea
Jadal questions target either the position itself or the evidence for it.

In classical Islamic dialectic (jadal), the structure of questions and answers is carefully classified. This lesson introduces the first two of four question types as outlined by major scholars: asking about the respondent's position (madhhab) and asking for the evidence supporting that position (dalīl). Mastering these types is essential for both constructing effective arguments and responding correctly in theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types framework
Note that several authorities (Abu Ya'la, al-Juwayni, al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, Ibn 'Aqil) agree that questions in jadal are four. This passage gives the first two; the remaining two will follow later.
The entire framework is important for context, even if only two are present here.
2
Recognize the first type: question about the madhhab
The questioner says: 'ما تقول في كذا؟' (What do you say about such and such?). This asks the respondent to state his position or opinion on the issue.
The term 'madhhab' here means the respondent's stance, not a school of thought (though related).
3
Recognize the second type: question about the dalīl
The questioner says: 'ما دليلك عليه؟' (What is your evidence for it?). This asks the respondent to provide the proof or reasoning behind his position.
The dalīl can be from scripture, reason, or consensus, depending on the field.
4
Understand the response structure
Each question type has a corresponding answer from the respondent. For a madhhab question, the respondent states his view; for a dalīl question, he presents the evidence.
The passage explicitly says 'يقابله جواب من جهة المسئول' (it is met by an answer on the part of the respondent).
5
Apply to a real jadal topic
Consider a debate on whether qiyas is a valid source of law. 'ما تقول في القياس؟' is a madhhab question; 'ما دليلك على جواز القياس؟' is a dalīl question. Practise categorizing similar examples.
This skill is crucial for formulating and responding to objections in actual dialectical sessions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثالث : وجوه السؤال والجواب في الجدل ذكر أبو يعلى الفراء وإمام الحرمين والخطيب البغدادي وابن عقيل أن أسئلة الجدل أربعة، قال أبو يعلى: السؤال على أربعة أضرب ، يقابل كل ضرب من الجواب من جهة المسئول . أحدها : السؤال عن المذهب، فيقول السائل : ما تقول في كذا؟ فيقابله جواب من جهة المسئول . والثاني: السؤال عن الدليل بأن يقول : ما دليلك عليه؟ فيقول المسئول : كذا .
The third topic: Aspects of Question and Answer in Jadal. Abu Ya'la al-Farra', Imam al-Haramayn, al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, and Ibn 'Aqil mentioned that the questions of jadal are four. Abu Ya'la said: The question is of four types, each type corresponds to an answer on the part of the respondent. One of them: the question about the position (madhhab), where the questioner says: 'What do you say about such and such?' and it is met by an answer on the part of the respondent. The second: the question about the evidence (dalil), where he says: 'What is your evidence for it?' and the respondent says: 'Such and such.'
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the types of questions and answers in jadal. It cites several authorities (Abu Ya'la, al-Juwaynī, al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, Ibn 'Aqil) who state that there are four types. The passage then gives the first two types as described by Abu Ya'la: (1) the question about the madhhab (position), where the questioner asks 'What do you say about such and such?' and the respondent gives his view; and (2) the question about the dalil (evidence), where the questioner asks 'What is your evidence?' and the respondent provides the proof. The third and fourth types are not included in this window. This is a foundational classification for understanding the structure of dialectical exchange.

السؤال عن الدليل

The Four Types of Questions in Jadal
Author presents the four-type classification of jadal questions with an alternative fivefold view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify jadal questions into four types and match each with its corresponding answer type.

Core Idea
Four question types in jadal: what, why, how, and objection — each demands its own answer.

In any structured debate, knowing what type of question you are asking or answering is crucial. The great Hanbali jurist Abu Ya'la al-Farra' and others classified jadal questions into four distinct types, each with a specific answer. This lesson unpacks that classification and explains why the author prefers a fourfold division over a fivefold one.

Guided Path
1
List the four questions
From Abu Ya'la's enumeration, identify: (1) question about the madhhab, (2) question about the proof, (3) question about the direction of the proof, (4) question by way of objection.
Each question type has a corresponding answer type mentioned later.
2
Understand each question's aim
The first seeks the respondent's position; the second seeks the evidence; the third seeks how the evidence supports the position; the fourth challenges the evidence itself.
3
Examine the alternative fivefold division
Ibn 'Aqil and Ibn al-Najjar add a question about correcting a claim in the proof and a question about entailment, but the author merges the latter into the third type.
This shows scholars sometimes refined the classification.
4
Apply the classification
Given a debate topic like 'Is it permissible to sell dates for dates?', formulate one question for each type following the scheme.
Practice ensures you can recognize question types in real debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثالث : وجوه السؤال والجواب في الجدل
ذكر أبو يعلى الفراء وإمام الحرمين والخطيب البغدادي وابن عقيل أن أسئلة الجدل أربعة، قال أبو يعلى:
السؤال على أربعة أضرب ، يقابل كل ضرب من الجواب من جهة المسئول . أحدها : السؤال عن المذهب، فيقول السائل : ما تقول في كذا؟ فيقابله جواب من جهة المسئول . والثاني: السؤال عن الدليل بأن يقول : ما دليلك عليه؟ فيقول المسئول : كذا .
والثالث : السؤال عن وجه الدليل، فيبينه المسئول .
والرابع : السؤال على سبيل الاعتراض والقدح فيه فيجيب المسئول عنه، ويبين بطلان اعتراضه وصحة ما ذكره من وجه دليله (1) في حين ذكر ابن عقيل في موضع آخر وابن النجار الفتوحي أنها خمسة، قال ابن النجار: اعلم أن سؤال الجدال على خمسة أقسام : سؤال عن المذهب، وسؤال عن الدليل، وسؤال عن وجه الدليل، وسؤال عن تصحيح دعوى في الدليل، وسؤال عن الإلزام، والله أعلم. وتحسين الجواب وتحريره يقوى به العلم والمعمل، فأول ضروب الجواب: الإخبار عن ماهية المذهب ثم الإخبار عن ماهية برهانه، ثم وجه دلالة البرهان عليه، ثم إجراء العلة في المعلول، وحياطته من الزيادة فيه والنقصان منه، لئلا يلحق به ما ليس منه ويخرج عنه ما هو منه والحجة في ترتيب الجواب كالحجة في ترتيب السؤال، لأن كل ضرب من ضروب الجواب مقابل بضرب من ضروب السؤال (2) . ونحن سنجري هنا على الطريقة التي ذكرها أبو يعلى من اعتبار الأسئلة والأجوبة على أربعة أضرب لأن سؤال إجراء العلة في المعلول تابع في حقيقته للسؤال عن وجه دلالة الدليل.
أولا : السؤال عن المذهب والجواب عنه :
وهو ينقسم إلى قسمين :
أحدهما : أن يسأله هل له مذهب في المسألة؟ نحو أن يقال له : هل لك مذهب في جواز المفاضلة في الفواكه والخضر أو منعه؟ والثاني : أن يورد عليه قولين لمن يتقلد المسئول مذهبه ويسأله : هل يختار
The third section: Aspects of question and answer in jadal. Abu Ya'la al-Farra', Imam al-Haramayn, al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, and Ibn 'Aqil mentioned that the questions of jadal are four. Abu Ya'la said: The question is of four types, each type corresponds to an answer from the respondent. First: the question about the madhhab, so the questioner says: What do you say about such-and-such? This is met by an answer from the respondent. Second: the question about the proof, such as saying: What is your proof for it? So the respondent says: Such-and-such. Third: the question about the direction of the proof, so the respondent clarifies it. Fourth: the question by way of objection and criticism against it, so the respondent answers it and shows the invalidity of his objection and the correctness of what he mentioned concerning the direction of his proof (1). Meanwhile Ibn 'Aqil in another place and Ibn al-Najjar al-Futuhi mentioned that they are five. Ibn al-Najjar said: Know that the question of argumentation is of five types: a question about the madhhab, a question about the proof, a question about the direction of the proof, a question about the correction of a claim in the proof, and a question about entailment. And Allah knows best. Improving and refining the answer strengthens knowledge and practice. So the first type of answer is: informing about the essence of the madhhab, then informing about the essence of its demonstration, then the direction of the demonstration's indication upon it, then applying the cause to the effect, and protecting it from increase and decrease, lest what is not from it be joined to it and what is from it be excluded from it. And the proof in the order of the answer is like the proof in the order of the question, because each type of answer corresponds to a type of question (2). And we will proceed here according to the method that Abu Ya'la mentioned, considering the questions and answers as four types, because the question of applying the cause to the effect is in its reality dependent on the question about the direction of the proof's indication. First: the question about the madhhab and its answer. It is divided into two parts: One of them: that he asks him: Does he have a position in the issue? Such as it being said to him: Do you have a position on the permissibility of preferring among fruits and vegetables or its prohibition? And the second: that he presents to him two opinions of someone whose madhhab the respondent follows and asks him: Does he choose...
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of questions and answers in jadal. The author first reports the views of earlier scholars: Abu Ya'la, al-Juwayni, al-Khatib, and Ibn 'Aqil agree that there are four types of questions. Each question has a corresponding answer from the respondent. The four types are: (1) question about the madhhab (the position), (2) question about the proof, (3) question about the direction of the proof (how the proof indicates), and (4) question by way of objection and criticism. The author then notes that Ibn 'Aqil (in another place) and Ibn al-Najjar count five types, adding a question about correcting a claim in the proof and a question about entailment. The author then mentions that the order of answers follows the order of questions: first informing about the madhhab, then the demonstration, then its direction, then applying the cause to the effect, while safeguarding against improper inclusion or exclusion. The author chooses to follow Abu Ya'la's fourfold division, arguing that the fifth type (applying the cause) is really a part of the third type (direction of the proof). Then the author begins explaining the first type: the question about the madhhab. This type is divided into two: (i) asking whether the respondent has a position on the issue at all, and (ii) presenting two opinions from the respondent's own school and asking which he chooses. The passage ends while beginning to describe the second part. The key insight is that the structure of jadal is highly organized; the types of questions define the types of responses, and awareness of this classification prevents disorderly debate.

Key Terms
السؤال عن المذهب
السؤال عن الدليل
السؤال عن وجه الدليل
السؤال على سبيل الاعتراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Ya'la, which type of question is answered by the respondent clarifying how the proof indicates the claim?
A) Question about the madhhab
B) Question about the proof
C) Question about the direction of the proof
D) Question by way of objection

Key Takeaway
The four question types form a complete sequence for testing a position in jadal: position, evidence, inference, and critique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a debate transcript, identify each question type according to Abu Ya'la's scheme. For example, if the questioner says 'State your position on the permissibility of gambling', that is a 'question about the madhhab'.
?
Construct a brief debate exchange on the topic 'Is the Quran created or uncreated?' where each of the four question types appears in order.

Question about the Madhhab: Two Sub-Types
Author details the question about the madhhab and its two divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two sub-types of the question about the madhhab and formulate appropriate responses.

Core Idea
Questioning the madhhab comes in two forms: asking if one has a position, and asking which of two positions one chooses.

The first and most fundamental question in a jadal exchange is about the respondent's own position (madhhab). Abu Ya'la divides this into two: asking whether the respondent has any position on the issue, and presenting two opinions from within his school and asking which he prefers. This lesson examines both sub-types with concrete examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first sub-type
The direct question: 'Do you have a position on X?' The passage gives the example: 'Do you have a position on the permissibility of preferring among fruits or its prohibition?'
This establishes whether the respondent has a view at all.
2
Identify the second sub-type
Presenting two opinions from the respondent's school and asking which he chooses. The passage mentions 'he presents to him two opinions of someone whose madhhab the respondent follows and asks him: Does he choose...'.
This tests the respondent's specific alignment within his own tradition.
3
Formulate responses
For the first sub-type, the answer is simply 'Yes, I hold that...' or 'No, I have no position.' For the second, the respondent must select one opinion and possibly justify why.
The response must be clear to allow the debate to proceed to proof.
4
Practice the distinction
Given a scenario like 'Is it permissible to combine prayers during travel?', decide which sub-type of question is being used in each case: 'Do you have a ruling on this?' (first sub-type) vs. 'Ahmad ibn Hanbal has two narrations: one allowing, one forbidding; which do you choose?' (second sub-type).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثالث : وجوه السؤال والجواب في الجدل
ذكر أبو يعلى الفراء وإمام الحرمين والخطيب البغدادي وابن عقيل أن أسئلة الجدل أربعة، قال أبو يعلى:
السؤال على أربعة أضرب ، يقابل كل ضرب من الجواب من جهة المسئول . أحدها : السؤال عن المذهب، فيقول السائل : ما تقول في كذا؟ فيقابله جواب من جهة المسئول . والثاني: السؤال عن الدليل بأن يقول : ما دليلك عليه؟ فيقول المسئول : كذا .
والثالث : السؤال عن وجه الدليل، فيبينه المسئول .
والرابع : السؤال على سبيل الاعتراض والقدح فيه فيجيب المسئول عنه، ويبين بطلان اعتراضه وصحة ما ذكره من وجه دليله (1) في حين ذكر ابن عقيل في موضع آخر وابن النجار الفتوحي أنها خمسة، قال ابن النجار: اعلم أن سؤال الجدال على خمسة أقسام : سؤال عن المذهب، وسؤال عن الدليل، وسؤال عن وجه الدليل، وسؤال عن تصحيح دعوى في الدليل، وسؤال عن الإلزام، والله أعلم. وتحسين الجواب وتحريره يقوى به العلم والمعمل، فأول ضروب الجواب: الإخبار عن ماهية المذهب ثم الإخبار عن ماهية برهانه، ثم وجه دلالة البرهان عليه، ثم إجراء العلة في المعلول، وحياطته من الزيادة فيه والنقصان منه، لئلا يلحق به ما ليس منه ويخرج عنه ما هو منه والحجة في ترتيب الجواب كالحجة في ترتيب السؤال، لأن كل ضرب من ضروب الجواب مقابل بضرب من ضروب السؤال (2) . ونحن سنجري هنا على الطريقة التي ذكرها أبو يعلى من اعتبار الأسئلة والأجوبة على أربعة أضرب لأن سؤال إجراء العلة في المعلول تابع في حقيقته للسؤال عن وجه دلالة الدليل.
أولا : السؤال عن المذهب والجواب عنه :
وهو ينقسم إلى قسمين :
أحدهما : أن يسأله هل له مذهب في المسألة؟ نحو أن يقال له : هل لك مذهب في جواز المفاضلة في الفواكه والخضر أو منعه؟ والثاني : أن يورد عليه قولين لمن يتقلد المسئول مذهبه ويسأله : هل يختار
The third section: Aspects of question and answer in jadal. Abu Ya'la al-Farra', Imam al-Haramayn, al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, and Ibn 'Aqil mentioned that the questions of jadal are four. Abu Ya'la said: The question is of four types, each type corresponds to an answer from the respondent. First: the question about the madhhab, so the questioner says: What do you say about such-and-such? This is met by an answer from the respondent. Second: the question about the proof, such as saying: What is your proof for it? So the respondent says: Such-and-such. Third: the question about the direction of the proof, so the respondent clarifies it. Fourth: the question by way of objection and criticism against it, so the respondent answers it and shows the invalidity of his objection and the correctness of what he mentioned concerning the direction of his proof (1). Meanwhile Ibn 'Aqil in another place and Ibn al-Najjar al-Futuhi mentioned that they are five. Ibn al-Najjar said: Know that the question of argumentation is of five types: a question about the madhhab, a question about the proof, a question about the direction of the proof, a question about the correction of a claim in the proof, and a question about entailment. And Allah knows best. Improving and refining the answer strengthens knowledge and practice. So the first type of answer is: informing about the essence of the madhhab, then informing about the essence of its demonstration, then the direction of the demonstration's indication upon it, then applying the cause to the effect, and protecting it from increase and decrease, lest what is not from it be joined to it and what is from it be excluded from it. And the proof in the order of the answer is like the proof in the order of the question, because each type of answer corresponds to a type of question (2). And we will proceed here according to the method that Abu Ya'la mentioned, considering the questions and answers as four types, because the question of applying the cause to the effect is in its reality dependent on the question about the direction of the proof's indication. First: the question about the madhhab and its answer. It is divided into two parts: One of them: that he asks him: Does he have a position in the issue? Such as it being said to him: Do you have a position on the permissibility of preferring among fruits and vegetables or its prohibition? And the second: that he presents to him two opinions of someone whose madhhab the respondent follows and asks him: Does he choose...
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of questions and answers in jadal. The author first reports the views of earlier scholars: Abu Ya'la, al-Juwayni, al-Khatib, and Ibn 'Aqil agree that there are four types of questions. Each question has a corresponding answer from the respondent. The four types are: (1) question about the madhhab (the position), (2) question about the proof, (3) question about the direction of the proof (how the proof indicates), and (4) question by way of objection and criticism. The author then notes that Ibn 'Aqil (in another place) and Ibn al-Najjar count five types, adding a question about correcting a claim in the proof and a question about entailment. The author then mentions that the order of answers follows the order of questions: first informing about the madhhab, then the demonstration, then its direction, then applying the cause to the effect, while safeguarding against improper inclusion or exclusion. The author chooses to follow Abu Ya'la's fourfold division, arguing that the fifth type (applying the cause) is really a part of the third type (direction of the proof). Then the author begins explaining the first type: the question about the madhhab. This type is divided into two: (i) asking whether the respondent has a position on the issue at all, and (ii) presenting two opinions from the respondent's own school and asking which he chooses. The passage ends while beginning to describe the second part. The key insight is that the structure of jadal is highly organized; the types of questions define the types of responses, and awareness of this classification prevents disorderly debate.

Key Terms
السؤال عن المذهب
إجراء العلة في المعلول

The Four Types of Dialectical Questions
Author lists four types of questions in jadal and justifies following Abu Ya'la against five-fold classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of questions in jadal and explain why the proposed fifth type is subsumed under the fourth.

Core Idea
Jadal questions are four; the fifth is not a separate category.

This passage introduces the key classification of questions in dialectical debate (jadal). Understanding this classification is crucial for structuring valid objections and responses in Islamic theological and legal disputations. The author lists four fundamental questions and then addresses a rival five-type scheme, justifying his adherence to the simpler four-type model of Abu Ya'la.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four question types
Locate the four types enumerated by the author: (1) question about the doctrine (madhhab), (2) question about the proof (dalil), (3) question about the aspect of the proof (wajh al-dalil), (4) question by way of objection and fault-finding (i'tirad wa qadḥ).
2
Note the rival five-type classification
The author mentions that Ibn 'Aqil and Ibn al-Najjar count five types, adding a question about validating a claim in the proof and a question about imposition (ilzam). Understand what each additional type entails.
The fifth type corresponds to the answer's step of 'applying the cause to the effect'.
3
Find the author's justification for adopting four
The author argues that the question about applying the cause (ijra' al-illa fi al-ma'lul) is in reality subordinate to the question about the aspect of the proof's implication. Therefore, it does not constitute a separate category.
This reduces the classification to four fundamental question-answer pairs.
4
Understand the corresponding answers
Each question type has a corresponding answer mode: for the madhhab, inform about its essence; for the dalil, inform about its demonstration; for wajh al-dalil, show the aspect of implication; for i'tirad, respond to the objection and safeguard the argument from excess or deficiency.
The correspondence ensures systematic and rigorous debate.

والثالث: السؤال عن وجه الدليل، فيبينه المسئول. والرابع: السؤال على سبيل الاعتراض والقدح فيه فيجيب المسئول عنه، ويبين بطلان اعتراضه وصحة ما ذكره من وجه دليله. في حين ذكر ابن عقيل في موضع آخر وابن النجار الفتوحي أنها خمسة، قال ابن النجار: اعلم أن سؤال الجدال على خمسة أقسام: سؤال عن المذهب، وسؤال عن الدليل، وسؤال عن وجه الدليل، وسؤال عن تصحيح دعوى في الدليل، وسؤال عن الإلزام، والله أعلم. وتحسين الجواب وتحريره يقوى به العلم والمعمل، فأول ضروب الجواب: الإخبار عن ماهية المذهب ثم الإخبار عن ماهية برهانه، ثم وجه دلالة البرهان عليه، ثم إجراء العلة في المعلول، وحياطته من الزيادة فيه والنقصان منه، لئلا يلحق به ما ليس منه ويخرج عنه ما هو منه والحجة في ترتيب الجواب كالحجة في ترتيب السؤال، لأن كل ضرب من ضروب الجواب مقابل بضرب من ضروب السؤال. ونحن سنجري هنا على الطريقة التي ذكرها أبو يعلى من اعتبار الأسئلة والأجوبة على أربعة أضرب لأن سؤال إجراء العلة في المعلول تابع في حقيقته للسؤال عن وجه دلالة الدليل.
The third: the question about the aspect of the proof, so the respondent clarifies it. The fourth: the question by way of objection and fault-finding in it, so the respondent answers it and shows the invalidity of his objection and the correctness of what he mentioned in terms of the aspect of his proof. Meanwhile, Ibn ‘Aqil in another place and Ibn al-Najjar al-Futuhi mentioned that they are five. Ibn al-Najjar said: Know that the dialectical questions are of five divisions: question about the doctrine, question about the proof, question about the aspect of the proof, question about the validation of a claim in the proof, and question about imposition, and God knows best. The refinement and proper formulation of the answer strengthen knowledge and practice. The first mode of answer: informing about the essence of the doctrine, then informing about the essence of its demonstration, then the aspect of the demonstration's implication upon it, then applying the cause to the effect, and safeguarding it from excess and deficiency, lest what is not part of it be attached to it and what is part of it be removed from it. And the rationale for ordering the answer is the same as the rationale for ordering the question, because each mode of answer corresponds to a mode of question. And we will proceed here according to the method mentioned by Abu Ya‘la of considering the questions and answers as four types, because the question of applying the cause to the effect is in reality subordinate to the question about the aspect of the proof's implication.

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of questions in dialectic (jadal). The author first enumerates four types: (1) question about the doctrine (madhhab), (2) question about the proof (dalil), (3) question about the aspect of the proof (wajh al-dalil), and (4) question by way of objection and fault-finding (i‘tiraḍ wa qadḥ). However, he notes that some scholars like Ibn ‘Aqil and Ibn al-Najjar count five types, adding a question about the validation of a claim in the proof and a question about imposition (ilzam). The author then explains the corresponding order of answers: first, informing about the essence of the madhhab; second, about the essence of its demonstration (burhan); third, about the aspect of implication; and fourth, applying the cause to the effect and safeguarding it from excess or deficiency. He justifies following Abu Ya‘la’s four-part scheme by stating that the question about applying the cause is actually part of the question about the aspect of implication. Thus, the classification is streamlined into four fundamental pairs of question and answer.

The Question About the Doctrine and Its Answer
Author details the first type of dialectical question and its answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two subtypes of the question about the doctrine (madhhab) and evaluate whether a respondent's answer follows the rules of jadal.

Core Idea
If your position has details, never give an unconditional answer.

In this passage, the author explains the first type of question in a dialectical exchange: the question about the doctrine. This foundational rule governs how a scholar must respond when asked about his position on an issue, especially when his school includes multiple opinions or conditional details. The examples from Hanbali and Shafi'i fiqh show how to avoid mistakes that could derail a debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subtypes of the question about the doctrine
The author divides the question into two: (1) asking whether the respondent has a doctrine on the issue, e.g., 'Do you have a doctrine on the permissibility of differentiating between fruits and vegetables?' (2) presenting two opinions from the school and asking which the respondent chooses, e.g., 'Malik has two narrations on wiping over khuffs while resident; do you choose one or are the proofs equal?'
The second subtype assumes the respondent follows a specific madhhab.
2
Determine the questioner's obligation
The questioner must clarify his question so the respondent understands exactly what is being asked. This prevents ambiguity and allows a proper answer.
Without clarity, the respondent cannot give a correct response.
3
Analyze the respondent's possible responses
Once the question is clear, the respondent must answer. If he has a settled doctrine, he says 'yes' or states his choice. If he has not yet settled, he may defer until he decides. If he chooses neither of the presented opinions, he may say 'I choose something else' and specify it.
The respondent has the right to choose a third opinion if it exists.
4
Recognize the danger of absolute answers when details exist
When asked a general question (e.g., 'What do you say about such-and-such?'), the respondent must check if his position is unconditional or detailed. If his position has details (e.g., touching women invalidates wudu only if with desire), he must either give the detailed answer or ask 'About which do you ask?' Giving an unconditional answer is a mistake.
The examples of the Hanbali (touching women) and Shafi'i (tanning hide) illustrate this rule.
5
Apply the rule to new scenarios
Imagine a Hanafi scholar asked about a contract where the school has conditions. If the question is general, the scholar must decide whether to detail or request specification.
This skill prevents miscommunication in juristic debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولا: السؤال عن المذهب والجواب عنه: وهو ينقسم إلى قسمين: أحدهما: أن يسأله هل له مذهب في المسألة؟ نحو أن يقال له: هل لك مذهب في جواز المفاضلة في الفواكه والخضر أو منعه؟ والثاني: أن يورد عليه قولين لمن يتقلد المسئول مذهبه ويسأله: هل يختار أحدهما؟ نحو أن يقول: لمالك رحمه الله روايتان في المسح على الخفين في الحضر، فهل تختار أحدهما أو يتساوى الدليلان عندك فلا تختار أحدهما؟ ويجب على السائل أن يبين سؤاله ليفهم المسئول مراده ويمكنه جوابه، فإذا بين السؤال وجب على المسئول الجواب، ثم ينظر فإن كان له في المسألة مذهب قال: «نعم»، وإن لم يتقرر له فيها مذهب آخر إلى أن ينظر ويتقرر مذهبه، وإن اختار أحد القولين أجاب به أيضًا وإن اختار غيرهما، كان مخيرًا بين يقول: الذي أختار غيرهما ويبين ما يختاره من غيرهما. وقد يأتي السؤال عن المذهب بطريقة أخرى، فيقول السائل: ما تقول في كذا؟ فيقابله جواب من جهة المسئول، فيقول: هكذا. فإذا سأل السائل عن حكم مطلق نظر المسئول فيما سأله، فإن كان مذهبه موافقا لما سأل عنه من غير تفصيل فيه أطلق الجواب عنه، وإن كان عنده فيه تفصيل كان بالخيار بين أن يفصله في جوابه، وبين أن يقول للسائل: هذا مختلف عندي فمنه كذا، ومنه كذا، فعن أيهما تسأل؟ فإذا ذكر أحدهما أجاب عنه وإن أطلق الجواب كان مخطئًا. مثاله: أن يسأل حنبلي عن مس النساء، هل ينقض الوضوء؟ وعنده إن كان لشهوة نقض وإن كان لغير شهوة لم ينقض، فيقول للسائل هذا التفصيل. وإن شاء قال: منه ما ينقض ومنه ما لا ينقض، فعن أيهما تسأل؟ ومثل أن يسأل شافعي عن جلد الميتة: هل يطهر بالدباغ؟ وعند المسئول أن جلد الكلب والخنزير لا يطهر، وكذلك ما تولد منهما أو من أحدهما، ويطهر ما عدا ذلك.. فيقول للسائل هذا التفصيل. وإن شاء قال له: منه ما يطهر بالدباغ ومنه ما لا يطهر، فعن أيهما تسأل؟ فأما إذا أطلق الجواب وقال: يطهر بالدباغ، كان مخطئًا.
First: the question about the doctrine and its answer. It is divided into two parts: One: that he asks him whether he has a doctrine regarding the issue; for example, it is said to him: Do you have a doctrine regarding the permissibility of making distinction (mufadala) among fruits and vegetables or its prohibition? The second: that he presents two opinions of the doctrine that the respondent follows and asks him: Do you choose one of them? For example, he says: Malik, may God have mercy on him, has two narrations concerning wiping over the khuffs in the homeland, so do you choose one of them, or are the two proofs equivalent for you such that you do not choose one of them? And it is obligatory upon the questioner to clarify his question so that the respondent understands his intent and can answer. When the question is clarified, it becomes obligatory upon the respondent to answer. Then he considers: if he has a doctrine on the issue, he says 'Yes'; if he has not yet settled on a doctrine regarding it, he says 'I will consider until my doctrine is settled.' And if he chooses one of the two opinions, he answers accordingly. And if he chooses something else, he has the choice between saying 'What I choose is something else' and specifying what he chooses from other than them. The question about the doctrine may also come in another manner: the questioner says 'What do you say about such-and-such?' and it is met by an answer from the respondent, who says 'Thus.' So if the questioner asks about an absolute ruling, the respondent looks into what he asked. If his doctrine agrees with what he asked about without any detail in it, he gives an absolute answer. If he has a detailed position, he has the choice between detailing it in his answer and saying to the questioner: 'This is different with me: some of it is thus, some of it is thus, so about which do you ask?' When the questioner specifies one, he answers accordingly. And if he gives an absolute answer, he is mistaken. Example: someone asks a Hanbali about touching women: does it invalidate wudu? The respondent holds that if it is with desire it invalidates, and if not with desire it does not invalidate. So he tells the questioner this detail. Or if he wishes, he says: 'Some of it invalidates and some of it does not, so about which do you ask?' Another example: someone asks a Shafi'i about the hide of a dead animal: is it purified by tanning? The respondent holds that the hide of a dog and a pig is not purified, and likewise what is produced from them or from one of them, and that what is other than that is purified. So he tells the questioner this detail. Or if he wishes, he says: 'Some of it is purified by tanning and some of it is not purified, so about which do you ask?' But if he gives an absolute answer and says 'It is purified by tanning,' he is mistaken.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage explains the first type of question in jadal, which is the question about the doctrine (madhhab). The author divides it into two subtypes: (1) asking whether the respondent has a doctrine on a specific issue (e.g., whether he holds an opinion on the permissibility of giving preference among fruits and vegetables); (2) presenting two conflicting opinions from a school (e.g., Imam Malik's two narrations on wiping over khuffs during residence) and asking the respondent to choose one or state that both proofs are equal. The author then lays down procedural obligations: the questioner must clarify his question so that the respondent understands; once clarified, the respondent must answer. If the respondent has a settled doctrine, he says 'yes'; if not, he may defer until he settles it. If he chooses one of the opinions, he states his choice; if he chooses something else, he may say 'I choose something else' and specify it. Another method of questioning is when the questioner asks an absolute unconditional question (e.g., 'What do you say about such-and-such?'), the respondent should reply accordingly. But if the respondent has a nuanced position (with details), he must either detail his answer or ask the questioner to specify which branch he intends. If he gives an unconditional answer despite having a detailed position, he is mistaken. Two concrete fiqhi examples illustrate this: (a) a Hanbali being asked about touching women and wudu - if he has a detailed position based on desire, he must not simply say 'it invalidates' but must specify; (b) a Shafi'i being asked about tanning the hide of a dead animal - different animals have different rulings, so he must give a qualified answer. This ensures precision and avoids miscommunication in dialectical exchanges.

Key Terms
سؤال عن المذهب
تفصيل
إطلاق الجواب

Rules for Questioning About the Madhhab and Answering
Author explains how a questioner asks about a jurist's school of thought and how to answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the correct procedure when a questioner asks about a jurist's madhhab, distinguishing between cases where the madhhab has multiple narrations and cases where it has an absolute ruling.

Core Idea
Questioning about a madhhab requires clarity: specify whether you want the chosen opinion or the absolute ruling.

In jadal, the first step is often to establish the respondent's legal position (madhhab) on the issue. This lesson covers the two main forms of such questioning: when the madhhab has conflicting reports, and when it has a ruling that may be absolute or detailed. Understanding these protocols ensures that the subsequent discussion of evidence is based on a correct understanding of the respondent's stance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of question
Determine if the questioner is asking about a specific ikhtilaf within the respondent's madhhab (e.g., Malik's two narrations on wiping over khuffs) or asking for an absolute ruling on a general issue (e.g., 'What do you say about touching women?').
2
Check the respondent's obligation
Once the question is clarified, the respondent must answer. If he has a settled position, he says 'Yes'; if not, he may delay to consider.
3
Handle the absolute question with detail
If the madhhab has a detailed ruling (e.g., touching women: with desire vs without), the respondent must either provide the detail or ask the questioner to specify which part he is asking about.
Giving an absolute answer when detail is required is an error.
4
Avoid error: never give an absolute answer when detail is required
The author gives examples: a Hanbali asked about touching women, and a Shafi'i asked about tanning dead animal skin. In both, an absolute answer is incorrect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما؟ نحو أن يقول : لمالك رحمه الله روايتان في المسح على الخفين في الحضر، فهل تختار أحدهما أو يتساوى الدليلان عندك فلا تختار أحدهما؟ ويجب على السائل أن يبين سؤاله ليفهم المسئول مراده ويمكنه جوابه، فإذا بين السؤال وجب على المسئول الجواب، ثم ينظر فإن كان له في المسألة مذهب قال : «نعم»، وإن لم يتقرر له فيها مذهب آخر إلى أن ينظر ويتقرر مذهبه، وإن اختار أحد القولين أجاب به أيضًا وإن اختار غيرهما، كان مخيرًا بين يقول : الذي أختار ،غيرهما ويبين ما يختاره من غيرهما (1) . وقد يأتي السؤال عن المذهب بطريقة أخرى، فيقول السائل : ما تقول في كذا؟ فيقابله جواب من جهة المسئول، فيقول: هكذا. فإذا سأل السائل عن حكم مطلق نظر المسئول فيما سأله، فإن كان مذهبه موافقا لما سأل عنه من غير تفصيل فيه أطلق الجواب عنه، وإن كان عنده فيه تفصيل كان بالخيار بين أن يفصله في جوابه، وبين أن يقول للسائل : هذا مختلف عندي فمنه كذا، ومنه كذا، فعن أيهما تسأل؟ فإذا ذكر أحدهما أجاب عنه وان أطلق الجواب كان مخطئًا . مثاله : أن يسأل حنبلي عن مس النساء، هل ينقض الوضوء؟ وعنده إن كان لشهوة نقض وإن كان لغير شهوة لم ينقض، فيقول للسائل هذا التفصيل . وإن شاء قال : منه ما ينقض ومنه ما لا ينقض، فعن أيهما تسأل؟ ومثل أن يسأل شافعي عن جلد الميتة : هل يطهر بالدباغ؟ وعند المسئول أن جلد الكلب والخنزير لا يطهر، وكذلك ما تولد منهما أو من أحدهما، ويطهر ما عدا ذلك .. فيقول للسائل هذا التفصيل . وإن شاء قال له : منه ما يطهر بالدباغ ومنه ما لا يطهر، فعن أيهما تسأل؟ فأما إذا أطلق الجواب وقال : يطهر بالدباغ، كان مخطئًا (2). وتجدر الإشارة هنا إلى أنه إذا اجتمع اثنان قد عرف كل واحد منهما مذهب صاحبه، وهما على خلاف في المذهب، أغناهما علمهما عن ضرب من ضروب السؤال، وهو السؤال عن ماهية المذهب» (1) . ولهذا قال جمهور أهل النظر : إن السائل إذا كان عالما بمذهب المسئول، وكان مذهبه مشهورًا عنده، لا شك فيه كان له أن يبتدئ بالسؤال عن الدلالة» (2) إلا أن ابن عقيل منع ذلك لجواز أن يكون المسئول غير مذهبه، وذلك يستلزم أن يكون قد تغير دليله أيضًا، قال : قلت : وقد يجري في حكم الاجتهاد أن لا يسقط السؤال، لجواز تغير يطرأ على المذهب الذي عرف به فيزول ما عرفه، وذلك لعدم الثقة بالبقاء على المذهب، فلا غنى إذا عن السؤال (3) .
One of them? For example, he says: 'Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) has two narrations regarding wiping over the khuffs while residing. Do you choose one of them, or are the two proofs equal in your view so you do not choose one?' It is obligatory for the questioner to clarify his question so that the respondent understands his intent and can answer. When the question is clarified, the respondent must answer. Then he considers: if he has a position on the issue, he says 'Yes'; if he has not yet settled on a position, he may delay until he considers and settles his position. If he chooses one of the two opinions, he answers accordingly; if he chooses something else, he is free to say 'What I choose is other than them' and state what he chooses from others. (1) The question about the madhhab may come in another way: the questioner says 'What do you say about such-and-such?' and the respondent answers accordingly. So if the questioner asks about an absolute ruling, the respondent looks at what he was asked. If his madhhab agrees with what was asked without any detail, he gives an absolute answer. If he has a detail regarding it, he has the choice between detailing it in his answer and saying to the questioner 'This is different with me: some of it is such, some is such; which of them do you ask about?' When the questioner mentions one of them, he answers it; if he gives an absolute answer, he is mistaken. Example: A Hanbali is asked about touching women: does it invalidate ablution? If, in his view, if it is with desire it invalidates, and if without desire it does not, he tells this detail to the questioner. Or if he wishes, he says 'Some of it invalidates and some does not; which of them do you ask about?' Another example: A Shafi'i is asked about the skin of a dead animal: is it purified by tanning? If the respondent holds that the skin of a dog and a pig is not purified, and likewise what is born from them or from one of them, and other skins are purified, he tells this detail to the questioner. Or if he wishes, he says 'Some of it is purified by tanning and some is not; which of them do you ask about?' If he gives an absolute answer and says 'It is purified by tanning', he is mistaken. (2) It should be noted here that when two people meet, each knowing the other's madhhab, and they disagree on the madhhab, their knowledge makes them dispense with one kind of question—the question about the essence of the madhhab. (1) Hence the majority of theorists said: if the questioner knows the respondent's madhhab and it is well-known to him without doubt, he may start by asking about the evidence. (2) However, Ibn 'Aqeel forbade this because it is possible that the respondent has changed his madhhab, which would entail that his evidence has also changed. He said: 'I say: In the ruling of ijtihad, the question may not be dropped, because a change may occur in the madhhab known about him, thus removing what was known, due to lack of confidence in remaining on the madhhab. Therefore, the question is indispensable.' (3)
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition on the etiquette of questioning and answering about legal positions (madhhab) in juristic debate. The author begins by illustrating a common scenario: a questioner asks an opponent who follows Imam Malik about a well-known ikhtilaf (disagreement) regarding wiping over khuffs while not traveling. The questioner must clarify his question so the respondent can understand and answer. The respondent, upon understanding, is obliged to answer. He may either give his chosen position if he has one, or if he has not settled, he may delay. The author then discusses a second method of questioning: asking about an absolute ruling. The respondent must check if his madhhab has an unconditional ruling or if it is detailed. If there is detail, he must either provide the detail or ask the questioner to specify which part he is asking about. Giving an absolute answer when detail is required is an error. The author provides examples from Hanbali fiqh (touching women and ablution) and Shafi'i fiqh (purification of dead animal skin by tanning). The passage then addresses a special case: when both debaters already know each other's madhhab, the majority of scholars (jumhur ahl al-nazar) say that the questioner may skip asking about the madhhab and go directly to asking about the evidence (dalil). However, Ibn 'Aqeel objects: even if the madhhab is known, it might have changed due to ijtihad, so the question about the madhhab is still necessary to avoid basing arguments on outdated positions.

Key Terms
السؤال عن المذهب
التفصيل
الإطلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a Shafi'i is asked about the skin of a dead animal and his madhhab says that the skin of dogs and pigs is not purified by tanning while others are, what is the correct response?
A) Say 'It is purified by tanning' without qualification.
B) Provide the detail, or ask the questioner to specify which animal he means.
C) Refuse to answer because the question is ambiguous.
D) Give an absolute answer and then correct himself later.

Key Takeaway
The correct response when asked about a madhhab depends on whether the madhhab has a single ruling or a detailed one; never give an absolute answer when detail is needed.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate

The Necessity of Asking About the Madhhab Even When the Opponent's Position is Known
Author explains how a questioner asks about a jurist's school of thought and how to answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn 'Aqeel's objection to the majority view that knowledge of the opponent's madhhab dispenses with asking, and evaluate the soundness of his reasoning.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a madhhab is not sufficient; the respondent may have changed his position, so the question remains necessary.

This lesson examines a subtle point in jadal etiquette: when two debaters already know each other's madhhab, do they still need to ask about it? The majority of theorists say no, but Ibn 'Aqeel argues yes, because a jurist may change his opinion through new ijtihad. The passage presents both views and Ibn 'Aqeel's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the majority position
The majority of theorists say that if the questioner knows the respondent's madhhab well, he may skip asking about it and go directly to the evidence.
2
Understand Ibn 'Aqeel's objection
He argues that it is possible the respondent has changed his madhhab, which would entail a change in his evidence as well. Therefore, asking about the madhhab is still necessary to avoid arguing based on an outdated position.
3
Analyse the basis of Ibn 'Aqeel's objection
His premise is that ijtihad can lead to changes in positions, and one cannot be certain of another's current stance. This is an epistemic concern.
4
Evaluate the debate
Consider which position is more cautious: the majority rely on common practice and trust that positions are stable, while Ibn 'Aqeel emphasises the possibility of change and the need for verification.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Rules for Questioning About the Evidence and Answering
Procedures for requesting evidence in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly apply the jadal procedure for requesting evidence and choose the appropriate method of presenting evidence based on the opponent's madhhab.

Core Idea
In jadal, the respondent may either prove his own view or refute the opponent's — whichever is more strategic.

After a disputant states his position (madhhab), the questioner asks for the proof. The respondent must then provide evidence. But the respondent has strategic choices: he can tailor his evidence based on whether he knows the questioner's school, and he may either validate his own position or invalidate the opponent's. This passage outlines the first of three methods of presenting evidence, with a concrete example from Maliki fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stage of debate
The passage assumes the questioner has already asked about the madhhab and received an answer. Now he asks for evidence: 'What is the evidence for that?'
2
Determine the respondent's knowledge of the opponent's madhhab
If known, the respondent may choose to prove his own view correct or disprove the opponent's view. If unknown, he must first ask the questioner about his madhhab so that evidence can be tailored.
3
Select the method of presenting evidence
The first method is to present evidence directly on the issue. Within this, the respondent may give evidence specific to his own view (e.g., Maliki analogy) or evidence that attacks the opponent's view.
Two other methods are mentioned but not detailed in this passage.
4
Analyze the Maliki example
For the obligation of intention in purification, the Maliki uses qiyas: 'This is a purification that extends beyond the place of its cause, so it requires intention, like tayammum.' This is evidence for his own view.
The common property ('illah) is that both purifications extend beyond the immediate cause of impurity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيا : السؤال عن الدليل والجواب عنه : والسؤال عن الدليل أن يقول السائل للمسئول بعد الجواب عن المذهب : ما الدليل على ذلك ؟ فيوجه على المسئول إقامة الدليل .. ولا يخلو إما أن يعرف مذهب السائل أو لا يعرف ؛ فإن عرف مذهبه دل عليه ثم هو بالخيار إن شاء دل على صحة قوله وإن شاء دل على فساد قول خصمه، وأيهما فعل من ذلك جاز . وإن لم يعرف مذهبه وفي المسألة أقوال مختلفة ومذاهب شتى، سأل السائل عن مذهبه ليكون الدليل على حسبه، فإذا عرف مذهبه دل عليه على ما تقدم وللمسئول في الدلالة ثلاث طرق : أ - أن يدل على المسألة بعينها فإن اختار هذا الطريق، حسب ما سئل، جاز بلا إشكال، ثم هو بالخيار ؛ إن شاء دل عليه بدليل يخص المسئول عنه، وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على وجوب النية في الطهارة أن هذه طهارة تتعدى محل موجبها، فافتقرت إلى النية كالتيمم .. وإن شاء دل على مسألة الخلاف بدليل (1) .
Second: Questioning about the evidence and answering it. Questioning about the evidence is that the questioner says to the respondent after the answer about the madhhab: 'What is the evidence for that?' Then the respondent is obliged to present evidence. And it is either that he knows the questioner's madhhab or he does not know. If he knows his madhhab, he presents it; then he has the choice: if he wishes, he presents evidence for the correctness of his own statement, and if he wishes, he presents evidence for the invalidity of his opponent's statement; whichever he does is permissible. And if he does not know his madhhab, and on the issue there are different opinions and various schools, he asks the questioner about his madhhab so that the evidence is according to him. When he knows his madhhab, he presents it as above. And for the respondent in presenting evidence, there are three methods: (A) He presents evidence on the very issue. If he chooses this method, as asked, it is permissible without any problem. Then he has the choice: if he wishes, he presents evidence that is specific to the respondent, such as a Maliki arguing for the obligation of intention in purification: 'This is a purification that extends beyond the place of its cause, so it requires intention, like tayammum.' And if he wishes, he presents evidence on the issue of dispute with evidence (1).
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition on the procedure of asking for evidence (dalil) after the madhhab has been established. The author begins by defining the question about evidence: after the respondent states his position, the questioner asks for the proof. The respondent must then provide evidence. The author distinguishes two scenarios: (1) If the respondent knows the questioner's madhhab, he may choose to either prove his own position correct or prove the opponent's position false. Both are permissible. (2) If he does not know the questioner's madhhab and there are multiple opinions, he must first ask the questioner about his madhhab so that the evidence can be tailored accordingly. Then the author introduces three methods of presenting evidence (though only the first is described in this passage). The first method (A) is to present evidence directly on the issue at hand. Within this method, the respondent has two options: give evidence specific to his own view (the author gives an example from Maliki fiqh: the obligation of intention in purification, analogizing it to tayammum because both are purifications that extend beyond the immediate cause) or give evidence for the opposing view (which is only mentioned; no example is given here). The passage ends mid-sentence, presumably to be continued with the other two methods (B and C) in subsequent material.

Key Terms
السؤال عن الدليل
الدلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first option for the respondent in presenting evidence (first method)?
A) He must always present evidence that proves his own view.
B) He may either present evidence for his own view or evidence against the opponent's view.
C) He must first ask the questioner for his madhhab.
D) He must present evidence from the Quran only.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, after stating one's position, the respondent must provide evidence; he may choose to support his own view or refute the opponent's, depending on knowledge of the opponent's madhhab.

The Order of Questioning in Jadal: Must You Ask About the Madhhab?
Author discusses the rule about the questioner skipping the madhhab question and Ibn Aqil's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Aqil's objection to skipping the madhhab question and identify the majority view on the permissible order of questioning in jadal.

Core Idea
In jadal, efficiency must yield to certainty: even known madhhabs must be re-verified.

This passage addresses a procedural question in dialectical debate: if the questioner already knows the respondent's madhhab, can he skip the initial 'What is your madhhab?' and go straight to asking for proof? The majority says yes, but Ibn Aqil objects, citing the possibility that the madhhab may have changed. This lesson walks through the two positions and the reasoning behind each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Find the majority view (al-jumhūr) and Ibn Aqil's objection in the Arabic. The majority permits skipping the madhhab question if the respondent's madhhab is known with certainty. Ibn Aqil forbids it.
2
Extract the reasoning for the majority
The majority argues that the madhhab question is instrumental: its purpose is to know the position to be debated. Once known, the question is unnecessary. Look for the phrase 'أغناهما علمهما'.
3
Extract Ibn Aqil's reasoning
Ibn Aqil argues that the respondent might have changed his madhhab, which would also change the proof. The questioner cannot be certain of the madhhab's stability. Find the phrase 'لجواز أن يكون المسئول غير مذهبه'.
4
Analyze the epistemological issue
The debate hinges on whether knowledge of the madhhab is certain or subject to change. Ibn Aqil assumes no confidence in the respondent's adherence. The majority assumes the respondent's known madhhab is reliable. Note the role of doubt (الظن) vs certainty (اليقين).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أغناهما علمهما عن ضرب من ضروب السؤال، وهو السؤال عن ماهية المذهب. ولهذا قال جمهور أهل النظر : إن السائل إذا كان عالما بمذهب المسئول، وكان مذهبه مشهورًا عنده، لا شك فيه كان له أن يبتدئ بالسؤال عن الدلالة إلا أن ابن عقيل منع ذلك لجواز أن يكون المسئول غير مذهبه، وذلك يستلزم أن يكون قد تغير دليله أيضًا، قال : قلت : وقد يجري في حكم الاجتهاد أن لا يسقط السؤال، لجواز تغير يطرأ على المذهب الذي عرف به فيزول ما عرفه، وذلك لعدم الثقة بالبقاء على المذهب، فلا غنى إذا عن السؤال.
Their knowledge made them independent of one type of question, which is the question about the essence of the madhhab. For this reason the majority of the people of theoretical reasoning said: If the questioner is knowledgeable of the respondent's madhhab, and his madhhab is well-known to him without doubt, he may begin with the question about the proof. However, Ibn Aqil forbade that because it is possible that the respondent might have changed from his madhhab, and that would entail that his proof might also have changed. He said: I said: And it may happen in the realm of ijtihad that the question is not dropped because of the possibility of a change that may occur to the madhhab known of him, thereby removing what he knew, due to the lack of confidence in adherence to the madhhab. So the question is indispensable.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage discusses the proper sequence of questioning in jadal. The author first quotes the view of the majority of scholars: if the questioner already knows the respondent's madhhab, he may skip the initial question 'What is your madhhab?' and proceed directly to ask for the proof (al-dalālah). This is because the purpose of the madhhab question is to know the position to be debated; if it is already known, the question serves no purpose. However, Ibn Aqil objects: the respondent might have abandoned his known madhhab or changed his opinion, which would invalidate the proof based on that madhhab. Since ijtihad can lead to changes, the questioner cannot be certain the madhhab has not changed. Therefore, the safety is to always ask. The passage highlights the tension between efficiency and certainty in debate.

Key Terms
السائل
المسئول
المذهب
الدلالة

B) When the questioner already knows the respondent's madhhab with certainty
C) When the questioner is a beginner
D) When the respondent asks to skip the madhhab question

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the madhhab question may be skipped if known with certainty, but Ibn Aqil's objection reminds us that certainty can be undermined by the possibility of change.

Three Valid Methods for Presenting Proof in Dialectic
Author enumerates three valid methods for the respondent to present proof, with examples and restrictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the three methods of presenting proof in a dialectical exchange and evaluate the validity of a given proof based on the conditions for each method.

Core Idea
Proof in dialectic can be presented directly, through a branch, or by building on another issue—but each method has conditions.

After the respondent has stated his madhhab, the questioner asks for proof. The author explains that the respondent has three permissible methods to present his proof, each with its own conditions and examples drawn from actual fiqh disputes. Understanding these methods will equip you to both construct valid arguments and detect invalid shortcuts in juristic debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dialectical context
Determine who is the questioner (sā'il) and who is the respondent (mas'ūl). Note whether the respondent knows the questioner's madhhab—this affects whether he may prove his own position or refute the opponent's.
2
Classify the proof method used
Read the respondent's proof and decide whether it is: (a) a direct proof on the exact issue, (b) a proof restricted to a branch of the issue, or (c) a proof that builds on a different issue. Use the textual clues from the passage.
Look for key phrases: 'يدل على المسألة بعينها', 'يفرض الدلالة في بعض شعب المسألة', 'يبنى المسألة على غيرها'.
3
Apply the conditions for the branch method
If the method is (b), verify that the chosen branch is genuinely part of the disputed issue. The counterexample of saffron water shows that if the branch is not of the same category, the proof fails.
Use the example: proving non-water removal with saffron water is invalid because saffron water is considered water.
4
Evaluate the validity of the proof
Based on the method and conditions, decide whether the respondent's proof is valid. For method (a) any direct proof is valid; for (b) the branch must be a true subdivision; for (c) building on another issue is always permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيا : السؤال عن الدليل والجواب عنه : والسؤال عن الدليل أن يقول السائل للمسئول بعد الجواب عن المذهب : ما الدليل على ذلك فيوجه على المسئول إقامة الدليل. ولا يخلو إما أن يعرف مذهب السائل أو لا يعرف؛ فإن عرف مذهبه دل عليه ثم هو بالخيار إن شاء دل على صحة قوله وإن شاء دل على فساد قول خصمه، وأيهما فعل من ذلك جاز. وإن لم يعرف مذهبه وفي المسألة أقوال مختلفة ومذاهب شتى، سأل السائل عن مذهبه ليكون الدليل على حسبه، فإذا عرف مذهبه دل عليه على ما تقدم وللمسئول في الدلالة ثلاث طرق: أ - أن يدل على المسألة بعينها فإن اختار هذا الطريق، حسب ما سئل، جاز بلا إشكال، ثم هو بالخيار؛ إن شاء دل عليه بدليل يخص المسئول عنه، وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على وجوب النية في الطهارة أن هذه طهارة تتعدى محل موجبها، فافتقرت إلى النية كالتيمم، وإن شاء دل على مسألة الخلاف بدليل يعمها ويعم غيرها، وذلك مثل استدلال المالكي على وجوب النية في الطهارة أن هذه عبادة، فافتقرت إلى النية كالصلاة. ب - أن يفرض الدلالة في بعض شعب المسألة وفصولها: واذا فعل هذا جاز أيضا، لأنه إذا كان الخلاف في الكل وثبت الدليل في بعضها، ثبت في الباقي بالإجماع. وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على الربا في المطعومات المدخرة للعيش غالبا مما لا يكال فيفرض الدليل في قليل البر وما أشبه ذلك مما يقع الخلاف في مواضع منه فيفرض الدلالة في بعضها، وإنما جاز ذلك لأنه دل على المسئول عنه بما سلكه من الطريق، وذلك أنه إذا كان الخلاف في الجميع واحدا، وثبت بعضها بما ذكره من الدليل ثبت الباقي بالإجماع. وإن أراد أن يفرض الدلالة في غير شعبة من شعب المسألة وفي غير فصل من فصولها لم يجز، وذلك مثل أن يسأل الحنفي عن إزالة النجاسة بغير الماء، فيقول: أنا أفرض الدلالة في إزالتها بماء الزعفران فإن الخلاف فيه وفي سائر المائعات واحد واذا ثبت هذا في ماء الزعفران ثبت في سائر المائعات، فهذا لا يجوز، وإنما كان كذلك لأن القصد بهذه المسألة إثبات جواز إزالة النجاسة بغير الماء، وماء الزعفران عندهم من جملة المياه ولهذا أجازوا الوضوء به، فإذا ثبت لهم جواز إزالة النجاسة بماء الزعفران لم يثبت لهم بذلك جواز إزالة النجاسة بغير الماء. ج - أن يبني المسألة على غيرها: فإذا فعل ذلك، كان جائزا، وإنما كان كذلك لأن البناء بيان طريق من طرق المسألة، فهو بمنزلة سائر طرق المسألة.
Secondly: The question about the proof and the answer to it. The question about the proof is that the questioner says to the respondent after the answer about the madhhab: 'What is the proof for that?' thus directing the respondent to produce proof. And it is inevitable that either he knows the madhhab of the questioner or does not know. If he knows his madhhab, he presents it; then he has the choice: if he wants, he may present proof for the correctness of his own statement, and if he wants, he may present proof for the falseness of his opponent's statement, and whichever he does of that is permissible. If he does not know his madhhab, and in the issue there are various opinions and diverse madhhabs, the questioner asks about his madhhab so that the proof is according to it. So when he knows his madhhab, he presents it as previously mentioned. And the respondent has three methods regarding the proof: (a) That he proves the exact issue. If he chooses this method, as requested, it is permissible without reservation; then he has the choice: if he wants, he may prove it by a proof specific to the subject at hand, such as the Maliki's reasoning for the obligation of intention in purification: 'This is a purification that extends beyond the place of its cause, so it requires intention, like tayammum.' Or if he wants, he may prove the issue of disagreement by a proof that covers it and others, such as the Maliki's reasoning: 'This is an act of worship, so it requires intention, like prayer.' (b) That he assumes the proof in some branches and subdivisions of the issue. If he does this, it is also permissible, because if the disagreement is about the whole and the proof is established for some of it, it is established for the rest by consensus. This is like the Maliki's reasoning about riba in foodstuffs generally stored for livelihood that are not measured: he assumes the proof in a little bit of wheat and similar things where disagreement occurs in some aspects, assuming the proof in some of them. It is permitted only because he has proven the subject at hand by the path he has taken, namely that when the disagreement about all is one, and some part is proven by the proof he mentioned, the rest is proven by consensus. However, if he wants to assume the proof in a branch that is not a branch of the issue or in a subdivision that is not a subdivision of it, it is not permissible. For example, if a Hanafi is asked about removing impurity with something other than water, and he says: 'I assume the proof in removing it with saffron water, for the disagreement about it and all other liquids is one, and if this is established for saffron water, it is established for all other liquids.' This is not permissible, because the purpose of this issue is to establish the permissibility of removing impurity with something other than water, and saffron water according to them is part of water, so they permit ablution with it. So if the permissibility of removing impurity with saffron water is established for them, the permissibility of removing impurity with something other than water is not established by that. (c) That he builds the issue on another issue. If he does that, it is permissible, because building is an explanation of a path among the paths of the issue, so it is like the other paths of the issue.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage explains the second major dialectical situation: after the respondent has stated his madhhab, the questioner asks for the proof. The author first clarifies the respondent's options based on whether he knows the questioner's madhhab. If known, the respondent may either prove his own position or refute the opponent's; both are allowed. If not, the questioner must first clarify his own madhhab. Then the author details three methods the respondent may use to present his proof. Method (a): Direct proof on the exact issue. The respondent can either use a proof specific to that issue (e.g., intention in purification because it extends beyond the cause) or a general proof that also covers other issues (e.g., it is worship). Method (b): Assuming the proof for a branch of the issue. This is valid because if the disagreement covers the whole class, proving one subset suffices by consensus for the remainder. An example is proving riba in some foodstuffs to extend to all. However, there is a restriction: the chosen branch must be a genuine part of the issue. The counterexample: using saffron water for impurity removal fails because saffron water is a type of water, not 'other than water', so proving on that branch does not serve the intended conclusion. Method (c): Building the issue on another issue entirely. This is allowed as a valid way to show a logical connection. The text emphasizes that this is equivalent to other methods. The passage provides concrete examples from fiqh (Maliki vs. Hanafi) illustrating how these methods work in practice.

Key Terms
السائل
المسئول
الدليل
شعبة
بناء

Checkpoint
Why is the example of assuming proof in saffron water for removing impurity with non-water invalid according to the passage?
A) Because saffron water is not a liquid
B) Because saffron water is considered water, so proving with it does not prove the general claim of removing impurity with non-water
C) Because the Hanafi school does not permit using saffron water
D) Because the disagreement about saffron water is not the same as about other liquids

Key Takeaway
The three methods—direct, via a genuine branch, or building on another issue—are valid paths for proof, but the branch method fails if the chosen branch is not a true subdivision of the disputed issue.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify which of the three methods (a, b, or c) is used in the following proof: 'The permissibility of selling dates for dates in equal measure is proven because it is a foodstuff, and all foodstuffs must be sold in equal measure when exchanged for the same type.' Then evaluate whether the respondent correctly applies the method.
