Building Issues and Handling Evidence Rejection in Jadal
Building issues and handling evidence rejection in jadal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of building an issue upon another in a jadal debate, identifying valid building scenarios and responding appropriately when an opponent rejects your evidence.

Core Idea
In jadal, you may build an issue on another only if they share the same logical method.

In dialectical debate, you sometimes need to base a disputed ruling on another issue that is more established. The author explains when this 'building' is permissible and how to handle a questioner who rejects your proof. This lesson will teach you the two types of building and the key condition of shared method, as well as the protocol when the opponent denies your evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of building
Look at the passage and note that building can be done either on a principle from usul al-fiqh (e.g., qiyas) or on a subsidiary issue (furu'). The examples given are: building the ruling on riba in honey on qiyas (usul), and building the impurity of hair on the fact that hair is not permeated by the soul (furu').
2
Check the condition of same method
The passage states that for building to be permissible, the two issues must share the same 'method' (tarīqa). This means they must be based on the same type of evidence or logical principle. Ask yourself: Are both issues approached through the same mode of reasoning?
Imam al-Haramayn adds a stricter condition: the cause ('illa) of the hypothesized issue must be comprehensive.
3
Recognize when building is invalid
The passage gives an example: a Hanafi building the killing of a Muslim for a disbeliever on the killing of a free person for a slave is invalid because the methods differ. Identify why the methods differ: one involves a comparison between Muslim and disbeliever, the other between free and slave.
4
Apply the protocol for evidence rejection
When the questioner rejects the respondent's proof (e.g., rejects dalīl al-khiṭāb or qiyas), the respondent must assert that it is his proof and give the questioner a choice: either concede it or transfer the discourse to the foundation of that proof. If the questioner refuses both, he is obstinate, and the respondent has fulfilled his duty.
The respondent is not required to provide proof that the questioner accepts; only to defend his own evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - أن يبني المسألة على غيرها : فإذا فعل ذلك، كان جائزا ، وإنما كان كذلك لأن البناء بيان طريق من طرق المسألة، فهو بمنزلة سائر طرق المسألة. وهذا على ضربين : أحدهما : أن يبني على المسألة من الأصول، مثل أن يسأل الظاهري مالكيًا عن الربا في العسل، فيقول له : هذا مبني عندنا على صحة القول بالقياس، فإن سلمت لي القول بالقياس، بنيت عليه هذه المسألة، وإن لم تسلم دللت عليه. والثاني : أن يبنيها على مسألة من مسائل الفروع، مثل أن يسأل المالكي، عن الشعر : هل ينجس بالموت؟ فيقول : هذه المسألة عندي مبنية على أن الشعر لا يحله الروح، فإن سلمت لي وإلا نقلت الكلام إليه . هذا إذا كانت المسألتان طريقتهما واحدة فإن اختلفت طرق المسائل، لم يجز بناء بعضها على بعض نحو أن يسأل الحنفي : هل يقتل المسلم بالكافر؟ فيقول : أنا أبني ذلك على أن الحر يقتل بالعبد، فهذا لا يصح في البناء، لأنهما مسألتان مختلفتان وليست إحداهما طريقا للأخرى، فلا يجوز أن [؟] عليها. هذا رأي الباجي، وهو قول جمهور أهل الجدل، وقال إمام الحرمين: إنما يجوز إذا كانت علة الفرض شاملة لسائر الأطراف، كما سبق بيانه . فإذا ذكر المسئول الدليل فإذا كان السائل يعتقد أن ما ذكره ليس بدليل، مثل أن يكون قد احتج بدليل الخطاب، والسائل حنفي لا يقول بدليل الخطاب، أو بالقياس والسائل ظاهري لا يقول بالقياس، فقال للمسئول : هذا ليس بدليل، فإن المسئول يقول له : هذا عندي دليل، وأنت بالخيار أن تسلمه أن تنقل الكلام إليه فأدل على صحته . فإن قال السائل : « لا أسلم لك ما احتججت به، ولا أنقل الكلام إلى أصل، كان معننا ومطالبا بما لا يجب عليه لأن المسئول لا يلزم أن يثبت مذهبه إلا بما هو دليل عنده ومن نازعه في دليله دل على صحته وقال بنصرته . فإذا فعل ذلك، فقد قام بما يجب عليه فيه وإن عدل إلى دليل غيره لم يكن منقطعًا، لأن ذلك لعجز السائل عن الاعتراض على ما احتج به وقصوره عن القدح فيه .
C. That he build the issue upon another: If he does that, it is permissible, because building is clarifying a method among the methods of the issue, so it is like the rest of the issue's methods. This is of two types: The first is to build upon the issue from the principles (usul), such as a Zahiri asking a Maliki about riba in honey, saying: 'This, according to us, is built upon the validity of qiyas. If you concede to me the validity of qiyas, I will build this issue upon it; if you do not concede, I will establish it.' The second is to build it upon an issue from the subsidiary matters (furu'), such as asking the Maliki about hair: does it become impure by death? He says: 'This issue, according to me, is built upon the fact that hair is not permeated by the soul. If you concede to me, then [ok]; otherwise, I will transfer the discourse to it.' This is when the two issues have the same method. If the methods of the issues differ, building one upon the other is not permissible, such as a Hanafi being asked: 'Is a Muslim killed for a disbeliever?' and he says: 'I build this upon the fact that a free person is killed for a slave.' This is invalid in building, because they are two different issues and one is not a method for the other, so it is not permissible to [?] upon it. This is the opinion of al-Baji, and it is the saying of the majority of the people of jadal. Imam al-Haramayn said: 'It is only permissible if the cause of the hypothesized matter encompasses all the sides,' as previously explained. Then, when the respondent mentions the proof, if the questioner believes that what he mentioned is not a proof, such as if he argued with dalil al-khitab and the questioner is a Hanafi who does not accept dalil al-khitab, or with qiyas and the questioner is a Zahiri who does not accept qiyas, and he says to the respondent: 'This is not a proof,' then the respondent says to him: 'This, according to me, is a proof, and you have the choice to either concede it or transfer the discourse to it so I may establish its correctness.' If the questioner says: 'I do not concede what you argued with, nor do I transfer the discourse to a foundation,' he is being stubborn and demanding what he is not obliged to, because the respondent is only required to establish his madhhab with what is a proof according to him. Whoever disputes with him regarding his proof, he indicates its correctness and defends it. If he does that, he has fulfilled what is required of him, even if he turns to another proof, he is not considered broken, because that is due to the questioner's inability to object to what he argued with and his weakness in refuting it.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage discusses the dialectical method of building one issue upon another in jadal. The author first states its permissibility and then divides it into two types. The first type involves building on a principle from usul al-fiqh, like a Maliki building the ruling on riba in honey upon the validity of qiyas. The second type involves building on a subsidiary issue, like the ruling on hair impurity being built on whether hair is permeated by the soul. The key condition is that the two issues must share the same method; if their methods differ, building is invalid, illustrated by the example of building the ruling of killing a Muslim for a disbeliever on the ruling of killing a free person for a slave. Then the author notes the opinion of Imam al-Haramayn that the cause must be inclusive. Finally, the passage addresses the scenario where the questioner rejects the respondent's evidence as invalid. The respondent may insist it is his proof and give the questioner a choice: either concede or challenge the foundation. If the questioner refuses both, he is considered obstinate, and the respondent is not obliged to provide further proof. This protects the respondent from being forced to use evidence he does not accept.

Key Terms
بناء المسألة على غيرها

Types of Building (Bina') in Jadal: On Usul and Furu'
Author defines two types of building in jadal: on usul and on furu' with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between building on usul and building on furu' in jadal and evaluate whether the condition of shared tariqa is satisfied.

Core Idea
Building in jadal requires the two issues to share the same method—different madhhabs or 'illas break the chain.

In dialectical disputation (jadal), a respondent often ties a contested ruling to a more foundational point—either a principle of usul or another subsidiary ruling. This technique is called building (bina'), and it can only work if the two issues are methodologically compatible. In this passage, the author explains the two types and gives vivid examples from actual fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of building
The passage contrasts building on an usul principle (e.g., qiyas) with building on a furu' issue (e.g., whether hair is pervaded by spirit). First, locate the definitions and the key examples.
Note the shift: building is extended here to furu', not just usul.
2
Analyse the first example (riba in honey)
In the first example, a Zahiri asks a Maliki about riba in honey. The Maliki builds his answer on the validity of qiyas. Trace the logic: if the questioner grants qiyas, the ruling follows; if not, the respondent must prove qiyas first.
3
Analyse the second example (hair impurity)
A Maliki is asked whether hair becomes impure at death. He builds on a separate issue: whether hair is pervaded by the spirit. This is a furu' issue. Identify the condition: the methods must be the same.
Here the method is the legal cause or reasoning pathway.
4
Test the condition using the counterexample
A Hanafi is asked about killing a Muslim for a disbeliever and tries to build it on the ruling that a free person is killed for a slave. The passage says this is invalid because the methods differ. Determine why—likely because the 'illa of 'equality in retaliation' does not apply across the two cases.
The Hanafi's attempted building is rejected because the issues do not share the same tariqa.
5
Evaluate the additional view of Imam al-Haramayn
Imam al-Haramayn adds a stricter condition: building is only permissible if the 'illa of the assumed ruling encompasses all sides. Consider how this refines the majority view of al-Baji.
This condition may prevent building when the 'illa is not comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما : أن يبني على المسألة من الأصول، مثل أن يسأل الظاهري مالكيًا عن الربا في العسل، فيقول له : هذا مبني عندنا على صحة القول بالقياس، فإن سلمت لي القول بالقياس، بنيت عليه هذه المسألة، وإن لم تسلم دللت عليه». والثاني : أن يبنيها على مسألة من مسائل الفروع، مثل أن يسأل المالكي، عن الشعر : هل ينجس بالموت؟ فيقول : هذه المسألة عندي مبنية على أن الشعر لا يحله الروح، فإن سلمت لي وإلا نقلت الكلام إليه . هذا إذا كانت المسألتان طريقتهما واحدة فإن اختلفت طرق المسائل، لم يجز بناء بعضها على بعض نحو أن يسأل الحنفي : هل يقتل المسلم بالكافر؟ فيقول : أنا أبني ذلك على أن الحر يقتل بالعبد، فهذا لا يصح في البناء، لأنهما مسألتان مختلفتان وليست إحداهما طريقا للأخرى، فلا يجوز أن عليها (1). هذا رأي الباجي، وهو قول جمهور أهل الجدل، وقال إمام الحرمين: إنما يجوز إذا كانت علة الفرض شاملة لسائر الأطراف(2)، كما سبق بيانه .
One of them: that he builds [the ruling] on the [original] principle from the foundations, such as when a Zahiri asks a Maliki about riba in honey, and he says to him: 'This is built in our view on the validity of qiyas; if you grant me qiyas, I build this ruling on it, and if you do not grant it, I will prove it.' And the second: that he builds it on a subsidiary issue, such as when a Maliki is asked about hair: 'Is it impure by death?' He says: 'This issue in my view is built on the fact that hair is not pervaded by the spirit; if you grant it, [fine]; otherwise, I transfer the discussion to it.' This applies when the two issues share the same method. If the methods differ, it is not permissible to build one upon the other, such as when a Hanafi is asked: 'Is a Muslim killed for a disbeliever?' He says: 'I build that on the fact that a free person is killed for a slave.' This is not valid in building, because they are two different issues and neither is a path to the other; thus it is not permissible to build upon it. This is the view of al-Baji, and it is the statement of the majority of jadal scholars. And Imam al-Haramayn said: It is only permissible if the cause of the assumed ruling encompasses all sides, as previously explained.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage from Jadal Usuliyyin explains the two types of building (bina') in dialectical disputation. Building is a technique where the respondent ties a contested ruling to a more foundational principle that the questioner either accepts or must be established separately. The first type is building on an usul principle, such as the validity of qiyas. The author gives an example: a Zahiri (who rejects qiyas) asks a Maliki about riba in honey. The Maliki respondent says that this issue is built on the acceptance of qiyas; if the questioner concedes qiyas, the ruling follows, otherwise the respondent will prove qiyas first. The second type is building on a furu' (subsidiary) issue. For example, a Maliki is asked about whether hair is impure upon death; he replies that this ruling is built on whether the hair is pervaded by the spirit—a separate issue. The condition is that both issues must share the same methodological approach (tariqa); if they differ, building is invalid. The author gives a counterexample: a Hanafi asked about killing a Muslim for a disbeliever should not build it on the ruling of killing a free person for a slave because the legal methodologies differ. Al-Baji and the majority of jadal scholars hold this view, while Imam al-Haramayn adds that building is only allowed if the 'illa (cause) of the assumed ruling encompasses all sides. This lesson illustrates a key dialectical tool for structuring disputes in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
بناء
أصول
فروع
طريقة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is building one ruling on another invalid?
A) When the two issues are derived from different madhhabs.
B) When the two issues do not share the same methodological approach (tariqa).
C) When the ruling is built on a furu' issue rather than an usul.
D) When the questioner does not accept qiyas.

Key Takeaway
Building (bina') in jadal is a powerful technique, but it is only valid when the two issues share the same methodological approach (tariqa); otherwise, the argument fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a jadal exchange where a Shafi'i builds the ruling on the permissibility of selling dates in advance (salam) on the general principle of qiyas. Identify whether the condition of shared tariqa is satisfied.
?
Evaluate the following attempted building: A Hanbali is asked about the impurity of dog saliva; he builds it on the ruling that dog hair is impure. Using the passage, determine whether this building is valid, and explain why.

Manifest vs. Hidden Proofs: The Respondent's Procedural Rights
Author explains respondent's options when proof is contested, distinguishing manifest and hidden proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a manifest proof (dalil jali) and a hidden proof (dalil khafi) and apply the correct procedural response when a questioner disputes each.

Core Idea
A manifest proof needs no prior acceptance; a hidden proof does.

In dialectical exchanges, not all proofs are equal when contested. The classical jadal tradition distinguishes manifest proofs (well-known) from hidden ones (less known). This lesson explains the respondent's obligations when the questioner rejects each type, and highlights a key disagreement from Abu Ali al-Tabari.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The respondent presents a proof; the questioner rejects it because he does not accept that type of proof (e.g., Hanafi rejecting dalil al-khitab, Zahiri rejecting qiyas).
The questioner must state his rejection explicitly.
2
Recognize the two options
The respondent offers two choices: either concede the proof (istislam) or transfer the discussion (naql al-kalam) to the proof itself so the respondent can demonstrate its validity.
If the questioner refuses both, he is considered obstinate.
3
Distinguish manifest vs. hidden
A manifest proof (dalil jali) is widely recognized and does not require prior acceptance. A hidden proof (dalil khafi) is not widely recognized; according to Abu Ali al-Tabari, it requires the questioner's prior acceptance before use.
The author sides with the majority: even for hidden proofs, the respondent can defend them.
4
Apply the rule
For a manifest proof, the questioner's refusal to accept or transfer leads to obstinacy. For a hidden proof, the majority view allows the respondent to defend it, while Tabari requires prior acceptance.
The respondent is not required to know the questioner's madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا ذكر المسئول الدليل فإذا كان السائل يعتقد أن ما ذكره ليس بدليل، مثل أن يكون قد احتج بدليل الخطاب، والسائل حنفي لا يقول بدليل الخطاب، أو بالقياس والسائل ظاهري لا يقول بالقياس، فقال للمسئول : هذا ليس بدليل، فإن المسئول يقول له : هذا عندي دليل، وأنت بالخيار أن تسلمه أن تنقل الكلام إليه فأدل على صحته . بين فإن قال السائل : « لا أسلم لك ما احتججت به، ولا أنقل الكلام إلى أصل، كان معننا ومطالبا بما لا يجب عليه لأن المسئول لا يلزم أن يثبت مذهبه إلا بما هو دليل عنده ومن نازعه في دليله دل على صحته وقال بنصرته . فإذا فعل ذلك، فقد قام بما يجب عليه فيه وإن عدل إلى دليل غيره لم يكن منقطعًا، لأن ذلك لعجز السائل عن الاعتراض على ما احتج به وقصوره عن القدح فيه . هذا إذا كان الدليل الذي احتج به أصلا جليا مشهورًا؛ فأما إن كان دليلا خفيا، فنازعه السائل فيه وامتنع من تسليمه فهو بمنزلة الجلي المشهور. وقال أبو علي الطبري : يكون المسئول منقطعًا، ولا يجوز أن يستدل بأصل خفي إلا بعد أن يستسلمه منه، فإن سلمه احتج به، وإن لم يسلمه دل عليه. فأما إذا احتج به، ثم نازعه السائل ولم يسلمه له لم يكن له تبيينه، ويكون ذلك من جهته وانقطاعا منه ويخالف الأصل المشهور، لأن شهرته تغنيه عن استسلامه وتبيينه . مثاله : أن يسأل حنبلي عن الحج هل يسقط بالموت؟ فيقول : لا يسقط ، لأنه حق تدخله النيابة استقر عليه حال الحياة، فلم يسقط بالموت، كالدين. فيقول السائل : لا أسلم أن النيابة تدخل الحج . فيقول له المسئول : إما أن تسلمه واما أن تنقل الكلام إلى النيابة فأدل على جوازها، فيكون المسئول مصيبًا إلا على قول أبي على الطبري، لأنه لا يلزمه أن يثبت مذهبه إلا بما هو دليل عنده، ومن نازعه فيما هو دليل عنده لا يلزمه أكثر من إقامة الدليل على صحته. وإذا فعل هذا فقد قام بما وجب عليه فهو بمنزلة المحتج بدليل جلي مشهور نازعه السائل فيه .. ولأن المسئول لا يلزمه معرفة مذهب السائل، لأنه لا تضره مخالفته ولا تنفعه موافقته، وانما المعوّل على الدليل يدل على صحة العلة وأوصافها، فإذا كان كذلك فخالفه السائل في أوصافها، لا يدل على تفريطه إذا أمكنه إقامة الدليل على صحتها ووجوب ،اعتبارها فلم يجز أن يكون سببًا لانقطاعه ومنسوبا إلى التقصير من أجله . وأما السائل إذا عارضه بما هو دليل عنده وليس بدليل عند المسئول، مثل أن
When the respondent mentions the proof, if the questioner believes that what he mentioned is not a proof — such as if he argued with the proof of address (dalil al-khitab) and the questioner is a Hanafi who does not accept dalil al-khitab, or with qiyas and the questioner is a Zahiri who does not accept qiyas — and he says to the respondent: 'This is not a proof,' then the respondent says to him: 'This is a proof in my view; you have the choice either to concede it or to transfer the discussion to it so that I may prove its correctness.' Then if the questioner says: 'I do not concede what you argued with, nor do I transfer the discussion to an [underlying] principle,' he is being obstinate and demanding what is not obligatory upon [the respondent], because the respondent is only required to establish his madhhab by what is a proof in his view, and whoever disputes his proof must demonstrate its correctness and defend it. If he does that, he has fulfilled what is incumbent upon him, and even if he turns to another proof, he is not considered refuted, because that is due to the questioner's inability to object to what he argued with and his failure to criticize it. This applies if the proof he argued with is a manifest and well-known principle. But if it is a hidden proof, and the questioner disputes it and refuses to concede it, it is like the manifest well-known [case]. However, Abu Ali al-Tabari said: The respondent is refuted, and it is not permissible for him to use a hidden principle unless he first secures its acceptance from [the questioner]; if he concedes it, he argues with it, and if he does not concede it, he proves it. But if he argues with it, then the questioner disputes it and does not concede it to him, he has no opportunity to explain it, and that would be a failure on his part and a refutation from him, and it differs from the manifest principle because its notoriety suffices from requiring its acceptance and explanation. Its example: A Hanbali is asked about Hajj: 'Is it dropped by death?' He says: 'It is not dropped, because it is a right that admits deputyship, established during life, so it is not dropped by death, like a debt.' The questioner says: 'I do not concede that deputyship enters into Hajj.' The respondent says to him: 'Either you concede it, or you transfer the discussion to deputyship so that I may prove its permissibility.' Thus the respondent is correct except according to the opinion of Abu Ali al-Tabari, because he is not required to establish his madhhab except by what is a proof in his view, and whoever disputes what is a proof in his view is not obligated to do more than provide evidence for its correctness. And if he does this, he has fulfilled what is obligatory upon him, and he is like one who argues with a manifest well-known proof that the questioner disputed. And because the respondent is not required to know the madhhab of the questioner, since his opposition harms him not nor does his agreement benefit him; rather, reliance is on the proof that demonstrates the correctness of the cause and its attributes. If that is the case, then if the questioner opposes him regarding its attributes, it does not indicate negligence on his part if he is able to provide evidence for its correctness and the necessity of considering it; therefore, it is not permissible that this be a cause for his refutation and that he be attributed with deficiency because of it. As for the questioner if he opposes him with what is a proof in his view but not a proof in the respondent's view, such as...
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of jadal by addressing what happens when a respondent presents a proof (dalil) that the questioner does not accept. The author gives two examples: a Hanafi who rejects dalil al-khitab, and a Zahiri who rejects qiyas. In such cases, the respondent informs the questioner that the proof is valid in his own view and gives the questioner a choice: either accept the proof as is, or 'transfer the discussion' (naql al-kalam) to the proof itself so that the respondent can justify it. If the questioner refuses both options, he is considered obstinate (mu'annan) and is demanding what is not required — because the respondent is only obligated to prove his position using evidence that he considers valid. If the questioner challenges the proof, the respondent must then defend it. The passage then draws an important distinction between a manifest, well-known proof (dalil jali mashhur) and a hidden proof (dalil khafi). For a manifest proof, the respondent is not considered refuted (munqati') even if he switches to another proof, because the questioner's failure to object stems from his own inability. For a hidden proof, however, the situation is different: Abu Ali al-Tabari holds that the respondent would be refuted if he uses a hidden proof without first securing the questioner's acceptance of it, because the notoriety of a manifest proof makes it unnecessary to seek prior acceptance. The author gives a concrete example: a Hanbali asked whether Hajj is dropped by death argues by analogy to a debt, using the attribute of deputyship (niyaba). The questioner refuses to concede that Hajj admits deputyship. The respondent then offers the two options: concede it or transfer to the issue of deputyship. According to the majority, the respondent is correct (musib) except on al-Tabari's view. The rationale is that the respondent is not required to know the questioner's madhhab, nor does the questioner's rejection harm him; the proof itself is what matters. If the questioner contests the attributes of the proof, the respondent need only demonstrate its correctness. The passage ends by hinting at the opposite scenario: when the questioner introduces a proof that is valid in his view but not in the respondent's view. This lesson illustrates a key procedural rule in dialectical exchanges, protecting the respondent from being declared defeated due to mere disagreement over the validity of a proof.

دليل خفي
انقطاع
استسلام

D) The questioner must concede the hidden proof.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between manifest and hidden proofs determines whether a respondent must seek prior acceptance before using a proof or can simply defend it when contested.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

You want to argue that the verse indicates wiping the whole head. Is this a manifest or hidden proof? What should you do if your opponent rejects it? The Hajj Example: Case Study in Jadal Procedure
Author illustrates the rules with a concrete example of Hajj and debt analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange in the Hajj example and identify the procedural steps according to the rules of jadal.

Core Idea
In a dispute about Hajj after death, the analogy to debt triggers debate about hidden proofs.

The author provides a concrete example to illustrate the rules: a Hanbali is asked whether Hajj is dropped by death. He argues by analogy to a debt, using the attribute of deputyship. The questioner rejects the premise. This example shows how the respondent handles the situation and where opinions diverge.

Guided Path
1
Set the scene
A Hanbali is asked: 'Is Hajj dropped by death?' The respondent says no.
The question is about a legal ruling after death.
2
Identify the respondent's proof
He argues that Hajj is not dropped because it is a right that admits deputyship, established during life, so it does not drop by death—analogous to a debt.
The proof uses qiyas with the attribute of deputyship (niyaba).
3
Note the questioner's objection
The questioner says: 'I do not concede that deputyship enters into Hajj.'
This is a dispute over the attribute of the proof.
4
Apply the procedural options
The respondent says: 'Either you concede it, or transfer the discussion to deputyship so I can prove its permissibility.'
This mirrors the rule from the first part of the passage.
5
Evaluate the correctness
The author says the respondent is correct except on Abu Ali al-Tabari's view. The majority holds that the respondent is not required to know the questioner's madhhab; he just needs to defend his proof.
Tabari's view requires prior acceptance for hidden proofs.

فإذا ذكر المسئول الدليل فإذا كان السائل يعتقد أن ما ذكره ليس بدليل، مثل أن يكون قد احتج بدليل الخطاب، والسائل حنفي لا يقول بدليل الخطاب، أو بالقياس والسائل ظاهري لا يقول بالقياس، فقال للمسئول : هذا ليس بدليل، فإن المسئول يقول له : هذا عندي دليل، وأنت بالخيار أن تسلمه أن تنقل الكلام إليه فأدل على صحته . بين فإن قال السائل : « لا أسلم لك ما احتججت به، ولا أنقل الكلام إلى أصل، كان معننا ومطالبا بما لا يجب عليه لأن المسئول لا يلزم أن يثبت مذهبه إلا بما هو دليل عنده ومن نازعه في دليله دل على صحته وقال بنصرته . فإذا فعل ذلك، فقد قام بما يجب عليه فيه وإن عدل إلى دليل غيره لم يكن منقطعًا، لأن ذلك لعجز السائل عن الاعتراض على ما احتج به وقصوره عن القدح فيه . هذا إذا كان الدليل الذي احتج به أصلا جليا مشهورًا؛ فأما إن كان دليلا خفيا، فنازعه السائل فيه وامتنع من تسليمه فهو بمنزلة الجلي المشهور. وقال أبو علي الطبري : يكون المسئول منقطعًا، ولا يجوز أن يستدل بأصل خفي إلا بعد أن يستسلمه منه، فإن سلمه احتج به، وإن لم يسلمه دل عليه. فأما إذا احتج به، ثم نازعه السائل ولم يسلمه له لم يكن له تبيينه، ويكون ذلك من جهته وانقطاعا منه ويخالف الأصل المشهور، لأن شهرته تغنيه عن استسلامه وتبيينه . مثاله : أن يسأل حنبلي عن الحج هل يسقط بالموت؟ فيقول : لا يسقط ، لأنه حق تدخله النيابة استقر عليه حال الحياة، فلم يسقط بالموت، كالدين. فيقول السائل : لا أسلم أن النيابة تدخل الحج . فيقول له المسئول : إما أن تسلمه واما أن تنقل الكلام إلى النيابة فأدل على جوازها، فيكون المسئول مصيبًا إلا على قول أبي على الطبري، لأنه لا يلزمه أن يثبت مذهبه إلا بما هو دليل عنده، ومن نازعه فيما هو دليل عنده لا يلزمه أكثر من إقامة الدليل على صحته. وإذا فعل هذا فقد قام بما وجب عليه فهو بمنزلة المحتج بدليل جلي مشهور نازعه السائل فيه .. ولأن المسئول لا يلزمه معرفة مذهب السائل، لأنه لا تضره مخالفته ولا تنفعه موافقته، وانما المعوّل على الدليل يدل على صحة العلة وأوصافها، فإذا كان كذلك فخالفه السائل في أوصافها، لا يدل على تفريطه إذا أمكنه إقامة الدليل على صحتها ووجوب ،اعتبارها فلم يجز أن يكون سببًا لانقطاعه ومنسوبا إلى التقصير من أجله . وأما السائل إذا عارضه بما هو دليل عنده وليس بدليل عند المسئول، مثل أن
When the respondent mentions the proof, if the questioner believes that what he mentioned is not a proof — such as if he argued with the proof of address (dalil al-khitab) and the questioner is a Hanafi who does not accept dalil al-khitab, or with qiyas and the questioner is a Zahiri who does not accept qiyas — and he says to the respondent: 'This is not a proof,' then the respondent says to him: 'This is a proof in my view; you have the choice either to concede it or to transfer the discussion to it so that I may prove its correctness.' Then if the questioner says: 'I do not concede what you argued with, nor do I transfer the discussion to an [underlying] principle,' he is being obstinate and demanding what is not obligatory upon [the respondent], because the respondent is only required to establish his madhhab by what is a proof in his view, and whoever disputes his proof must demonstrate its correctness and defend it. If he does that, he has fulfilled what is incumbent upon him, and even if he turns to another proof, he is not considered refuted, because that is due to the questioner's inability to object to what he argued with and his failure to criticize it. This applies if the proof he argued with is a manifest and well-known principle. But if it is a hidden proof, and the questioner disputes it and refuses to concede it, it is like the manifest well-known [case]. However, Abu Ali al-Tabari said: The respondent is refuted, and it is not permissible for him to use a hidden principle unless he first secures its acceptance from [the questioner]; if he concedes it, he argues with it, and if he does not concede it, he proves it. But if he argues with it, then the questioner disputes it and does not concede it to him, he has no opportunity to explain it, and that would be a failure on his part and a refutation from him, and it differs from the manifest principle because its notoriety suffices from requiring its acceptance and explanation. Its example: A Hanbali is asked about Hajj: 'Is it dropped by death?' He says: 'It is not dropped, because it is a right that admits deputyship, established during life, so it is not dropped by death, like a debt.' The questioner says: 'I do not concede that deputyship enters into Hajj.' The respondent says to him: 'Either you concede it, or you transfer the discussion to deputyship so that I may prove its permissibility.' Thus the respondent is correct except according to the opinion of Abu Ali al-Tabari, because he is not required to establish his madhhab except by what is a proof in his view, and whoever disputes what is a proof in his view is not obligated to do more than provide evidence for its correctness. And if he does this, he has fulfilled what is obligatory upon him, and he is like one who argues with a manifest well-known proof that the questioner disputed. And because the respondent is not required to know the madhhab of the questioner, since his opposition harms him not nor does his agreement benefit him; rather, reliance is on the proof that demonstrates the correctness of the cause and its attributes. If that is the case, then if the questioner opposes him regarding its attributes, it does not indicate negligence on his part if he is able to provide evidence for its correctness and the necessity of considering it; therefore, it is not permissible that this be a cause for his refutation and that he be attributed with deficiency because of it. As for the questioner if he opposes him with what is a proof in his view but not a proof in the respondent's view, such as...
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of jadal by addressing what happens when a respondent presents a proof (dalil) that the questioner does not accept. The author gives two examples: a Hanafi who rejects dalil al-khitab, and a Zahiri who rejects qiyas. In such cases, the respondent informs the questioner that the proof is valid in his own view and gives the questioner a choice: either accept the proof as is, or 'transfer the discussion' (naql al-kalam) to the proof itself so that the respondent can justify it. If the questioner refuses both options, he is considered obstinate (mu'annan) and is demanding what is not required — because the respondent is only obligated to prove his position using evidence that he considers valid. If the questioner challenges the proof, the respondent must then defend it. The passage then draws an important distinction between a manifest, well-known proof (dalil jali mashhur) and a hidden proof (dalil khafi). For a manifest proof, the respondent is not considered refuted (munqati') even if he switches to another proof, because the questioner's failure to object stems from his own inability. For a hidden proof, however, the situation is different: Abu Ali al-Tabari holds that the respondent would be refuted if he uses a hidden proof without first securing the questioner's acceptance of it, because the notoriety of a manifest proof makes it unnecessary to seek prior acceptance. The author gives a concrete example: a Hanbali asked whether Hajj is dropped by death argues by analogy to a debt, using the attribute of deputyship (niyaba). The questioner refuses to concede that Hajj admits deputyship. The respondent then offers the two options: concede it or transfer to the issue of deputyship. According to the majority, the respondent is correct (musib) except on al-Tabari's view. The rationale is that the respondent is not required to know the questioner's madhhab, nor does the questioner's rejection harm him; the proof itself is what matters. If the questioner contests the attributes of the proof, the respondent need only demonstrate its correctness. The passage ends by hinting at the opposite scenario: when the questioner introduces a proof that is valid in his view but not in the respondent's view. This lesson illustrates a key procedural rule in dialectical exchanges, protecting the respondent from being declared defeated due to mere disagreement over the validity of a proof.

دليل خفي
انقطاع
استسلام

The Hajj example demonstrates that a respondent can defend even a disputed attribute by offering the questioner the choice to concede or transfer the discussion, and according to the majority, this suffices to avoid refutation.

Objecting to Clear and Hidden Proofs in Jadal
Author explains the difference between clear (jali) and hidden (khafi) proofs and the obligations of respondent and objector.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the dialectical treatment of clear and hidden proofs and apply the rules of objection using the example of Hajj and debt analogy.

Core Idea
The respondent's burden of proof shifts depending on the notoriety of his evidence.

In jadal, not all proofs are equal. A clear proof requires immediate acceptance, while a hidden proof may be challenged. This lesson examines the rules governing objections and the respondent's obligations, illustrated by a Hanbali's argument about Hajj.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish clear vs hidden proof
Identify whether the proof (dalil) adduced is well-known (jali) or obscure (khafi). The text states that a clear proof is accepted immediately, while a hidden proof can be disputed.
This distinction determines the respondent's next actions.
2
Respondent's obligation for clear proof
If the proof is clear, the respondent does not need to seek acceptance or clarify it; the objector's dispute is invalid.
The author says: 'its notoriety suffices him from seeking acceptance and clarification.'
3
Respondent's obligation for hidden proof
If the proof is hidden, the respondent must either obtain the objector's acceptance or demonstrate the proof. Failure to do so, according to Abu Ali al-Tabari, makes the respondent 'cut off' (munqati').
The text contrasts this with the clear proof case.
4
Analyze the Hanbali example
Identify the proof type in the example: the analogy between Hajj and debt is a hidden proof because the objector disputes that substitution applies to Hajj. The respondent offers two options: accept or move to discuss substitution.
The author validates the respondent's approach except on al-Tabari's view.
5
Apply the rule to determine outcome
Based on the author's ruling, the respondent is not cut off because he can establish the proof. Only al-Tabari would consider him cut off for not having prior acceptance.
This illustrates the practical application of the rule.

هذا إذا كان الدليل الذي احتج به أصلا جليا مشهورا؛ فأما إن كان دليلا خفيا، فنازعه السائل فيه وامتنع من تسليمه فهو بمنزلة الجلي المشهور. وقال أبو علي الطبري : يكون المسئول منقطعا، ولا يجوز أن يستدل بأصل خفي إلا بعد أن يستسلمه منه، فإن سلمه احتج به، وإن لم يسلمه دل عليه. فأما إذا احتج به، ثم نازعه السائل ولم يسلمه له لم يكن له تبيينه، ويكون ذلك من جهته وانقطاعا منه ويخالف الأصل المشهور، لأن شهرته تغنيه عن استسلامه وتبيينه. مثاله : أن يسأل حنبلي عن الحج هل يسقط بالموت؟ فيقول : لا يسقط ، لأنه حق تدخله النيابة استقر عليه حال الحياة، فلم يسقط بالموت، كالدين. فيقول السائل : لا أسلم أن النيابة تدخل الحج . فيقول له المسئول : إما أن تسلمه وإما أن تنقل الكلام إلى النيابة فأدل على جوازها، فيكون المسئول مصيبا إلا على قول أبي على الطبري، لأنه لا يلزمه أن يثبت مذهبه إلا بما هو دليل عنده، ومن نازعه فيما هو دليل عنده لا يلزمه أكثر من إقامة الدليل على صحته. وإذا فعل هذا فقد قام بما وجب عليه فهو بمنزلة المحتج بدليل جلي مشهور نازعه السائل فيه .. ولأن المسئول لا يلزمه معرفة مذهب السائل، لأنه لا تضره مخالفته ولا تنفعه موافقته، وإنما المعول على الدليل يدل على صحة العلة وأوصافها، فإذا كان كذلك فخالفه السائل في أوصافها، لا يدل على تفريطه إذا أمكنه إقامة الدليل على صحتها ووجوب اعتبارها فلم يجز أن يكون سببا لانقطاعه ومنسوبا إلى التقصير من أجله . وأما السائل إذا عارضه بما هو دليل عنده وليس بدليل عند المسئول، مثل أن يعارض خبره المسند بخبر مرسل أو خبر المعروف بخبر المجهول، وما أشبه ذلك، وقال المسئول : إما أن تسلم ذلك لي فيكون معارضا لما رويته، وإما أن تنقل الكلام إلى مسألة المرسل والمجهول ؛ فهذا ليس للسائل أن يقوله ويخالف المسئول فيه، لأن السائل تابع للمستدل فيما يورده المسئول ويحتج به، لأنه لما عن دليله الذي دله على صحة مذهبه والطريق الذي أداه إلى اعتقاده لزمه أن ينظر معه فيما يورده، فإن كان فاسدا بين فساده وإن لم يكن فاسدا صار إليه سلمه له. ولهذا المعنى جاز للمسئول أن يفرض المسألة حيث أجازه، وكان السائل تابعا له، ولم يجز للسائل أن ينقله إلى جنبة أخرى ويفرضها فيه وليس للسائل مطالبة المسئول بتعيين الدلالة كما ليس له ذلك في الفتوى، فيقول : لا نستدل إلا بالدليل الفلاني، أو في الموضع الفلاني، إلا أن يكون السائل مسترشدا مستهديا، فيقول : كيف وجه التعلق بالدليل الفلاني، أو وجه تصحيح الجانب الفلاني؟ أو يقول : هل في المسألة من دليل العقل، أو من السنة المتواترة، أو من نوع كذا من الإجماع، أومن القياس؟ . . فيكون سؤاله صحيحا، والمسئول بإجابته إلى ذلك مأخوذا مصيبا
This is if the proof he adduced as an origin is clear and well-known. But if it is a hidden proof, and the objector disputes it and refuses to accept it, then it is like the clear and well-known. Abu Ali al-Tabari said: The respondent becomes cut off, and it is not permissible to argue with a hidden origin except after he has obtained acceptance of it from him. If he accepts it, he argues with it; if he does not accept it, he demonstrates it. But if he argued with it, then the objector disputed it and did not accept it, then he has no right to clarify it, and that is from his side and a cut-off from him, and it differs from the well-known origin, because its notoriety suffices him from seeking acceptance and clarification of it. Example: A Hanbali is asked about Hajj: is it dropped by death? He says: No, because it is a right that admits substitution, established during life, so it is not dropped by death, like debt. The objector says: I do not accept that substitution enters Hajj. The respondent says: Either you accept it, or you move the discussion to substitution so I may demonstrate its permissibility. Then the respondent is correct except according to Abu Ali al-Tabari, because he is not required to prove his school except by what is a proof according to him, and whoever disputes him regarding what is a proof according to him is not required more than establishing the proof of its correctness. And if he does this, he has done what was obligatory upon him, so he is like one arguing with a clear well-known proof that the objector disputed. And because the respondent is not required to know the school of the objector, since his opposition does not harm him nor his agreement benefit him; rather reliance is on the proof that indicates the correctness of the cause and its attributes. So if that is the case, and the objector opposed him in its attributes, that does not indicate negligence on his part if he can establish the proof of its correctness and the obligation of considering it, so it is not permissible that it be a reason for his cut-off and being attributed to shortcoming. As for the objector, if he counters him with what is a proof according to him but not a proof according to the respondent, such as countering his musnad hadith with a mursal hadith, or the known reporter's report with an unknown one, and similar, and the respondent says: Either you accept that for me so it is a counter to what I narrated, or you move the discussion to the issue of mursal and unknown. This the objector has no right to say and oppose the respondent in it, because the objector is a follower of the one adducing the proof in what the respondent presents and argues with. Because when the respondent argues with his proof which indicates the correctness of his school and the path that led him to his belief, it is necessary for the objector to examine with him what he presents; if it is corrupt, he shows its corruption; if it is not corrupt, he follows it and accepts it. For this meaning it is permissible for the respondent to assume the issue where he permits it, and the objector is a follower of him, and it is not permissible for the objector to transfer it to another side and assume it in it. And the objector does not have the right to demand from the respondent the specification of the indication, just as he does not have that in fatwa, saying: We only argue with such and such a proof, or in such and such a place. Except if the objector is seeking guidance and direction, then he says: How is the aspect of attachment to such and such a proof? Or the aspect of correcting such and such a side? Or he says: Is there in the issue a proof from reason, or from the mutawatir sunna, or from a certain type of consensus, or analogy? Then his question is correct, and the respondent in answering that is taken and correct.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical explanation of the rules governing objections in jadal, specifically when the respondent uses a clear (jali) versus a hidden (khafi) proof. The author begins by stating that if the proof is clear and well-known, the objector's dispute is unacceptable. If the proof is hidden, the objector may dispute it, and the respondent must either obtain acceptance or demonstrate the proof. Abu Ali al-Tabari's stricter view is noted: the respondent is cut off if he fails to get acceptance. An example follows: a Hanbali is asked whether Hajj is dropped by death. He analogizes it to debt (dayn) which is not dropped by death, using the 'illa of substitution (niyaba). The objector disputes that substitution applies to Hajj. The respondent then offers two options: accept the premise or move to discuss substitution. The author validates the respondent's approach, except on al-Tabari's view. The argument proceeds logically: the respondent only needs to prove his own evidence; he need not know the objector's school. The objector cannot force the respondent to use specific proofs. However, if the objector is sincerely seeking guidance, he may ask about the aspect of the proof (wajh al-dalil), and the respondent should answer. The passage ends by noting that the objector may ask about reason, sunna, consensus, or analogy, and then the respondent's answer is correct.

السائل

The rules of jadal require that the respondent's burden depends on the clarity of his proof, and the objector may not force specific proofs unless seeking guidance.

Questioning the Aspect of the Proof (Wajh al-Dalil)
Author discusses the third type of objection: asking the respondent to clarify how the proof supports the ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objector may demand clarification of the proof's aspect and explain why a single proof can have multiple aspects.

Core Idea
A single proof text can support a ruling from one aspect but not another — that is why wajh al-dalil matters.

In jadal, after the respondent presents a proof, the objector may challenge not the proof itself but its relevance to the ruling. This is the question about the aspect of the proof (wajh al-dalil). The passage explains when such a demand is legitimate and why it is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The respondent cites a verse or hadith as proof, but its relevance to the legal ruling is not immediately clear.
2
Recognize the objector's right
The objector may demand clarification of the specific aspect (wajh) of the proof that indicates the ruling.
This demand is called al-mutalaba.
3
Understand the rationale
A single proof can be approached from multiple angles: one aspect may necessitate the ruling, another may not.
The objector only needs to demand clarification if the aspect presented does not clearly necessitate the ruling.
4
Determine when it is obligatory
The question is obligatory (wajib) when the proof is ambiguous and its aspect is unclear.
If the proof is clear, the demand is not needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثا : السؤال عن وجه الدليل والجواب عنه : وذلك أن يستدل المسئول بآية أو خبر فلا يتبين المسئول دليله منه، فيطالبه ببيان وجه الدليل وإنما يحتاج السائل إلى المطالبة بوجه الدلالة إذا كان المجيب قد ذكر دليله من وجه لا يقتضي الحكم، إذ كان الشيء الواحد قد يشار إليه من جهتين : إحداهما تقتضي الحكم والأخرى لا تقتضيه فإن كان الدليل الذي استدل به المسئول غامضا يحتاج إلى بيان وجب السؤال
Third: The question about the aspect of the proof and the answer to it. That is, the respondent argues with a verse or a hadith, but the respondent's proof is not evident from it, so the objector demands clarification of the aspect of the proof. The objector only needs to demand the aspect of indication if the answerer has mentioned his proof from an aspect that does not necessitate the ruling, since a single thing may be pointed to from two aspects: one necessitates the ruling and the other does not. So if the proof that the respondent argued with is ambiguous and needs clarification, the question is obligatory.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical explanation of a specific type of objection in jadal: the question about the aspect of the proof (wajh al-dalil). The author states that when the respondent cites a verse or hadith, but the proof's relevance to the ruling is not clear, the objector may demand clarification of how the proof indicates the ruling. The need for this demand arises when the answerer's proof is presented from an aspect that does not clearly necessitate the legal ruling. The author explains that a single proof text can be approached from multiple angles: one angle may support the ruling, while another does not. Therefore, if the respondent's proof is ambiguous, the objector has the right to ask for the specific aspect that indicates the ruling. The passage ends by affirming that such a question is obligatory (wajib) when clarification is needed.

Key Terms
وجه الدليل
المطالبة

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the objector can demand clarification of how a proof supports the ruling when the proof is ambiguous, because a single proof may have multiple aspects.

When to Ask for the Aspect of Evidence
Author explains when to ask for wajh al-dalil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when it is permissible to demand clarification of wajh al-dalil in a dialectical exchange, and identify the correct response.

Core Idea
Not every challenge to a proof is valid; demanding wajh al-dalil is only permitted when the evidence is ambiguous.

In disputation, a common tactic is to ask 'What is the aspect of your evidence?' But is this always legitimate? The authors of jadal works clarify that such a demand is only valid when the connection between the proof and the ruling is not obvious. This lesson dissects the rule and its application through a Maliki example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The respondent presents a proof (e.g., a verse or hadith). The questioner does not see how it supports the claim. He may ask for the aspect (wajh) of the evidence. But is he always allowed to?
2
Distinguish ambiguous vs. clear evidence
The passage says: if the evidence is ambiguous (ghamid) and needs clarification, the question is obligatory. If the evidence is clear (zahir jali), the question is impermissible and counts as obstinacy or ignorance.
Ambiguity here means the connection between proof and ruling is not directly apparent from the wording or meaning.
3
Analyze the Maliki example
The Malikis argue that in hawala, if the debtor dies insolvent, the assignee can revert to the assignor. They cite the hadith: 'Delay of the wealthy is injustice...' The connection is not obvious. The respondent clarifies: the condition of solvency implies that the assignee's right is protected, so if lost, he can recover.
The question about wajh was good because the evidence was ambiguous.
4
Apply the rule to a new case
Given a proof and claim, decide whether the demand for wajh is permissible. Use the criteria: is the connection clear from the text or meaning?
Practice with Islamic examples from Fiqh or Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثا : السؤال عن وجه الدليل والجواب عنه : وذلك أن يستدل المسئول بآية أو خبر فلا يتبين للمسئول دليله منه، فيطالبه ببيان وجه الدليل وإنما يحتاج السائل إلى المطالبة بوجه الدلالة إذا كان المجيب قد ذكر دليله من وجه لا يقتضي الحكم، إذ كان الشيء الواحد قد يشار إليه من جهتين : إحداهما تقتضي الحكم والأخرى لا تقتضيه فإن كان الدليل الذي استدل به المسئول غامضا يحتاج إلى بيان وجب السؤال عنه، فإن تجاوزه إلى غيره كان مخطئًا، لأنه لا يجوز تسليمه إلا بعد أن ينكشف وجه الدليل منه من جهة المسئول على ما سأل عنه، وذلك نحو استدلال المالكي على أن المحال يرجع على المحيل بموت المحال عليه مفلسا، بقوله : 'مطل الغني ظلم، وإذا أحيل أحدكم على مليء فليتبع'، فيقال له : ما وجه الدليل من هذا الخبر؟ فيبين المستدل ذلك، فتحسن المطالبة بالبيان في مثل هذا، لأن ظاهر اللفظ لا ينبئ عن الدليل حتى يكشف عنه بأن يقول: 'شرط الملاءة في الحوالة'، ومعلوم أنه إنما شرط لئلا يتلف مال المحال، ولو كان إذا تعذر من جهة المحال عليه ثبت له الرجوع لم يكن لشرط الملاءة معنى لأنه حقه لا يتلف سواء كانت الحوالة على مليء أو غيره وإن كان وجه الدليل ظاهرًا جليّا، لم يجز هذا السؤال، وكان السائل عنه متعنتا أو جاهلا. وفي ذلك يقول إمام الحرمين رحمه الله : ومتى ظهر وجه تعلق الدليل بالمدلول من غير حاجة إلى تكليف في الكشف والإظهار، بأن يكون لفظا بينا ظهر في الإيجاب أو الإسقاط بنفسه، أو كان معنى ظاهرًا غير خفي ولا غامض، لم يكن للمعترض المطالبة ببيان وجه تعلقه بالحكم فإن فعل كان مخطئًا إذا أشار إلى وجه ظهوره.
Third: The question about the aspect of evidence and the answer to it. That is that the opponent (al-mas'ul) adduces a verse or report, but the evidence from it is not clear to the questioner (al-sa'il), so he demands clarification of the aspect of the evidence. The questioner only needs to demand the aspect of indication if the respondent has mentioned his evidence in a way that does not necessitate the ruling, since one thing may be pointed to from two aspects: one necessitates the ruling and the other does not. If the evidence with which the opponent has argued is ambiguous and needs clarification, then the question is obligatory. If he moves beyond it to something else, he is mistaken, because it is not permissible to accept it until the aspect of evidence from it becomes clear from the opponent's side regarding what he asked about. An example of that is the Maliki's argument that the assignee (al-muhal) may revert to the assignor (al-muhil) if the assignee dies insolvent, using his statement: 'Delay of the wealthy is injustice, and if one of you is assigned to a wealthy person, he should follow.' So it is said to him: What is the aspect of evidence from this report? Then the arguer clarifies it, and demanding clarification in such a case is good, because the apparent wording does not indicate the evidence until he reveals it by saying: 'The condition of solvency in the assignment (hawala) is stipulated.' And it is known that it is only stipulated so that the property of the assignee is not lost. If it were that when it becomes impossible from the side of the assignee he could revert, then stipulating solvency would have no meaning, because his right is not lost whether the assignment is to a wealthy person or other. If the aspect of evidence is clear and obvious, this question is not permissible, and the questioner would be either obstinate or ignorant. Concerning that, Imam al-Haramayn, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Whenever the aspect of connection of evidence to the evidenced appears without need for effort in uncovering and manifesting, such as a clear wording that appears in obligation or cancellation by itself, or a meaning that is apparent, not hidden or ambiguous, then the objector has no right to demand clarification of the aspect of its connection to the ruling. If he does so, he is mistaken if he points to the aspect of its appearance.'
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage discusses the third type of question in dialectical argumentation (jadal), which is the question about the aspect (wajh) of the evidence. The author first defines it: when the respondent (al-mas'ul) presents a proof (an ayah or hadith) but the questioner (al-sa'il) does not see how it supports the claim, he may ask for clarification of the aspect connecting the proof to the ruling. This is only permissible when the evidence is ambiguous in that its connection to the ruling is not directly apparent. The author explains that a single text might be understood from two angles: one supporting the ruling and the other not. If the evidence is ambiguous, it is obligatory to ask for clarification; failing to do so and moving to a different objection is an error. The author then provides a concrete example from Maliki fiqh regarding the assignment of debt (hawala). The Malikis argue that if the debtor (muhal 'alayh) dies insolvent, the assignee (muhal) may return to the assignor (muhil). They cite the hadith: 'Delay of the wealthy is injustice, and if one of you is assigned to a wealthy person, he should follow.' The apparent wording does not directly indicate the ruling; the arguer clarifies by stating that the condition of solvency is stipulated to prevent loss of the assignee's property. If the assignee could always recover from the assignor upon the debtor's insolvency, then stipulating solvency would be pointless. Thus, the hadith implies that the assignee's right is not lost, allowing recourse. The author then contrasts this with clear evidence: if the connection is obvious (e.g., a clear explicit text or an evident meaning), then asking for the aspect is not allowed and would be considered obstinacy or ignorance. The passage concludes with a quote from Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni reiterating that when the connection is clear without needing elaboration, the objector cannot demand such clarification; if he does, he is mistaken. This passage teaches the etiquette (adab) of debate: questions must target genuine ambiguity, not merely challenge clear proofs.

Key Terms
وجه الدليل (wajh al-dalil): The aspect or angle from which the evidence (text or meaning) indicates the ruling; the specific way in which the proof points to the legal judgment. [Note: In later usul works, 'wajh' often refers to the specific indication (al-dalala) itself; here it is the perspective of connection.]
الدليل (al-dalil): The proof or evidence, such as a verse or hadith, that is cited to establish a legal ruling. [Note: Standard term across usul; no significant shift.]
المسئول (al-mas'ul): The respondent in a dialectical exchange; the one who presents the evidence and answers objections. [Note: In jadal literature, al-mas'ul is the defender of the thesis; in later adab al-bahth, it may be called al-mujib or al-mudda'i.]
السائل (al-sa'il): The questioner or objector in a dialectical exchange; the one who challenges the evidence and demands clarification. [Note: Same as above; in later texts, al-sa'il may be called al-mu'tarid.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it good to demand clarification in the Maliki example?
A) Because the text is unclear
B) Because the text is clear
C) Because the hadith is weak
D) Because the opponent is ignorant

Key Takeaway
Asking for the aspect of evidence is only legitimate when the evidence is ambiguous; when clear, such a question is obstructive.

A respondent cites the verse 'Allah has permitted trade and forbidden usury' (Quran 2:275) to prove that a specific transaction is permissible. The questioner asks: 'What is the wajh al-dalil?' Is this permissible? Explain using the criteria from the passage.
?

Understanding I'tirad: Objection as a Roadblock in Debate
Author defines i'tirad linguistically as prevention and gives its origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define i'tirad in its linguistic sense and explain how its metaphorical meaning applies to dialectical objections.

Core Idea
An objection in debate is a roadblock—it prevents the opponent's argument from proceeding.

In the study of jadal (dialectics), the term 'i'tirad' is central. Before diving into its technical use, the author grounds us in its linguistic root: al-man' (prevention). Just as a building blocking a road prevents travelers from passing, an objection in debate blocks the opponent's argument from reaching its conclusion. This lesson unpacks that metaphor, giving you a clear, intuitive grasp of what an objection truly is in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'i'tirad' in the Arabic passage. Notice that the author first gives its linguistic meaning before applying it to dialectic.
2
Extract the core linguistic meaning
The author states: 'والاعتراض في اللغة المنع' — i'tirad in language is prevention (al-man'). Commit this definition to memory; it is the foundation.
3
Examine the origin/metaphor
The author explains the origin: if a building or something obstructs a road, it prevents travelers (sabila) from traversing it. This concrete image clarifies why 'i'tirad' carries the sense of blocking.
4
Apply the metaphor to dialectic
In a debate, an objection functions exactly like that obstacle: it prevents the opponent's argument from proceeding smoothly to its conclusion. The objector 'blocks' the path of reasoning.
Contrast this with mere criticism, which may not halt the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعا : السؤال على وجه الاعتراض والقدح والجواب عنه : والاعتراض في اللغة المنع ، والأصل فيه أن الطريق إذا اعترض فيه بناء أو غيره منع السابلة من سلوكه
Fourth: The question in the form of objection and refutation, and the answer to it. And i'tirad (objection) in language is prevention. Its origin is that if a building or something else obstructs a road, it prevents travelers from traversing it.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This short passage opens the discussion of the fourth type of question in jadal: the question that is an objection (i'tirad) and refutation (qadh). The author first gives the linguistic meaning of i'tirad: it is 'prevention' (al-man'). He then explains the origin of this meaning: if a building or something blocks a road, it prevents travelers from using that road. The implication is that in debate, an objection is something that blocks the opponent's argument, preventing it from reaching its conclusion. This sets the stage for a more detailed treatment of how objections are raised and answered, which likely continues in subsequent pages.

Key Terms
الاعتراض (al-i'tirad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Objection
B) Refutation
C) Prevention
D) Argument

Key Takeaway
I'tirad literally means prevention; in dialectic, it is a challenge that blocks the opponent's argument from proceeding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When to Demand the Face of Evidence in Jadal
Author explains when demanding clarification of evidence is valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a demand for clarification of the evidence (wajh al-dalil) is valid based on the clarity of the evidence in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Ask for evidence clarification only when the evidence is obscure, not when it is clear.

In jadal, the questioner may request the respondent to explain how the evidence supports the claim. But this is not always allowed. This passage from a classical jadal text explains the rule: only when the evidence's connection to the ruling is not apparent. We will analyze the Maliki example and the distinction between clear and obscure evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage begins by stating that if a respondent goes beyond the question, he is mistaken. Then it introduces the main topic: when it is valid to ask for the face of the evidence. Focus on the condition for asking.
The respondent's error is not the main point; the rule about asking is central.
2
Extract the rule
The rule: demanding clarification is commendable only when the apparent wording does not indicate the evidence. The passage says: 'لا يجوز تسليمه إلا بعد أن ينكشف وجه الدليل' and later 'تحسن المطالبة بالبيان في مثل هذا'.
3
Apply the rule to the Maliki example
The Maliki uses the hadith 'مطل الغنى ظلم...' to infer that the assignee can reclaim from the assignor if the assignee dies insolvent. The questioner asks for the face of the evidence because the hadith does not explicitly mention this case. The respondent explains that the condition of solvency implies it. This question is valid because the evidence is obscure.
The example illustrates an obscure face of evidence.
4
Recognize the exception for clear evidence
If the face of the evidence is apparent—whether through clear wording or obvious meaning—the question is not permissible. The questioner in that case is either obstinate or ignorant. Imam al-Haramayn's statement reinforces this: no need for clarification when the connection is obvious.
The exception prevents unnecessary questioning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنه، فإن تجاوزه إلى غيره كان مخطئًا، لأنه لا يجوز تسليمه إلا بعد أن ينكشف وجه الدليل منه من جهة المسئول على ما سأل عنه، وذلك نحو استدلال المالكي على أن المُحال يرجع على المحيل بموت المحال عليه مفلسا، بقوله : (مطل الغنى ظلم، وإذا أحيل أحدكم على مليء فليتبع)، فيقال له : ما وجه الدليل من هذا الخبر؟ فيبين المستدل ذلك، فتحسن المطالبة بالبيان في مثل هذا، لأن ظاهر اللفظ لا ينبئ عن الدليل حتى يكشف عنه بأن يقول: «شرط الملاءة في الحوالة»، ومعلوم أنه إنما شرط لئلا يتلف مال المحال، ولو كان إذا تعذر من جهة المحال عليه ثبت له الرجوع لم يكن لشرط الملاءة معنى لأنه حقه لا يتلف سواء كانت الحوالة على مليء أو غيره. وإن كان وجه الدليل ظاهرًا جليّا ، لم يجز هذا السؤال، وكان السائل عنه متعنتا أو جاهلا. وفي ذلك يقول إمام الحرمين رحمه الله : ومتى ظهر وجه تعلق الدليل بالمدلول من غير حاجة إلى تكليف في الكشف والإظهار، بأن يكون لفظا بينا ظهر في الإيجاب أو الإسقاط بنفسه، أو كان معنى ظاهرًا غير خفي ولا غامض، لم يكن للمعترض المطالبة ببيان وجه تعلقه بالحكم فإن فعل كان مخطئًا إذا أشار إلى وجه ظهوره.
From it, if he goes beyond it to something else, he is mistaken, because it is not permissible to accept it except after the face of the evidence from it is revealed from the side of the respondent regarding what he asked about. That is like the Maliki's inference that the assignee (al-muhal) has recourse against the assignor (al-muhil) upon the death of the assignee (al-muhal 'alayh) bankrupt, by his saying: 'Delay by the rich is injustice, and if one of you is assigned to a wealthy person, let him follow.' It is said to him: What is the face of the evidence from this report? Then the inferer explains that, so demanding clarification is commendable in such a case, because the apparent wording does not indicate the evidence until he uncovers it by saying: 'The condition of solvency in the assignment (hawala)' and it is known that it was only stipulated so that the property of the assignee is not lost. If it were the case that when it becomes impossible from the side of the assignee he has recourse, then the condition of solvency would have no meaning because his right is not lost whether the assignment is to a solvent person or someone else. And if the face of the evidence is apparent and clear, this question is not permissible, and the questioner about it is either obstinate or ignorant. Regarding this, Imam al-Haramayn may Allah have mercy on him said: Whenever the face of the evidence's connection to the indicated is apparent without need for effort in disclosure and manifestation, whether it is a clear wording that appears by itself in obligation or cancellation, or a meaning that is apparent, not hidden nor obscure, then it is not for the objector to demand clarification of the face of its connection to the ruling; if he does so, he is mistaken when he points to its apparent face.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage discusses the protocol of demanding clarification of the evidence (وجه الدليل) in dialectical debate (jadal). The author states that if a respondent goes beyond the question, he is mistaken. The questioner may only ask for the face of the evidence when the evidence is not apparent from the text. He gives an example from Maliki usul: the hadith 'مطل الغنى ظلم' is used to argue that the assignee can reclaim from the assignor if the assignee dies insolvent. The questioner asks for the face of the evidence, since the hadith does not explicitly mention the case. The inferer explains that the condition of solvency (ملاءة) in assignment implies that if the assignee is insolvent, the assignor remains liable. If the face of the evidence is clear, asking for clarification is either obstinacy (عنت) or ignorance (جهل). Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni reinforces this: if the connection between evidence and ruling is obvious—whether by clear wording or apparent meaning—the objector may not demand clarification.

المطالبة ببيان

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the legitimacy of demanding clarification of evidence hinges on whether the evidence's connection to the ruling is apparent; if it is, the demand is not permissible.

Understanding Sual al-I'tirad in Jadal
Defines the term 'sual al-i'tirad' and its purpose in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three targets of objection questioning (da'wa, dalil, manner of inference) and explain how they are attacked.

Core Idea
Objection questioning is the engine of jadal: it forces the respondent to defend every part of his argument.

In the discipline of jadal, precision in terminology is essential. This lesson unpacks the definition of sual al-i'tirad (objection questioning), the primary tool of the questioner. You will learn exactly what parts of an argument are vulnerable to objection and how the questioner's role is to expose contradictions and flaws. This foundational concept governs all subsequent debate moves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition's genus
The passage begins with 'والمراد به في الاصطلاح' – it is defining a term. Recognize that this is a technical definition within jadal. The term being defined is 'sual al-i'tirad'.
2
Extract the agent and target
The definition states that the questioner (السائل) directs objections to the respondent (المسئول). Note the roles: the questioner is the agent, the respondent is the target of the objection.
3
Identify the three possible targets
The passage lists three things that can be objected to: the claim (دعوى), the evidence (دليل), and the manner of inference (وجه الاستدلال). Write down each and note that they represent the entire argument structure.
4
Understand the purpose
The purpose is to expose contradictions (تناقض) or defects (اختلال). The questioner works to 'extract the contradiction' or 'show the defect' in the subject or evidence.
5
Recognize the alternative name
Ibn 'Aqil and Ibn al-Najjar call it 'sual al-ilzam' (question of obligation), emphasizing that the questioner forces the respondent to address inconsistencies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد به في الاصطلاح أن يتوجه السائل إلى دعوى المسئول أو دليله باعتراضات تقدح في الدعوى أو الدليل أو وجه الاستدلال من الدليل . فسؤال الاعتراض إنما يكون مسلطا على الموضوع ذاته أو على الأدلة، أي العمل على إخراج التناقض الكائن في الموضوع إن وجد، أو على بيان الخلل في الأدلة إن كان هناك اختلال وقد سمى كل من ابن عقيل الحنبلي وابن النجار الفتوحي، سؤال الاعتراض، بسؤال الإلزام، أي أن السائل يلزم المسئول بما يتطرق إلى دعواه أو دليله أو وجه استدلاله من اعتراضات أو تناقضات ذاتية.
And what is meant by it in terminology is that the questioner directs to the respondent's claim or his evidence with objections that impair the claim, the evidence, or the manner of inference from the evidence. So the objection questioning is only directed at the subject itself or at the evidences, meaning the work of extracting the contradiction existing in the subject if it exists, or to show the defect in the evidences if there is an imbalance. And both Ibn 'Aqil al-Hanbali and Ibn al-Najjar al-Futuhi named the objection questioning as 'the question of obligation', meaning that the questioner obliges the respondent with what pertains to his claim, his evidence, or his manner of inference of objections or intrinsic contradictions.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage defines the key concept of 'sual al-i'tirad' (objection questioning) in the discipline of jadal (dialectic). It begins by specifying the target of the objection: it may be directed against the respondent's claim (da'wa), the evidence (dalil), or the manner of drawing inference from that evidence. The purpose is to expose contradictions within the subject itself or to highlight flaws in the evidence. The passage notes that two scholars, Ibn 'Aqil and Ibn al-Najjar, refer to this as 'sual al-ilzam' (question of obligation), emphasizing that the questioner compels the respondent to address objections or contradictions that arise from his own position. This passage establishes the fundamental framework of objection in jadal.

Key Terms
سؤال الاعتراض
السائل
المسئول
دعوى
دليل
سؤال الإلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To request a definition of terms
B) To expose contradictions in the subject or flaws in the evidence
C) To ask for a repetition of the argument
D) To express agreement with the respondent

Key Takeaway
Sual al-i'tirad is the deliberate act of directing objections to the respondent's claim, evidence, or inference to expose contradictions or defects, and it is also called sual al-ilzam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the following excerpt from a classical jadal text: 'Al-mu'tarid qala: innana la nusallim anna al-hukm fi al-asl muthbitun li al-'illah.' Identify the target of the objection: is it the da'wa, the dalil, or the manner of inference? Explain your reasoning.
?
Using the definition from this lesson, write a short paragraph describing the role of the questioner in a jadal session, including the three possible targets of his objections.
