Istifsaar: Definition and Conditions
Defines istifsaar and conditions for its use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an istifsaar question is appropriate in a given jadal exchange by checking the conditions of ambiguity or obscurity.

Core Idea
Istifsaar clarifies ambiguity but is only proper when the term is truly ambiguous.

In classical jadal, not every question is an objection. The first type of objection questioning is istifsaar, a request for clarification. But this tool has strict conditions: you cannot ask about what is clear. In this lesson, we examine what istifsaar is and when it is justified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of istifsaar
Read the definition: 'a request for explanation of the meaning of a wording if it is obscure or ambiguous'. Note the particles used (هل, الهمزة).
2
Understand the purpose
The goal is to clarify the point of dispute (تحرير محل النزاع). Why is this important before any other objection?
3
Check the conditions for its use
The passage says it is only proper if the wording is ambiguous (مجملاً) between equal meanings or obscure (غريبا) to the listener. If the term is clear, asking is obstinacy or ignorance.
The questioner must also show why it is ambiguous.
4
Apply the condition to an example
Consider a debate where a jurist says 'The sale is fasid (defective)'. Another asks 'What do you mean by fasid? Is it invalid or merely irregular?' Is this istifsaar appropriate? Check if the term 'fasid' is ambiguous in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسؤال الاعتراض له أشكال كثيرة ومتنوعة يرد على كل منها في الجدل جواب خاص به ونذكر منها على سبيل المثال لا الحصر أ - الاستفسار : وهو أول ما يبدأ به من الاعتراضات إذا كان هناك ما يدعو إليه، ويراد به «طلب شرح معنى اللفظ إذا كان غريبا أو مجملا، ويقع ب(هل) وبالهمزة ونحوها مما يسأل به عن التصور، فيستفسر عن صورة المسألة ومعنى المقدمات» ذلك تحرير محل النزاع والكشف عن موطن الخلاف وإدراك كل منهما لمذهب الآخر . والغرض من وسؤال الاستفسار لا يُستساغ التوجه به دائمًا، إذ أنه إنما يحسن إذا كان اللفظ مجملا مترددًا بين محامل على السوية، أو غريبا لا يعرفه السامع المخاطب، فعلى السائل بيان كونه مجملاً أو غريبًا، لأن الاستفسار عن الواضح عناد أو جهل وقد نازع الإمام الزركشي في اعتبار هذا السؤال مما يدخل تحت الاعتراض، فقال: واعلم أن في عد هذا من الاعتراضات نظرا لأنه طليعة جيشها وليس من أقسامها، إذ الاعتراض عبارة عما يخدش به كلام المستدل، والاستفسار ليس من هذا القبيل، بل هو يعرف المراد ويبين له ليتوجه عليه السؤال، فإذا هو طليعة السؤال فليس بسؤال لكن الطوفي اعتبر أنه يدخل في الاعتراضات، لأنه من قبيل المنع، لأن المعترض منع أن لفظ المستدل مفيد أو جائز الإيراد في المناظرة. والجواب عن سؤال الاستفسار ، أو جواب المستدل أو المعترض على سؤال الاستفسار أحد وجهين: أحدهما : منع تعدد احتمالات اللفظ إن أمكن بأن يقول : لا نسلم أن لهذا اللفظ إلا محمل واحد ويبين ذلك عن أئمة اللغة أو بأنا اتفقنا على أن اللفظ يطلق على هذا المعنى الواحد والأصل عدم جواز إطلاقه على غيره نفيا للمجاز والاشتراك، فمن ادعى إطلاقه عليه، فعليه الدليل . الوجه الثاني : أن يبين رجحان اللفظ في أحد المجملين، بأمر ما من الأمور المرجحة، إما بالنقل عن أهل اللغة، أو باشتهاره في عرفهم . . . وحينئذ لا يكون مجملا، وإن أمكنه أن يبين صدق اللفظ على المجملين بالتواطؤ، والمراد القدر المشترك بينهما نفيًا للمجاز والاشتراك، كفاه أيضا.
And objection questioning has many and varied forms, each of which has a specific answer in jadal. We mention from them by way of example, not limitation: a - Al-Istifsaar (inquiry): It is the first of the objections to begin with if there is something calling for it. It is intended as 'a request for explanation of the meaning of a wording if it is obscure or ambiguous, and it occurs with 'hal' (هل) and with the hamza and the like of what is asked about conception, so one inquires about the form of the issue and the meaning of the premises.' That is to clarify the point of dispute and reveal the locus of disagreement and for each of them to understand the other's school. And the purpose of the inquiry question is not always acceptable to use, because it is only proper if the wording is ambiguous, fluctuating between equal meanings, or obscure that the addressee does not know. So the questioner must show that it is ambiguous or obscure, because inquiry about what is clear is obstinacy or ignorance. Imam al-Zarkashi disputed considering this question as falling under objection, saying: 'Know that counting this among objections is questionable because it is the vanguard of the army and not one of its parts. Indeed, objection is an expression of what scratches the speech of the one providing evidence, and inquiry is not of this sort. Rather, it defines what is meant and clarifies it so that the question can then be directed; so it is the vanguard of the question, not the question itself.' But al-Tufi considered that it does enter into objections because it is a form of prevention, as the objector prevents that the wording of the evidence-giver is beneficial or permissible to present in the debate. The answer to the inquiry question, or the answer of the evidence-giver or the objector to the inquiry question, has two ways: One: to prevent the multiplicity of probabilities of the wording if possible, by saying: 'We do not accept that this wording has more than one meaning' and show that from the imams of language, or that we have agreed that the wording applies to this one meaning, and the default is that it is not permissible to apply it to other than that, negating metaphor and homonymy. Whoever claims its application to [another meaning] must provide evidence. The second way: to show the preponderance of the wording in one of the two ambiguous meanings, by some preponderating factor, either by transmission from the language scholars, or by its fame in their custom. Then it is not ambiguous. And if he can show the applicability of the wording to both ambiguous meanings by equivocation (tawāṭu'), intending the common meaning between them, negating metaphor and homonymy, that also suffices him.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage introduces 'al-istifsaar' (inquiry) as the first type of objection questioning. It defines istifsaar as a request for clarification of a term that is obscure or ambiguous, using interrogative particles like 'hal' (هل) or the hamza. The goal is to precisely define the point of dispute. However, such inquiry is only appropriate when the term is genuinely ambiguous or obscure; asking about something clear is considered stubbornness. A significant debate is then presented: Imam al-Zarkashi argues that istifsaar is not actually an objection because it does not directly attack the argument but merely clarifies, serving as a 'vanguard' for later objections. In contrast, al-Tufi classifies it as an objection because it involves a form of 'man' (prevention): the questioner prevents the respondent from using the term as intended without clarification. The passage then details two ways to respond to an istifsaar question: first, by denying that the term has multiple meanings (claiming it is univocal), providing evidence from language authorities or agreed usage. Second, by showing that one meaning is preponderant due to contextual factors, or by demonstrating that the term applies to both meanings in a common core sense (tawāṭu') without metaphor or homonymy. This lesson illustrates the nuanced categorization of objections in jadal and the procedures for handling ambiguity.

Key Terms
الاستفسار
المجمل
الغريب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is an istifsaar question not justified?
A) When the term is ambiguous
B) When the term is obscure
C) When the term is clear
D) When the term is homonymous

Key Takeaway
Istifsaar is a tool for clarifying ambiguous or obscure terms, but it is only admissible when the term genuinely needs explanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a debate about the term 'istihsan' in usul al-fiqh. A debater says: 'The ruling is based on istihsan.' Is it appropriate to use istifsaar to ask 'What do you mean by istihsan?' Explain using the conditions from this lesson.

Is Istifsaar an Objection? The Debate Between al-Zarkashi and al-Tufi
Presents debate on whether istifsaar is an objection and response strategies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the arguments of al-Zarkashi and al-Tufi regarding istifsaar and evaluate which position is stronger based on the criteria of jadal.

Core Idea
Al-Zarkashi sees istifsaar as the vanguard of objection, not the objection itself; al-Tufi sees it as prevention.

After understanding what istifsaar is, we face a deeper question: Is it actually an objection? Two prominent scholars disagree. Al-Zarkashi argues that istifsaar clarifies rather than attacks, serving as a prelude. Al-Tufi counters that it is a form of prevention (man'), which is a recognized objection. In this lesson, we dissect this debate and the responses it generates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read al-Zarkashi's view: istifsaar is not an objection because objection 'scratches' the argument, while istifsaar only clarifies. Then read al-Tufi's view: istifsaar is a form of prevention (man'), which is an objection.
Note the key term 'man'.
2
Analyze the reasoning
Consider why al-Zarkashi calls istifsaar a 'vanguard' (طليعة). What does that imply? Al-Tufi calls it prevention: by asking for clarification, the objector prevents the opponent from proceeding with an ambiguous term.
3
Examine the two ways to answer an istifsaar
The passage gives two strategies: (1) deny multiplicity of meanings (claim univocity), (2) show preponderance of one meaning or tawāṭu'. How do these responses relate to the debate?
These are part of the jadal procedure.
4
Apply the debate to a contemporary example
In a modern fiqh discussion, a speaker uses the term 'riba' without specifying whether it includes credit card interest. An interlocutor asks for clarification. Is this istifsaar an objection? Argue from both al-Zarkashi and al-Tufi's perspectives.

Understanding Al-Man': The Premise-Rejection Objection
Defines al-man' as an objection that rejects premises or the relation of the ruling to the description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four possible targets of a man' objection and use this framework to analyze dialectical arguments.

Core Idea
Man' is the art of rejecting a premise, not the whole argument.

In dialectical debates, objections have precise forms. Al-man' is one such form where the questioner prevents the argument by rejecting a specific component. This lesson will help you parse the four targets of man' as defined in the classical jadal tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action described
Read the definition: man' is 'the manifestation of the claim of opposition.' It is the questioner's refusal to accept all or some of the premises of the evidence. Note that it is not a counter-argument but a rejection.
2
List the four targets
From the passage, extract the four aspects that can be rejected: (1) the description (waṣf) itself, (2) the suitability of the description for the ruling to depend on it, (3) the ruling (ḥukm) itself, (4) the relation of the ruling to the description.
These are not mutually exclusive; a man' can target more than one.
3
Understand each target
For each target, think of a concrete example from fiqh: e.g., rejecting that 'intoxication' exists in the case, or that intoxication is a valid cause for prohibition, or that the ruling of prohibition is correct, or that the ruling actually follows from the description.
The 'description' is the cause ('illa) in legal analogy.
4
Distinguish from other objections
Compare man' with mu'arada (counter-evidence). Man' denies a premise; mu'arada provides an alternative proof. Practice identifying which type of objection is being used in a short debate snippet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسمى المنع أو الممانعة، وهو إظهار دعوى المخالفة. هو عدم قبول السائل مقدمات الدليل كلها أو بعضها، سواء كان في نفس الوصف، أو في صلاحية الوصف لترتب الحكم عليه، أو في نفس الحكم، أو نسبة الحكم إلى الوصف.
And it is called al-man' (prevention) or al-mumāna'a (mutual prevention), and it is the manifestation of the claim of opposition. It is that the questioner does not accept the premises of the evidence, all or some of them, whether it is in the description itself, or in the suitability of the description for the ruling to depend on it, or in the ruling itself, or in the relation of the ruling to the description.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-man'' (prevention) as a second type of objection in jadal. It is also called 'mumāna'a' and is described as expressing a claim of opposition (mukhālafa). Specifically, it is the questioner's refusal to accept all or some of the premises of the respondent's evidence. The rejection can target four aspects: the description (waṣf) itself, the suitability of that description to carry the ruling (i.e., the 'illa or cause), the ruling itself, or the relation between the ruling and the description. This provides a clear categorization of how a questioner can challenge the validity of an argument by denying its components.

Key Terms
المنع (al-manʿ)
الممانعة (al-mumānaʿa)
الوصف (al-waṣf)
الحكم (al-ḥukm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the possible targets of a man' objection?
A) Only the description and the ruling
B) Only the premises that are explicit
C) The description, its suitability for the ruling, the ruling itself, or the relation between ruling and description
D) The entire argument, not individual parts

Key Takeaway
Al-man' is a specific objection that rejects premises by targeting the description, its suitability, the ruling, or their relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Istifsar and Man' as Objections in Jadal
Author defines istifsar and man' as types of objections in dialectical debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish istifsar (request for clarification) from man' (denial) in a jadal debate and identify the correct basis (sanad) for a valid man'.

Core Idea
Istifsar is the vanguard of objections, not an objection itself; man' requires a basis.

In dialectical debate, not every response is a direct objection. This lesson clarifies two key tools: istifsar, which seeks clarification before objecting, and man', the actual denial of premises. Understanding the difference helps you engage in structured jadal. The passage also illustrates man' with a real usul al-fiqh debate between Dawud al-Zahiri and al-Baji.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two tools
Read the first part of the passage to find definitions of istifsar and man'. Note how the author distinguishes istifsar as a 'vanguard' rather than a direct objection.
Al-Tufi's alternative view is mentioned: consider istifsar as a type of denial.
2
Analyze the responses to istifsar
The author gives two ways to answer an istifsar: either deny multiple meanings or show preponderance. Understand each method and how they shift the burden of proof.
3
Examine the definition and basis of man'
Man' is the non-acceptance of premises. It must have a sanad (basis). Look at the formulaic examples given: 'We do not accept... because...'
Sanad can be based on reality or the questioner's assumption.
4
Apply to the example
Read the example of Dawud al-Zahiri and al-Baji. Identify the premise Dawud uses (verse of permission) and how al-Baji denies its validity. Determine the sanad for al-Baji's man'.
Al-Baji's basis: the verse is just one general among others and cannot be given priority in conflict.
5
Practice distinguishing istifsar and man'
Consider a hypothetical jadal scenario: one speaker says 'The Qur'an is created.' The opponent responds: 'What do you mean by created?' Is this istifsar or man'? Then the opponent says: 'I deny that the Qur'an is created because it is Allah's speech and His speech is uncreated.' Identify each move.
The first response is istifsar (seeking clarification), the second is man' (denial with a basis).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن في عد هذا من الاعتراضات نظرا لأنه طليعة جيشها وليس من أقسامها، إذ الاعتراض عبارة عما يخدش به كلام المستدل، والاستفسار ليس من هذا القبيل، بل هو يعرف المراد ويبين له ليتوجه عليه السؤال، فإذا هو طليعة السؤال فليس بسؤال لكن الطوفي اعتبر أنه يدخل في الاعتراضات، لأنه من قبيل المنع، لأن المعترض منع أن لفظ المستدل مفيد أو جائز الإيراد في المناظرة. والجواب عن سؤال الاستفسار أحد وجهين: أحدهما: منع تعدد احتمالات اللفظ إن أمكن بأن يقول: لا نسلم أن لهذا اللفظ إلا محمل واحد ويبين ذلك عن أئمة اللغة أو بأنا اتفقنا على أن اللفظ يطلق على هذا المعنى الواحد والأصل عدم جواز إطلاقه على غيره نفيا للمجاز والاشتراك، فمن ادعى إطلاقه عليه فعليه الدليل. الوجه الثاني: أن يبين رجحان اللفظ في أحد المجملين بأمر ما من الأمور المرجحة، إما بالنقل عن أهل اللغة، أو باشتهاره في عرفهم. وحينئذ لا يكون مجملا، وإن أمكنه أن يبين صدق اللفظ على المجملين بالتواطؤ والمراد القدر المشترك بينهما نفيا للمجاز والاشتراك، كفاه أيضا. ويسمى المنع أو الممانعة: وهو إظهار دعوى المخالفة. هو عدم قبول السائل مقدمات الدليل كلها أو بعضها، سواء كان في نفس الوصف، أو في صلاحية الوصف لترتب الحكم عليه، أو في نفس الحكم، أو نسبة الحكم إلى الوصف. والمنع لا بد له من سند، إذ السند عند أهل الميزان، ما يكون المنع مبنيا عليه، أي ما يكون مصححًا لورود المنع في نفس الأمر أو في زعم السائل، كأن يقال: لا نسلم كذا، لما لا يجوز أن يكون كذا، أو لا نسلم لزوم ذلك وإنما يلزم لو كان كذا، أو لا نسلم هذا وكيف يكون هذا أو هذا والحال أنه كذا. مثال ذلك: ما ذهب إليه الإمام داود الظاهري وأكثر أصحابه من أن أصل الأشياء على الإباحة، وأن ذلك ثابت بالشرع واستدلوا بقوله تعالى: خَلَقَ لَكُم مَا فِي الْأَرْضِ جَمِيعًا، وقالوا: إذا ما تعارض خبران ولم يمكن الجمع أو الترجيح بينهما، رجع إلى هذه الإباحة التي شرها الشرع. وقد منع الباجي هذا الاستدلال بهذه الآية، وقال في الاعتراض عليه: وهذا ليس بصحيح، لأن الآية المبيحة عنده هي من جملة العمومات المبيحة، لا فرق بينها وبين غيرها، وليست هي أن تكون أصلا بأولى من سائرها، فإذا عارضتها آية أخرى فلا يخلو أن تكون عامة مثلها أو أخص منها أو أعم منها، فإن كانت عامة مثلها فقد وقع التعارض بينهما فوجب أن يسقطها على أصله، وإن كانت أخص منها وجب عندنا أن يؤخذ بالحاظرة لصحة بناء المبيحة عليها، وإن كانت الحاظرة أعم منها وجب الأخذ بالمبيحة لصحة بناء العامة الحاظرة عليها، وعند داود في هذه الوجوه كلها تسقط الآيتان على نحو ما يفعل في سائر الآيات المتعارضة.
Know that counting this among the objections is questionable because it is the vanguard of the objections, not one of its divisions. For the objection is an expression of what scratches the speech of the arguer, and the request for clarification is not of this kind. Rather, it makes known the intended meaning and clarifies it so that the question may be directed to it. Thus it is the vanguard of the question, not a question. But al-Tufi considered that it falls under objections because it is a type of denial, since the objector denied that the arguer's wording is beneficial or permissible to use in the debate. The answer to the question of clarification is one of two ways: First: denial of multiple possibilities of the word if possible, by saying: We do not accept that this word has only one meaning, and he explains that from the authorities of language, or by saying we agreed that the word applies to this single meaning and the origin is the impermissibility of applying it to something else, denying metaphor and homonymy. Whoever claims its application to something else must provide evidence. The second way: he shows the preponderance of the word in one of the two ambiguous meanings by something from the preponderating factors, either by transmission from the people of language, or by its fame in their custom. Then it is not ambiguous, and if he can show the truth of the word for both ambiguous meanings by equivoque, and the intended meaning is the common factor between them, denying metaphor and homonymy, that also suffices him. And the denial is called opposition: it is manifesting a claim of contradiction. It is the questioner's non-acceptance of all or some of the premises of the proof, whether it is in the description itself, or in the suitability of the description for the ruling to follow, or in the ruling itself, or in the attribution of the ruling to the description. The denial must have a basis, for the basis according to the people of the scale is what the denial is built upon, i.e., what validates the occurrence of the denial in reality or in the opinion of the questioner, such as saying: We do not accept such and such, because it is not permissible that it be such, or we do not accept the necessity of that, but it would be necessary if it were such, or we do not accept this, and how can this be that when the situation is such. Example of that: what Imam Dawud al-Zahiri and most of his followers held, that the origin of things is permissibility, and that is established by the Law, and they used the verse: He created for you all that is on the earth, and they said: If two reports conflict and it is not possible to reconcile or prefer between them, one returns to this permissibility which the Law established. Al-Baji denied this reasoning with this verse, and said in objection to it: This is not correct, because the permissive verse according to him is among the general permissive verses, no difference between it and others, and it is not more worthy to be an origin than others. If another verse conflicts with it, it must be either general like it, or more specific, or more general. If it is general like it, then conflict occurs and he must drop both according to his principle. If it is more specific, then in our view the restrictive must be taken because the permissive is built upon it. If the restrictive is more general, then the permissive must be taken because the general restrictive is built upon it. According to Dawud, in all these cases both verses drop, just as is done with other conflicting verses.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage deals with two key tools in dialectical debate (jadal): istifsar (request for clarification) and man' (denial/opposition). The author begins by addressing a disagreement about whether istifsar is a type of objection. He argues that it is actually a prelude (tali'a) to objections, not one of the objection categories, because an objection scratches or damages the arguer's speech, whereas istifsar merely seeks to clarify the intended meaning. However, al-Tufi counted it as an objection because it involves a kind of denial—the objector denies that the arguer's wording is clear or permissible. The author then explains two possible responses to an istifsar: either deny that the wording has multiple meanings (by specifying one meaning based on language authorities or agreement, shifting the burden of proof to the one claiming otherwise), or show preponderance of one meaning through language customs or transmission. Next, the author defines man' as showing a claim of contradiction, i.e., the questioner rejects all or some premises of the proof. This rejection can target the description, its suitability for the ruling, the ruling itself, or the attribution. Key is that man' must have a basis (sanad)—a reason that justifies the denial either in reality or in the questioner's assumption. Several formulaic examples are given: 'We do not accept such and such because it cannot be...' etc. The author then illustrates man' with a concrete usul al-fiqh example: Dawud al-Zahiri's principle that everything is permissible unless proven otherwise, using the verse 'He created for you all that is on the earth' as a general permissive verse. Al-Baji objects by arguing that this verse is just one among many general verses; when it conflicts with another verse, the same rules of conflict resolution apply—it cannot be given priority. The passage thus teaches how to correctly apply man' with a valid basis, using a real debate from Islamic legal theory.

المنع
السند

C) Dawud's principle is not based on any textual evidence.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a valid denial (man') must be based on a clear ground (sanad), while istifsar is a preliminary step to clarify meaning before objecting.

Naqd: The Dialectical Tool for Denying Generality
Definition of naqd as denial of generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a naqd objection in a jadal exchange and distinguish it from other types of objections such as man' and mu'arada.

Core Idea
Naqd targets the universal scope of a claim, not its truth.

In jadal (dialectical debate), an arguer often supports his position by presenting a universal judgment. But how does an opponent respond? This lesson introduces al-naqd—the specific objection that denies the generality of the arguer's claim. Understanding naqd is crucial for mastering the dynamics of Islamic dialectics, where the scope of a ruling can be as important as its content.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its linguistic origin
The Arabic word naqd literally means 'breaking'. This foreshadows its technical use: breaking a claim's universality.
2
Distinguish the technical definition
Naqd in jadal is 'denial of a claim of generality' (man' da'wa al-'umum). This is not just any denial; it specifically attacks the universal application of the arguer's proof.
3
Understand the dialectical context
The passage describes a scenario: the arguer presents a universal judgment (kulli); the objector responds by denying its universality (man' al-'umum). This shows naqd is a direct response to the scope.
4
Contrast with other objections
Compare naqd with man' (denial of a premise) and mu'arada (counter-argument). Naqd is unique because it focuses on the claim's universality, not its premises or conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - النقض: النقض في اللغة: هو الكسر، وفي الاصطلاح: منع دعوى العموم، وذلك أن المستدل يدعي دعواه أو دليله في صورة حكم كلي، مدعيا أن دعواه عامة بالنسبة إلى ما تثبته أو تنفيه ويكون في عمومها سند لصحتها ومبرر قوي لادعائه التمسك بها، فيتوجه المعترض إلى دعواه بمنع عمومها وعدم اطرادها في
C - Refutation: Refutation in language is breaking; in terminology, it is the denial of a claim of generality. That is, the arguer claims his assertion or his proof in the form of a universal judgment, claiming that his assertion is general with respect to what it affirms or denies, and in its generality there is a basis for its validity and a strong justification for his claim to adhere to it. So the objector directs his objection to his assertion by denying its generality and its lack of universality in
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage introduces the third type of objection: al-naqd (refutation). The author first gives the linguistic meaning: breaking or breaking down. Then the technical definition: it is the denial of a claim of generality (man' da'wa al-'umum). The situation is that the arguer (mustadill) presents his proof as a universal judgment, claiming that it applies generally to all cases that it affirms or negates. This generality provides a basis (sanad) and strong justification for his position. The objector then attacks this claim by denying the generality and asserting that the rule does not apply universally (lack of universal application, 'adam ittirad). This is a distinct form of objection because it specifically targets the scope of the claim, not just a premise or a description. The text cuts off, but the idea is clear: naqd is used to show that the arguer's universal claim does not hold in all cases, thus weakening his proof.

دعوى العموم
اطراد

Key Takeaway
Naqd is a specialized objection in jadal that denies the universality of a claim, forcing the arguer to defend the scope of his proof.

Definition and Example of al-Man' in Jadal
Defines al-man' and its requirement of sanad, with an example from usul al-fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a valid man' (prevention) in a jadal exchange by checking for the presence of a sanad (support).

Core Idea
A valid objection in jadal requires a supporting reason, not just a denial.

In dialectical debate (jadal), simply saying 'I deny that' is not enough. This lesson introduces the tool of al-man' (prevention), which must be backed by a sanad—a reason that justifies the objection. We will see how al-Baji used a rigorous sanad to challenge a well-known usuli position, illustrating the proper method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the denial (man')
Locate the statement where the objector refuses to accept a premise or inference. In the example, al-Baji says 'This is not correct' in response to Dawud's use of the verse.
The denial is the moment of man' itself.
2
Extract the sanad (support)
Find the reasoning that the objector provides to justify the denial. Al-Baji explains why the permissive verse cannot be a foundational principle over other verses.
The sanad is the argument that makes the man' legitimate.
3
Analyze the structure of the sanad
Break down the sanad into its logical components. Al-Baji considers cases: if the conflicting verse is equally general, more specific, or more general, and shows the consequences.
A good sanad often uses conditional reasoning.
4
Verify the sanad's adequacy
Check whether the sanad genuinely addresses the claim and is not itself arbitrary. The passage implies the sanad must make the objection plausible in itself or in the claimant's view.
A sanad can be either objectively valid or based on the opponent's own assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنع لا بد له من سند، إذ السند عند أهل الميزان، ما يكون المنع مبنيا عليه، أي ما يكون مصححًا لورود المنع في نفس الأمر أو في زعم السائل، كأن يقال: لا نسلم كذا، لما لا يجوز أن يكون كذا، أو لا نسلم لزوم ذلك وإنما يلزم لو كان كذا، أو لا نسلم هذا وكيف يكون هذا أو هذا والحال أنه كذا. مثال ذلك؛ ما ذهب إليه الإمام داود الظاهري وأكثر أصحابه من أن أصل الأشياء على الإباحة، وأن ذلك ثابت بالشرع واستدلوا بقوله تعالى: {خَلَقَ لَكُم مَّا فِي الْأَرْضِ جَمِيعًا}، وقالوا: إذا ما تعارض خبران ولم يمكن الجمع أو الترجيح بينهما، رجع إلى هذه الإباحة التي شرها الشرع. وقد منع الباجي هذا الاستدلال بهذه الآية، وقال في الاعتراض عليه: وهذا ليس بصحيح، لأن الآية المبيحة عنده هي من جملة العمومات المبيحة، لا فرق بينها وبين غيرها، وليست هي أن تكون أصلاً بأولى من سائرها، فإذا عارضتها آية أخرى فلا يخلو أن تكون عامة مثلها أو أخص منها أو أعم منها، فإن كانت عامة مثلها فقد وقع التعارض بينهما فوجب أن يسقطها على أصله، وإن كانت أخص منها وجب عندنا أن يؤخذ بالحاظرة لصحة بناء المبيحة عليها، وإن كانت الحاظرة أعم منها وجب الأخذ بالمبيحة لصحة بناء العامة الحاظرة عليها، وعند داود في هذه الوجوه كلها تسقط الآيتان على نحو ما يفعل في سائر الآيات المتعارضة.
And prevention (al-man') must have a support (sanad), for the support according to the people of the measure is that upon which the prevention is based, i.e., that which validates the occurrence of the prevention in reality or in the claimant's opinion, such as saying: We do not accept such and such, because it is not permissible that it be such and such, or we do not accept that implication, it would only be implied if it were such and such, or we do not accept this, and how can it be this or that while the case is such and such. An example of that is the view of Imam Dawud al-Zahiri and most of his followers that the original state of things is permissibility (ibaha), and that this is established by the Law, and they argued with His statement: {He created for you all that is in the earth}, and they said: If two reports conflict and reconciliation or preponderance is not possible, one returns to this permissibility which the Law has prescribed. And al-Baji prevented this inference with this verse, and said in objection to it: This is not correct, because the permissive verse according to him is among the general permissive texts, no distinction between it and others, and it is not more entitled to be the principle than others. So if another verse conflicts with it, it is either general like it, or more specific than it, or more general than it. If it is general like it, conflict occurs between them, so it is necessary that they both fall according to his principle. If it is more specific, then according to us it is necessary to take the restrictive one because the permissive one is based on it. If the restrictive one is more general, then it is necessary to take the permissive one because the general restrictive one is based on it. And according to Dawud, in all these cases both verses fall in the manner done with other conflicting verses.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines the dialectical tool of al-man' (prevention/denial) in jadal. It first states that al-man' requires a sanad (support), which is the basis upon which the denial rests, validating it either objectively or in the claimant's view. The author then gives examples of how to phrase a supported prevention: rejecting a premise due to its impossibility, denying an implication unless a condition holds, or rejecting a claim given the current state of affairs. The passage then provides a concrete example from usul al-fiqh: Imam Dawud al-Zahiri's position that the original ruling of things is permissibility (ibaha), citing the Quranic verse "He created for you all that is in the earth." When conflicting reports (hadiths) cannot be reconciled or weighed, they return to this principle of ibaha. Al-Baji objected to this inference by arguing that the permissive verse is just one of many general texts, not inherently more foundational. He explains that if another verse conflicts, its relationship (equal generality, specificity, or superiority) determines which rule applies, and according to Dawud all conflicting verses fall. This example illustrates how a valid man' must be supported by reasoning, not merely asserted.

Key Terms
المنع
السند
الإباحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A valid objection in jadal (man') must be accompanied by a sanad (support) that justifies the denial, as demonstrated by al-Baji's systematic critique of Dawud al-Zahiri's inference.

Definition and Types of al-Naqd
Defines al-naqd and its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between naqd al-da'wa and naqd al-dalil and identify their subtypes in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Al-naqd breaks the opponent's claim by showing it doesn't hold everywhere.

In jadal, a powerful tool is al-naqd: refuting the opponent's claim or evidence by presenting a counterexample. This lesson defines naqd and explains its two main types and their subtypes, using a historical example from debates on qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic and technical meanings
Note that naqd literally means 'breaking'. In jadal terminology, it means preventing the claim of generality—showing that a universal judgment does not apply in all cases.
2
Identify naqd al-da'wa
Naqd al-da'wa attacks the universality of the opponent's claim. The objector shows that the claim does not hold in some specific instances, thus 'breaking' its generality.
3
Identify naqd al-dalil
Naqd al-dalil shows that the evidence fails to support the judgment in some cases. It can be performed by preventing a premise in summary (ijmali) or in detail (tafshili).
4
Differentiate between naqd ijmali and tafshili
Naqd ijmali prevents a premise in a general way without specifying which premise, whereas naqd tafshili prevents a specific premise, optionally with a support (sanad).
5
Analyze an example
Consider the opponents of qiyas who claimed all analogical reasoning is invalid. Al-Baji refuted this by showing that the claim is not universal—the Quran itself uses analogy. Identify this as naqd al-da'wa.
See the passage for the full example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض: يورد النقض، في اللغة: هو الكسر، وفي الاصطلاح منع دعوى العموم، وذلك أن المستدل دعواه أو دليله في صورة حكم كلي، مدعيا أن دعواه عامة بالنسبة إلى ما تثبته أو تنفيه ويكون في عمومها سند لصحتها ومبرر قوي لادعائه التمسك بها، فيتوجه المعترض إلى دعواه بمنع عمومها وعدم اطرادها في صور جزئية، فتكون الدعوى منقوضة. أما نقض الدليل، فهو بيان تخلف الحكم المدعى ثبوته أو نفيه عن دليل المعلل ووجه الدال عليه في بعض الصور، فإن وقع بمنع شيء من مقدمات الدليل على الإجمال، سمي: نقضًا إجماليا، لأن حاصله إلى منع شيء من مقدمات يرجع الدليل على الإجمال، وإن وقع بالمنع المجرد أو مع السند، سمي: نقضا تفصيليًا، لأنه منع مقدمة معينة. مثال ذلك أن المنكرين لحجية القياس استدلوا على إبطاله بقوله تعالى: {ذَلِكَ بِأَنَّهُمْ قَالُوا إِنَّمَا الْبَيْعُ مِثْلُ الرِّبَا وَأَحَلَّ اللَّهُ الْبَيْعَ وَحَرَّمَ الرِّبَا}، الاستدلال: أن الله سبحانه وتعالى ذمهم على تمثيل البيع بالربا وقياسه عليه، فدل على إبطال القياس. وقد نقض الإمام الباجي هذا الاستدلال من جهة عمومه، فإذا كان القياس في مثل هذا السلوك باطلا، فليس ذلك بدليل على بطلانه في كل المجالات، ودليل النقض أن الله تعالى قد استعمل القياس في استدلالات عديدة في القرآن الكريم. قال الباجي: «والجواب؛ أن هذا خطأ، لأننا لا نقول: إن كل قياس صحيح، فتبطل جميع الأقيسة إذا بطل منها نوع، وقد مثل هو تعالى أمثلة كثيرة، واستدل بأقيسة، وذلك قوله تعالى: {وَضَرَبَ لَنَا مَثَلًا وَنَسِيَ خَلْقَهُ قَالَ مَن يُحْيِ الْعِظَامَ وَهِيَ رَمِيمٌ قُلْ يُحْيِيهَا الَّذِي أَنشَأَهَا أَوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ}، فمثل النشأة الثانية بالأولى، وقال {ضَرَبَ اللَّهُ مَثَلًا عَبْدًا مَمْلُوكًا لَا يَقْدِرُ عَلَى شَيْءٍ وَمَن رَّزَقْنَاهُ مِنَّا رِزْقًا حَسَنًا فَهُوَ يُنفِقُ مِنْهُ سِرًّا وَجَهْرًا هَلْ يَسْتَوُونَ الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ بَلْ أَكْثَرُهُمْ لَا يَعْلَمُونَ}، وقبله قال تعالى: {فَلَا تَضْرِبُوا لِلَّهِ الْأَمْثَالَ إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَعْلَمُ وَأَنتُمْ لَا تَعْلَمُونَ}.
Al-Naqd (refutation): It is introduced, in language meaning breaking, and in terminology it is the prevention of the claim of generality. That is, the arguer makes his claim or evidence in the form of a universal judgment, claiming that his claim is general with respect to what it affirms or negates, and in its generality there is support for its correctness and a strong justification for his adherence to it. So the objector targets his claim by preventing its generality and its not applying in some particular instances, so the claim is refuted. As for refutation of the evidence (naqd al-dalil), it is the demonstration of the non-occurrence of the judgment claimed to be affirmed or negated from the evidence of the giver of the cause and the aspect indicating it in some instances. If it occurs by preventing something from the premises of the evidence in summary, it is called cumulative refutation (naqd ijmali), because its outcome is to prevent something from the premises to which the evidence returns in summary. If it occurs by bare prevention or with support, it is called detailed refutation (naqd tafshili), because it is the prevention of a specific premise. An example of that is that the deniers of the validity of analogical reasoning (qiyas) argued for its invalidity by His statement: {That is because they said: 'Buying and selling is only like usury, and Allah has permitted buying and selling and forbidden usury'}, the inference being that Allah blamed them for comparing sale to usury and analogy upon it, so it indicates the invalidity of qiyas. And Imam al-Baji refuted this inference from the aspect of its generality: if analogy is invalid in such behavior, that is not evidence for its invalidity in all fields. And the evidence of the refutation is that Allah Himself has used analogy in numerous inferences in the Quran. Al-Baji said: 'The answer is that this is wrong, because we do not say that every analogy is correct, so all analogies become invalid if one type is invalid. And He, exalted, has given many examples and inferred by analogies. That is His statement: {And he strikes for Us an example and forgets his creation; he says: 'Who will give life to bones while they are decayed?' Say: 'He will give them life who produced them the first time.'} So He likened the second creation to the first. And He said: {Allah strikes an example: a slave owned, unable to do anything, and one whom We have provided from Us with good provision, so he spends from it secretly and publicly; are they equal? Praise be to Allah! But most of them do not know.} And before that He said: {So do not strike examples for Allah; indeed Allah knows and you do not know.}'
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage defines al-naqd (refutation/counterexample) in jadal. It first gives the linguistic meaning (breaking) and then the technical meaning: preventing a claim of generality. The objector shows that the arguer's universal claim does not hold in some specific instances, thus 'breaking' its generality. The passage distinguishes two types of naqd: (1) naqd al-da'wa (refutation of the claim) — attacking the universality of the claim itself; (2) naqd al-dalil (refutation of the evidence) — showing that the evidence fails to prove the judgment in some cases. Within naqd al-dalil, there are two subtypes: ijmali (cumulative) — preventing a premise in general; and tafshili (detailed) — preventing a specific premise, either with or without a support (sanad). The passage then gives an example: opponents of qiyas (analogical reasoning) cite the verse where Allah blames those who compared sale to usury, arguing this invalidates all qiyas. Al-Baji refutes this by showing the inference is not universal: the Quran itself uses analogy in many places (e.g., comparing the second creation to the first, and the slave vs. the free man). This is a classic naqd of the claim's generality—the opponents' universal conclusion (all qiyas is invalid) is broken by counterexamples from the Quran.

Key Terms
النقض
نقض الدعوى
نقض الدليل
نقض إجمالي
نقض تفصيلي

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between naqd al-da'wa and naqd al-dalil?
A) Naqd al-da'wa attacks the evidence; naqd al-dalil attacks the claim
B) Naqd al-da'wa attacks the claim's generality; naqd al-dalil shows the evidence fails in some case
C) Both are the same
D) Naqd al-da'wa is only used for legal rulings

Key Takeaway
Naqd is a core jadal tool that breaks an opponent's claim or evidence by pointing out exceptions or failures in specific cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which type of naqd is used in this exchange: The opponent claims 'All ijtihad is permissible.' The objector says, 'But the Prophet forbade ijtihad in matters already settled by revelation.'
?
Create a debate scenario where naqd al-dalil (specifically naqd tafshili) would be appropriate, and write a brief exchange.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Applying al-Naqd: The Qiyas Example
Example of naqd al-da'wa in action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Baji's naqd of the opponents' argument against qiyas and explain how it exemplifies naqd al-da'wa.

Core Idea
Al-Baji used naqd al-da'wa to show that a general ban on qiyas is broken by the Quran's own use of analogy.

In this lesson, we examine a historical debate: opponents of qiyas claimed that all analogical reasoning is invalid because the Quran blames those who compare sale to usury. Al-Baji refuted this by showing that the claim is not universal—the Quran itself uses qiyas. This is a classic naqd al-da'wa, demonstrating how to break an overgeneralization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponents' claim and evidence
The opponents claim that qiyas (analogical reasoning) is invalid. Their evidence is the Quranic verse blaming those who said 'sale is like usury' and analogized them.
The verse is {ذَلِكَ بِأَنَّهُمْ قَالُوا إِنَّمَا الْبَيْعُ مِثْلُ الرِّبَا...}
2
Identify al-Baji's refutation
Al-Baji argues that blaming a particular false analogy does not invalidate all analogical reasoning. He calls this a mistake because 'we do not say that every analogy is correct'.
3
Analyze how al-Baji uses naqd al-da'wa
Al-Baji attacks the generality of the opponents' claim by providing counterexamples: the Quran itself uses analogy in many places (e.g., comparing the second creation to the first). This shows the claim 'all qiyas is invalid' is not universal.
4
Evaluate the strength of the refutation
The refutation is strong because it points to actual instances of qiyas in the Quran, directly contradicting the opponents' universal negative. It does not deny that some qiyas can be invalid, but breaks the claim that all is invalid.
This is a textbook example of naqd al-da'wa.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض: يورد النقض، في اللغة: هو الكسر، وفي الاصطلاح منع دعوى العموم، وذلك أن المستدل دعواه أو دليله في صورة حكم كلي، مدعيا أن دعواه عامة بالنسبة إلى ما تثبته أو تنفيه ويكون في عمومها سند لصحتها ومبرر قوي لادعائه التمسك بها، فيتوجه المعترض إلى دعواه بمنع عمومها وعدم اطرادها في صور جزئية، فتكون الدعوى منقوضة. أما نقض الدليل، فهو بيان تخلف الحكم المدعى ثبوته أو نفيه عن دليل المعلل ووجه الدال عليه في بعض الصور، فإن وقع بمنع شيء من مقدمات الدليل على الإجمال، سمي: نقضًا إجماليا، لأن حاصله إلى منع شيء من مقدمات يرجع الدليل على الإجمال، وإن وقع بالمنع المجرد أو مع السند، سمي: نقضا تفصيليًا، لأنه منع مقدمة معينة. مثال ذلك أن المنكرين لحجية القياس استدلوا على إبطاله بقوله تعالى: {ذَلِكَ بِأَنَّهُمْ قَالُوا إِنَّمَا الْبَيْعُ مِثْلُ الرِّبَا وَأَحَلَّ اللَّهُ الْبَيْعَ وَحَرَّمَ الرِّبَا}، الاستدلال: أن الله سبحانه وتعالى ذمهم على تمثيل البيع بالربا وقياسه عليه، فدل على إبطال القياس. وقد نقض الإمام الباجي هذا الاستدلال من جهة عمومه، فإذا كان القياس في مثل هذا السلوك باطلا، فليس ذلك بدليل على بطلانه في كل المجالات، ودليل النقض أن الله تعالى قد استعمل القياس في استدلالات عديدة في القرآن الكريم. قال الباجي: «والجواب؛ أن هذا خطأ، لأننا لا نقول: إن كل قياس صحيح، فتبطل جميع الأقيسة إذا بطل منها نوع، وقد مثل هو تعالى أمثلة كثيرة، واستدل بأقيسة، وذلك قوله تعالى: {وَضَرَبَ لَنَا مَثَلًا وَنَسِيَ خَلْقَهُ قَالَ مَن يُحْيِ الْعِظَامَ وَهِيَ رَمِيمٌ قُلْ يُحْيِيهَا الَّذِي أَنشَأَهَا أَوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ}، فمثل النشأة الثانية بالأولى، وقال {ضَرَبَ اللَّهُ مَثَلًا عَبْدًا مَمْلُوكًا لَا يَقْدِرُ عَلَى شَيْءٍ وَمَن رَّزَقْنَاهُ مِنَّا رِزْقًا حَسَنًا فَهُوَ يُنفِقُ مِنْهُ سِرًّا وَجَهْرًا هَلْ يَسْتَوُونَ الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ بَلْ أَكْثَرُهُمْ لَا يَعْلَمُونَ}، وقبله قال تعالى: {فَلَا تَضْرِبُوا لِلَّهِ الْأَمْثَالَ إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَعْلَمُ وَأَنتُمْ لَا تَعْلَمُونَ}.
Al-Naqd (refutation): It is introduced, in language meaning breaking, and in terminology it is the prevention of the claim of generality. That is, the arguer makes his claim or evidence in the form of a universal judgment, claiming that his claim is general with respect to what it affirms or negates, and in its generality there is support for its correctness and a strong justification for his adherence to it. If it occurs by preventing something from the premises of the evidence in summary, it is called cumulative refutation (naqd ijmali), because its outcome is to prevent something from the premises to which the evidence returns in summary. If it occurs by bare prevention or with support, it is called detailed refutation (naqd tafshili), because it is the prevention of a specific premise. An example of that is that the deniers of the validity of analogical reasoning (qiyas) argued for its invalidity by His statement: {That is because they said: 'Buying and selling is only like usury, and Allah has permitted buying and selling and forbidden usury'}, the inference being that Allah blamed them for comparing sale to usury and analogy upon it, so it indicates the invalidity of qiyas. And Imam al-Baji refuted this inference from the aspect of its generality: if analogy is invalid in such behavior, that is not evidence for its invalidity in all fields. And the evidence of the refutation is that Allah Himself has used analogy in numerous inferences in the Quran. Al-Baji said: 'The answer is that this is wrong, because we do not say that every analogy is correct, so all analogies become invalid if one type is invalid. And He, exalted, has given many examples and inferred by analogies. Commentary

Understanding Al-Mu'arada: Opposition in Jadal
Defines mu'arada linguistically and terminologically with example from Juwayni

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-mu'arada in a dialectical exchange and apply its method of counter-proof using the opponent's own evidence.

Core Idea
Mu'arada is not mere denial; it is a counter-proof using the opponent's own evidence.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), al-mu'arada is a key technique. This lesson defines it both linguistically and terminologically, then walks through al-Juwayni's classic example where he counters an opponent's argument by reinterpreting the same Quranic verse, demonstrating mu'arada in action.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic root
The word mu'arada comes from mumana'a (prevention). In language, it means prevent or oppose. Recognize that the term implies active resistance, not passive denial.
Linguistic roots help grasp the technical shade of meaning.
2
Grasp the technical definition
In juristic terminology, mu'arada is 'preventing the opponent by claiming equality in indication' or 'establishing a proof against what the opponent has established.' It is not just contradiction, but a structured counter-argument.
Equality (musawa) is central: you meet the opponent's proof with a proof of equal or stronger force.
3
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponents claimed that the word 'iman' in the verse (Q. 2:143) means 'prayer', not 'belief', proving that Shar'i names shift from linguistic meanings. They used this as evidence for their position.
This is the claim that mu'arada will oppose.
4
See how al-Juwayni constructs the counter-proof
Al-Juwayni uses the same verse but interprets 'iman' as 'belief' (tasdiq), arguing it refers to the believers' belief in prayers to the two qiblas. He does not bring new evidence; he reinterprets the opponent's evidence to support his own view.
This is the essence of mu'arada: using the same proof but with a different explanation.
5
Recognize the method
Mu'arada refutes by showing that the opponent's proof can be understood in a way that does not support their claim. It forces the opponent to either accept the reinterpretation or explain why their interpretation is necessary.
This technique is common in usul al-fiqh and kalam debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
د - المعارضة : وهي في اللغة : من الممانعة . وفي عرف الفقهاء : ممانعة الخصم بدعوى المساواة . أو مساواة الخصم في دعوى الدلالة . وعبر عنه بعضهم بأنه : إقامة الشيء في مقابلة ما يناقضه، واصطلاحًا : إقامة الدليل على خلاف ما أقامه عليه الخصم ذلك أن القائلين بأن الأسماء الشرعية تدل على معان غير لغوية، إذ أن الشرع نقلها عن أصل وضعها اللغوي إلى معان شرعية جديدة ليست في اللغة، ومن بين ما استدلوا به على ذلك بقوله تعالى : ﴿ ... وما كان الله ليضيع إيمَنكُمْ قالوا : فالإيمان هو التصديق في اللغة والمراد به في الآية صلاة الذين صلوا إلى بيت المقدس ثم نسخ التوجه إليها، فهذا خارج من اللغة وضعًا واستعمالا ومنقول إلى غيره. وقال إمام الحرمين الجويني معارضًا : وأما الأسماء اللغوية فهي الجارية على ما كانت عليه في أصل الوضع من غير تحريف ونقل، وهي الأغلب من ألفاظ صاحب الشرائع . واستدل على رأيه هذا بنفس دليل المخالفين، ولكن على سبيل المعارضة فقال : يقال لهم : أما قوله تعالى : . وما كان اللهُ لِيُضِيعَ إِيمَنكُمْ ... فمعناه : وما كان الله ليضيع تصديقكم بالصلوات إلى القبلتين، فهو إذا محمول على حقيقة اللغة»
D - Al-Mu'arada (Opposition): It is in language: from al-mumana'a (prevention). In the usage of the jurists: the prevention of the opponent by claiming equality. Or making the opponent equal in claiming indication. Some have expressed it as: placing a thing in opposition to what contradicts it. Terminologically: the establishment of proof against what the opponent has established. That is because those who say that the Shar'i names indicate non-linguistic meanings, since the law transferred them from their original linguistic meaning to new Shar'i meanings not in the language, and among what they used as evidence for that is His saying the Most High: '... And Allah would never let your faith be lost.' They said: al-iman is al-tasdiq (belief) in language, and what is intended by it in the verse is the prayer of those who prayed towards Jerusalem then the direction was abrogated, so this is outside the language in terms of original meaning and usage and is transferred to something else. And Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said in opposition: As for the linguistic names, they are those that continue as they were in the original coinage without alteration or transfer, and they are the majority of the words of the Lawgiver. And he argued for this opinion of his using the same evidence of the opponents, but by way of opposition, saying: It is said to them: As for His saying the Most High: '... And Allah would never let your faith be lost.' Its meaning is: And Allah would never let your belief in the prayers to the two directions be lost. Thus, it is carried on the linguistic reality.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of al-mu'arada (opposition) in the context of jadal. It begins with the linguistic meaning, tracing it to al-mumana'a (prevention). In the technical usage of the jurists, it is described as the opponent preventing the claim by asserting equality, or making the opponent equal in the claim of indication (dilala). Some define it as placing something in opposition to what contradicts it. The terminological definition is the establishment of a proof against what the opponent has established. The author then provides an illustrative example from the debate on whether Shar'i names (like 'iman' in the verse) indicate non-linguistic meanings. The proponents argue that the word 'iman' in the Quran, which they claim means 'prayer' rather than 'belief', shows that Shar'i terms have been transferred from their original linguistic meanings to new Shar'i meanings. Al-Juwayni counters by using the same verse but interpreting it differently: he argues that 'iman' retains its linguistic meaning of 'tasdiq' (belief), and the verse refers to the believers' belief in the prayers to the two directions (before and after the abrogation). Thus, he uses the same evidence but shows that it can be understood in line with the linguistic reality, thereby demonstrating mu'arada by offering a counter-interpretation.

Key Terms
المعارضة
الممانعة
الدليل

Checkpoint

C) He brought a different verse to prove his point.

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada is the art of countering an opponent's proof with the same evidence, forcing a reinterpretation or showing inconsistency.

Understanding Al-Qalb as a Dialectical Tool
Defines al-qalb linguistically and terminologically with example from al-Shirazi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-qalb, distinguish it from other jadal techniques, and identify its two linguistic roots.

Core Idea
Al-qalb turns the opponent's own reasoning against him.

Al-qalb (inversion) is a powerful tool in jadal (dialectics) where the respondent uses the very same reasoning the opponent presented to argue for the opposite conclusion. This lesson unpacks its linguistic and terminological definitions, including al-Juwayni's alternative term al-ishtirak, and prepares you to recognize when qalb is being used.

Guided Path
1
Examine the Linguistic Roots
Read the two linguistic meanings: (1) making the upper part lower, (2) turning something inside out. Note how these metaphors apply to logical inversion: a cause becomes a judgment, or a witness for the opponent becomes a witness against him.
The first root relates to cause-judgment inversion; the second to description-witness inversion.
2
Grasp the Terminological Definition
Al-Kafawi defines qalb as 'linking the opposite of what the arguer said with its cause'. Understand that qalb does not simply contradict; it uses the opponent's own 'illa (cause) or reasoning to derive the opposite conclusion.
Al-Juwayni calls this 'al-ishtirak' (sharing) because the opponent shares the same reasoning pattern.
3
Differentiate from Other Dialectical Moves
Contrast qalb with simple refutation (ibtal) or counterargument. Qalb is a specific technique where the respondent adopts the opponent's evidence but flips the result. It is a kind of symmetrical rebuttal.
This makes qalb especially effective rhetorically because it uses the opponent's own premises.
4
Identify the Alternative Terminology
Note that al-Juwayni uses al-ishtirak (participation) to denote the same concept. The passage states he defines it as 'the equalizing of an opponent with his opponent in what he presents as contradictory'. Understand that this is a terminological variation.
Later usul works consistently use 'qalb'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هـ - القلب : قال الكفوي : القلب : هو في اصطلاح الأصول عبارة عن ربط خلاف ما قاله المستدل بعلته للإلحاق بأصله. وفي اللغة على معنيين : أحدهما جعل أعلى الشيء أسفل، ومنه أخذ قلب العلة حكمًا وبالعكس، لأن العلة أعلى من الحكم لكونها أصلا، والحكم أسفل لكونه تبعا . . والثاني : جعل ظاهر الشيء باطنا كقلب الجراب ومنه أخذ قلب الوصف شاهدا على الخصم بعد أن يكون شاهدا للخصم والمقصود بالقلب في اصطلاحنا هو الاستدلال على الخصم بمثل ما استدل به ويسميه الجويني بالاشتراك، ويعرفه بأنه : مساواة الخصم خصمه فيما يورده على التنافي مثال ذلك عند الأصوليين، ما نجده عند أبي إسحاق الشيرازي رحمه الله حيث أورد استدلال الأشاعرة، ومنهم القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني على أن الأمر لا صيغة له تدل على الفعل، بقولهم : إثبات صيغة الأمر إنما يكون بالعقل أو النقل ؛ فلا يجوز أن يكون بالعقل، لأن العقل لا مجال له في إثبات الأسامي واللغات لأنها لو كانت معلومة بالعقل لما احتيج إلى قراءة كتب الأدب والمصنفات في اللغة وسماعها من أهل الخبرة بها، وإن كان طريق إثباتها بالنقل فلا يخلو إما أن يكون النقل تواترا أو آحادًا، فإن كان تواترا وجب أن يقع العلم به ضرورة لكل أحد، لأن ما طريقه الضرورات يشترك العقلاء في معرفته، ولو كان كذلك لعلمناه كما علمتم، فثبت أنه لا تواتر فيه، وإن كان آحادًا فلا يثبت به لأنه أصل من الأصول، وإثبات أصل من الأصول بأخبار الآحاد لا يجوز، فلا وجه لإثبات ذلك».
H - Al-Qalb (Inversion): Al-Kafawi said: Al-Qalb in the terminology of the usul is the expression for linking the opposite of what the arguer said with its cause to annex it to its origin. In language it has two meanings: One is making the upper part of a thing lower, from which is derived the inversion of the cause into a judgment and vice versa, because the cause is higher than the judgment as it is the origin, and the judgment is lower as it is the follower. The second is making the apparent of a thing hidden, like inverting a bag, and from it is derived making a description a witness against the opponent after it had been a witness for the opponent. And the intended meaning of al-qalb in our terminology is to reason against the opponent with the same kind of reasoning he used, and al-Juwayni calls it al-ishtirak (participation), and defines it as: the equalizing of an opponent with his opponent in what he presents as contradictory. An example of that among the usuliyyun is what we find from Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, may Allah have mercy on him, where he mentioned the reasoning of the Ash'aris, among them al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, that the command has no linguistic form that indicates the act, by their saying: The establishment of a form for command is only through reason or transmitted tradition; it cannot be through reason, because reason has no place in establishing names and languages, for if they were known by reason, there would be no need to read books of literature and works on language and to hear them from experts; and if the method of establishing them is through transmitted tradition, then it is either mutawatir or ahad; if it is mutawatir, then knowledge of it would necessarily occur to everyone, because what is necessary is shared by rational people in knowing it, and if that were so, we would know it as you know it, so it is established that it is not mutawatir; and if it is ahad, then it is not established because it is a fundamental principle, and establishing a fundamental principle through ahad reports is not permissible, so there is no way to establish that.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage defines al-qalb (inversion) as a dialectical technique. Al-Kafawi's definition in usul al-fiqh describes it as linking the opposite of what the arguer said with its cause, so that the cause can be annexed to its origin in a reversed manner. The linguistic origin has two senses: first, turning something top-down (like inverting a cause into a judgment and vice versa, because the cause is higher and the judgment lower); second, turning something inside out (like inverting a bag, which becomes a metaphor for making a description that once supported the opponent now evidence against him). The terminological meaning is to argue against the opponent using the same kind of reasoning he used. Al-Juwayni calls this al-ishtirak (participation) and defines it as equalizing the opponent with the opponent in what he presents as contradictory. The author then gives an example from Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, who recorded the argument of the Ash'aris (including al-Baqillani) that the command (amr) has no linguistic form indicating the act. Their argument: establishing a form for command can only be through reason or transmission. Reason cannot do it because names and languages are conventional, not rational; if they were rational, one would not need to study language books. Transmission must be either mutawatir or ahad. Mutawatir would produce necessary knowledge by all rational people, but since we do not all know this, it is not mutawatir. Ahad reports cannot establish fundamental principles. Thus, the form cannot be established. This argument is presented as a basis for someone to apply qalb by using the same reasoning to counter the opponent.

Key Terms
القلب
العلة
الاشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) al-ishtirak
C) al-ijma'
D) al-qiyas

Key Takeaway
Al-qalb is a dialectical technique that uses the opponent's own reasoning to derive the opposite conclusion, often by inverting the relationship between cause and judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the Ash'ari argument from the passage. Construct a qalb response that uses the same premises (reason vs. transmission, mutawatir vs. ahad) to argue that the form of command is established. Write a short paragraph as if you were a Hanafi usuli replying to al-Baqillani.

Analyzing the Ash'ari Argument for Qalb Application
Defines al-qalb linguistically and terminologically with example from al-Shirazi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to break down the Ash'ari argument about the command form and identify how a qalb response would invert it.

Core Idea
Qalb exploits the same reasoning to flip the conclusion.

The Ash'ari argument denying a linguistic form for command is a classic example of an argument vulnerable to qalb. This lesson walks you through the structure of that argument and shows how a debater could turn it around using qalb. You will practice recognizing the premises that can be inverted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Conclusion of the Ash'ari Argument
The Ash'aris aim to prove that 'the command has no linguistic form that indicates the act'. This is the claim they defend. Write it down.
This is the mudda'a (claim) that the opponent would want to refute.
2
Map the Premises
The argument uses a dichotomy: establishment of a form is either by reason or by transmission. Then it eliminates reason, then splits transmission into mutawatir and ahad, eliminating both. List each premise clearly.
The reasoning is a series of conditional and disjunctive steps.
3
Locate the Invertible Components
Qalb works by using the same logical structure but applying it to a different conclusion. For example, the premise 'if reason cannot establish names, then transmission is the only way' can be used to argue that transmission does establish the form, if we can show that one of its subcategories (e.g., ahad) is sufficient.
The key is to flip the evaluation of the sub-premises.
4
Construct a Qalb Response
Imagine you are a Hanafi usuli. Use the same premises but argue that ahad reports are sufficient to establish linguistic forms because they are matters of convention, not fundamentals. Thus, the form is established by ahad transmission, and the Ash'ari conclusion is false.
This is a typical qalb: using the opponent's own 'illa (reasoning) to reach the opposite ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

5 min
Definition and Concept of Qalb in Jadal
Definition of qalb in jadal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qalb in jadal and distinguish its technical meaning from its linguistic roots.

Core Idea
Qalb turns the opponent's argument against him by using his own reasoning.

This lesson introduces the dialectical technique of qalb (inversion), a powerful tool in jadal. You will learn its definition, its two linguistic origins, and how it differs from mere contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Extract the technical definition
Identify the definition of qalb in usul al-fiqh: 'linking the opposite of what the arguer said with its cause for attaching to its origin.' Write it down.
2
Understand the linguistic meanings
Note the two linguistic meanings: (1) turning upside down, (2) turning inside out. Reflect on how each relates to the technical term.
3
Learn al-Juwayni's synonym
Al-Juwayni calls qalb 'al-ishtirak' (sharing). He defines it as the opponent equaling the arguer in what he presents. Recognize that this highlights the symmetry of the technique.
4
Differentiate qalb from contradiction
Qalb is not any counterargument; it must use the same reasoning structure as the opponent. Compare it to a simple contradictory claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القلب : هو في اصطلاح الأصول عبارة عن ربط خلاف ما قاله المستدل بعلته للإلحاق بأصله. وفي اللغة على معنيين : أحدهما جعل أعلى الشيء أسفل، ومنه أخذ قلب العلة حكمًا وبالعكس، لأن العلة أعلى من الحكم لكونها أصلا، والحكم أسفل لكونه تبعا . . والثاني : جعل ظاهر الشيء باطنا كقلب الجراب ومنه أخذ قلب الوصف شاهدا على الخصم بعد أن يكون شاهدا للخصم والمقصود بالقلب في اصطلاحنا هو الاستدلال على الخصم بمثل ما استدل به ويسميه الجويني بالاشتراك، ويعرفه بأنه : مساواة الخصم خصمه فيما يورده على التنافي مثال ذلك عند الأصوليين، ما نجده عند أبي إسحاق الشيرازي رحمه الله حيث أورد استدلال الأشاعرة، ومنهم القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني على أن الأمر لا صيغة له تدل على الفعل، بقولهم : إثبات صيغة الأمر إنما يكون بالعقل أو النقل ؛ فلا يجوز أن يكون بالعقل، لأن العقل لا مجال له في إثبات الأسامي واللغات لأنها لو كانت معلومة بالعقل لما احتيج إلى قراءة كتب الأدب والمصنفات في اللغة وسماعها من أهل الخبرة بها، وإن كان طريق إثباتها بالنقل فلا يخلو إما أن يكون النقل تواترا أو آحادًا، فإن كان تواترا وجب أن يقع العلم به ضرورة لكل أحد، لأن ما طريقه الضرورات يشترك العقلاء في معرفته، ولو كان كذلك لعلمناه كما علمتم، فثبت أنه لا تواتر فيه، وإن كان آحادًا فلا يثبت به لأنه أصل من الأصول، وإثبات أصل من الأصول بأخبار الآحاد لا يجوز، فلا وجه لإثبات ذلك». ولأن الشيرازي يقول بأن للأمر صيغة موضوعة في اللغة تدل على الفعل، فإنه لم يرتض هذا الاستدلال واعترض عليه بطريق القلب، حيث استخدم هذا الدليل في الاحتجاج على الخصم، وبين أن هذا الدليل هو حجة على الخصم وليس حجة له ، فقال : والجواب : أنا نقلب ذلك عليه في إثبات الاشتراك في هذه الصيغة، فإنه يزعم أنها تحتمل الإقدام وتحتمل الإحجام وهي مشتركة بين الأمرين، فلا يخلو إما أن يكون قد أثبت هذا الاشتراك بالعقل أو بالنقل، فلا يجوز أن يكون بالعقل، لأن العقل لا مجال له في ذلك على ما ذكروا، ولا يجوز أن يكون بالنقل، لأنه لا يخلو إما أن يكون تواترا لأنه لو وجد لعلمناه ضرورة كما علمنا سائر الأضداد والألفاظ المشتركة كالعين واللون، ولا يجوز أن يكون آحادًا لأن مثل ذلك لا يثبت بأخبار الآحاد ، لأنه يكثر استعماله وتعم البلوى به فلا يكون النقل فيه آحادًا، فكل عذر له في إثبات الاشتراك فهو عذرنا في إثبات كونه صيغة موضوعة لاستدعاء (الفعل)
Al-qalb: In the terminology of usul, it is an expression for linking the opposite of what the mutadallil (arguer) said with its 'illa (cause) for attaching to its origin. Linguistically, it has two meanings: first, making the upper part of something lower, from which is taken the inversion of the cause in ruling and vice versa, because the cause is higher than the ruling as it is a root, and the ruling is lower as it is a branch. Second, making the outer part of something inner, like turning a bag inside out, from which is taken the inversion of the description as a witness against the opponent after it was a witness for the opponent. What is intended by al-qalb in our terminology is to argue against the opponent by means similar to what he argued with, and al-Juwayni calls it al-ishtirak (sharing), and defines it as: the opponent equaling his opponent in what he presents in opposition. An example of that with the usuliyyin is what we find with Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, may Allah have mercy on him, where he presented the argument of the Ash'aris, including al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, that the command (al-amr) has no linguistic form indicating the action, by their statement: 'The establishment of the form of command can only be by reason or transmission; it cannot be by reason, because reason has no scope in establishing names and languages, for if they were known by reason, there would be no need to read books of literature and works on language and hear them from experts. If the way of establishing them is by transmission, then it cannot be other than either mutawatir (massively transmitted) or ahad (single reports). If it is mutawatir, knowledge of it would necessarily occur to everyone, because what is known by necessities is shared by rational people in knowing it, and if it were so, we would know it as you know; so it is established that there is no tawatur in it. If it is ahad, then it cannot be established because it is a fundamental principle, and establishing a fundamental principle by ahad reports is not permissible, so there is no way to establish that.' And because al-Shirazi says that the command has a linguistic form indicating the action, he did not accept this argument and objected by way of qalb, using this argument in disputation against the opponent, and showed that this argument is a proof against the opponent and not for him. He said: The answer: I invert that against him in establishing ishtirak (ambiguity) in this form, for he claims that it can imply action and can imply omission and is shared between the two matters. Then it cannot be other than that he has established this ishtirak either by reason or transmission. It cannot be by reason, because reason has no scope in that according to what they mentioned. And it cannot be by transmission, because it cannot be other than either mutawatir, for if it existed we would know it necessarily as we know other opposites and ambiguous words like 'ayn and lawn, and it cannot be ahad because such a thing is not established by ahad reports, because it is widely used and the need for it is general, so transmission about it cannot be ahad. So every excuse he has in establishing ishtirak is our excuse in establishing that it is a form set for demanding action.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage defines qalb (inversion/reversal), a key dialectical technique in jadal, and illustrates it with a detailed example from Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi. The author begins by giving the technical definition of qalb in usul al-fiqh: linking the opposite of what the arguer said with its cause (illa) to attach it to its origin (asl). This means the opponent takes the same reasoning used by the arguer and applies it to the opponent's own position. The author then notes two linguistic meanings of qalb: (1) turning something upside down, and (2) turning something inside out. Both are relevant to the technical usage, where the cause (illa) and ruling are 'inverted'. The specific jadal definition is then given: arguing against the opponent by means similar to what he argued with. Al-Juwayni calls this 'ishtirak' (sharing), meaning the opponent equals the arguer in what he presents as opposition. The example is from al-Shirazi's refutation of the Ash'ari claim that the imperative form (amr) has no linguistic meaning indicating action. The Ash'aris argued that the meaning of the command form can only be established by reason or transmission. Reason cannot do it. Transmission must be either mutawatir (massively transmitted) or ahad (single reports). It cannot be mutawatir because everyone would know it, and it cannot be ahad because it is a fundamental principle. Al-Shirazi inverts this argument: he applies the same reasoning to the Ash'ari claim that the command form is ambiguous (ishtirak between action and omission). He shows that the Ash'aris themselves cannot establish this ambiguity by reason or transmission either. If they claim it is established by mutawatir transmission, then everyone would know it, but they don't. If by ahad, then it fails because it is a widely used matter. Thus, their argument is turned against them. This illustrates the essence of qalb: using the opponent's own proof structure to refute his position.

Key Terms
القلب
الاشتراك
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Making the upper part of something lower
B) Arguing against the opponent by means similar to what he argued with
C) Using a different argument to refute the opponent
D) Turning a bag inside out

Key Takeaway
Qalb is a jadal technique where the arguer uses the opponent's own reasoning to refute him, not a new argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a qalb refutation by applying the same reasoning to the opponent's own position (e.g., his claim that the only sources are Quran and Sunnah relies on personal opinion in interpretation).
